Classification and Critique of Priority Types
Critique of adding a fifth type of priority and proposal of a sixth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classification of priority into four or five types is based on induction rather than demonstration, and how a sixth type can be rationally conceived.

Core Idea
Priority types are human classifications, not divinely fixed categories.

In Islamic kalam, scholars classify priority (taqaddum) into types. Some add a fifth type — priority by nature. But the author critiques this, showing that the limitation to four or five is arbitrary, relying solely on induction. He then introduces a sixth type: priority in existence without causal or temporal connection. This lesson follows that critical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of hypothetical questioning and logical reasoning.
2
Identify the critique
The author challenges the existing classification by pointing out that the limitation to four or five types has no proof except induction. Highlight this key point.
This shows the author's critical approach to inherited classifications.
3
Understand the sixth type
Examine how the author argues for a sixth type: priority in existence without regard to causal necessitation or time/place. Note the example of one and two.
The author claims this is rationally necessary (ma'qul bi al-darura).
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author would present a critique without definitive resolution. How does this affect your understanding of kalam as a discipline?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زاد قسما خامسًا وهو التقدم بالطبع ; كتقدم الواحد على الاثنين، ولو طالبهم مطالب لِمَ حصرتم الأقسام في أربعة أو خمسة لم يجدوا على الحصر دليلا سوى الاستقراء، حتى لو قيل لهم: ما الذي أنكرتم على من زاد قسما سادسا وهو التقدم والتأخر في الوجود من غير التفات إلى الإيجاب بالذات ولا التفات إلى الزمان والمكان والتقدم للواحد على الاثنين شديد الشبه بهذا القبيل، فإن الواحد ليس بعلة يلزم منه وجود الاثنين ضرورة، بل يمكن أن يتصور شيئان ; أحدهما وجوده بذاته، والثاني وجوده مستفاد من غيره، ثم بعد ذلك يقع البحث في أنه يستفيد وجوده منه اختيارًا أو طبعا أو ذاتا، هذا معقول بالضرورة.
Among them is one who added a fifth division, which is priority by nature; like the priority of one over two. And if a questioner were to ask them: Why did you limit the divisions to four or five? They would not find for the limitation any proof except induction. Even if it were said to them: What do you reject against one who added a sixth division, namely priority and posteriority in existence without regard to essential necessitation and without regard to time and place? And the priority of one over two is very similar to this kind. For the one is not a cause from which the existence of two necessarily follows. Rather, two things can be conceived: one whose existence is by itself, and the second whose existence is derived from another. Then afterwards the investigation occurs whether it derives its existence from it by choice, or by nature, or by essence. This is rationally necessary.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the classification of types of priority. The author notes that some scholars have added a fifth type, priority by nature, exemplified by the priority of one over two. He then points out that if one were to ask them why they limited the divisions to four or five, they would have no proof except induction. That is, the classification is not based on a demonstrative proof but on empirical generalization. He then introduces a possible sixth type: priority and posteriority in existence without regard to causal necessitation or time and space. He argues that this sixth type is plausible because the priority of one over two is similar: one is not a cause of two, yet one is prior. He then explains that two things can be conceived: one existing by itself, another deriving existence from another. Then the investigation turns on how the derivation occurs—by choice, nature, or essence. The author concludes that this is rationally conceivable.

Key Terms
تَقَدُّم
طَبْع
عِلَّة
وُجُوب
اِسْتِقْرَاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the division of priority into four or five types is exhaustive and divinely established.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the classification is based on human induction, not definitive proof, and that alternative divisions (like a sixth type) are rationally possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for the limitation of the divisions of priority to four or five?
A) Divine revelation
B) Rational demonstration
C) Induction
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The classification of priority is not fixed by revelation but a product of human reasoning, leaving room for alternative divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You classify priorities daily—between duties, desires, and relationships. On what basis do you make these classifications? Are they built on induction from your limited experience, or on divine guidance? How would you react if someone proposed a different priority order that challenged yours?
?
The author argues that even the priority of one over two is a rational concept, not a fixed reality. What does that say about your own 'self-evident' truths? Are you willing to admit that some of your deepest convictions might be based on mere induction, not absolute certainty?
?
If you were asked to defend your life's priorities before Allah, could you do so with a demonstrative proof? Or would you fall back on 'this is how it has always been'—the very induction the author criticizes? What one priority will you re-examine today in light of this lesson?

Why the World Must Be Finite: Rational Necessity vs. Mental Estimation
Argument for the necessary finitude of the world and the unreality of mental estimations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the finitude of the world is a rational necessity in Islamic theology and why imaginary extrapolations beyond the proof do not constitute real possibilities.

Core Idea
Imagining a larger world does not make it possible; rational proof establishes necessary limits.

The author of the passage addresses a common objection: could God have created the world larger? He argues that the finitude of the world is not arbitrary but is demonstrated by rational proof. Any speculation beyond that proof is merely mental estimation, not genuine possibility. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for understanding the concepts of originated and eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very dense style with parallel structures.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage asserts that the finitude of the world is conceptually necessary (واجب تصوره عقلا). Find where this is stated and note the supporting phrase 'since the proof has indicated it'.
3
Examine the example about size
The author presents a hypothetical question: could God have created the world larger? Focus on how he answers: the finitude is necessary, and any 'beyond' is mental estimation.
This example illustrates the distinction between rational possibility and imaginary estimation.
4
Understand the parallel with time
The author parallels place and time: estimating a place beyond the world is like estimating a time before the world. Both are mental, not real.
This reinforces that the finitude is not just spatial but also temporal.
5
Connect to originated and eternal
The final part ties the finitude to the very concepts of originated (حادث) and eternal (قديم). The world's having a beginning is understood necessarily from the concept of occurrence.
This shows that the finitude is not an arbitrary limit but follows from the nature of contingent existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
؛ إذ البرهان قد دل عليه، وما وراء ذلك تقدير وهمي يسمى تجويزا عقليا ، والتقديرات والتجويزات لا تقف ولا تتناهى، وهو كما إذا سألتم: هل كان يجوز عن يحدث العالم أكبر مما خلقه بحيث يكون نسبة نهايته إلى مكاننا أكبر مسافة؟ فيجاب عنه : إن إثبات الحد والتناهي للعالم واجب تصوره عقلا ؛ إذ البرهان قد دل عليه وما وراء ذلك فتقدير ذهني يسمى تجويزا عقليا، والتقديرات والتجويزات لا تتناهى، فتقدير مكان وراء العالم مكانًا كتقدير زمان وراء العالم زمانًا، وبالجملة حدث العالم حيث يتصور الحدوث والحادث ما له أول والقديم ما لا أول له والجمع بين ما له أول وبين ما لا أول له فحال هذا ما نعقله من الحدوث ضرورة، وهو كتناهي العالم من الحجمية والجسمية حذو القدة بالقدة.
Since the proof has indicated it, and beyond that is an imaginary estimation called a rational permission, and estimations and permissions do not stop nor are they infinite. It is like when you ask: Is it permissible that He created the world larger than He made it, such that the ratio of its end to our location is a greater distance? The answer is: Establishing the limit and finitude of the world is conceptually necessary intellectually, because the proof has indicated it, and beyond that is a mental estimation called a rational permission, and estimations and permissions are not finite. So estimating a place beyond the world is like estimating a time beyond the world. In summary, the occurrence of the world is where occurrence is conceived, and the originated has a beginning, and the eternal has no beginning, and combining what has a beginning with what has no beginning—the state of this is what we understand of occurrence necessarily, and it is like the finitude of the world in volume and corporeality, exactly matching.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the necessary finitude of the world. The author begins by stating that the proof (البرهان) has established the finitude of the world, and any extrapolation beyond that is merely an imaginary estimation (تقدير وهمي) called a rational permission (تجويز عقلي). These estimations and permissions have no end. He illustrates with an example: if someone asks whether God could have created the world larger, the answer is that the finitude is conceptually necessary because the proof has demonstrated it. Any beyond is mental. He then parallels place and time: imagining a place beyond the world is like imagining a time before the world—both are purely mental. In conclusion, the occurrence (حدث) of the world is understood where we conceive of occurrence: the originated (حادث) has a beginning, the eternal (قديم) has no beginning, and combining the two is impossible. This is necessarily understood from the concept of occurrence itself. This necessary finitude is like the world's finitude in size and corporeality, exactly matching.

Key Terms
تقدير وهمي
تجويز عقلي
حدوث
قديم
تناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because we can imagine a larger world, it is possible for the world to be larger.
Correction: The author clarifies that imagination does not correspond to real possibility; the proof has established the world's finitude as necessary.

Checkpoint
In the author's example, what is the answer to the question 'Could God have created the world larger than He did?'
A) Yes, because God is all-powerful.
B) No, because the proof has established the world's finitude as necessary.
C) It is impossible to know.
D) It depends on whether space is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The finitude of the world is rationally necessary; any mental estimation beyond that is not a real possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often let your mind wander to 'what if' scenarios about your life—what if you had more wealth, more time, a different past. This lesson teaches that such imaginings are infinite and unreal. How much of your anxiety comes from dwelling on imaginary possibilities rather than accepting what is rationally necessary? Will you commit to distinguishing between proof-based certainty and mere mental estimation in your daily thoughts?
?
The passage compares imagining a larger world to imagining a time before the world—both are 'mental estimation' with no reality. When you struggle with doubts about God's wisdom in your life, are you essentially indulging in 'mental estimations' about how things could have been different? How can you use the proof of God's wisdom (like the proof of the world's finitude) to anchor yourself against these imaginations?
?
You understand that the originated has a beginning and the eternal does not. Yet you often act as if your own existence is self-sufficient, forgetting your contingency. This lesson forces you to confront that you are a حادث (originated) with a beginning. How does acknowledging your own finitude and dependence on the Eternal change the way you approach your ambitions and fears? What will you do today to live in light of this truth?

Contradiction in the Second Premise: Possible and Necessary
Analyzes the second premise of the opponent, showing its verbal and logical contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'what may possibly exist must exist' is contradictory both in wording and in meaning, and why the attempted resolution 'possible by itself, necessary by another' fails.

Core Idea
Possible and necessary are opposites; no reinterpretation removes the contradiction.

The opponent's second premise claims that whatever can possibly exist necessarily exists. At first glance, this seems to confuse two distinct categories. In this lesson, we follow the author's critique, which exposes a double contradiction: one in the very words used, and another in the logical implications. Understanding this critique sharpens our grasp of how possibility and necessity function in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the two distinct levels of critique: wording and meaning.
The passage uses technical terms like جواز, وجوب, and بالغير.
2
Analyze the verbal contradiction
Identify why 'possible' and 'necessary' are opposites. Then see the attempted explanation 'possible by itself, necessary by another' and the author's refutation.
The author argues that necessity by another only relates to existence, not to the thing's own necessity.
3
Examine the contradiction in meaning
Understand the infinite regress argument: if all possibles were necessary, they would all exist at once, but actual existence shows temporal order implying arrangement among essences.
This builds on a prior discussion about types of priority and posteriority.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Consider why the premise is invalid and how this relates to the broader argument about the necessity of the First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام على المقدمة الثانية؛ وهي أن ما يجوز وجوده [يجب وجوده]. قلنا هذا الكلام أولا متناقض لفظاً ومعنى؛ أما اللفظ فإن الجواز والوجوب متناقضان إلا أن يُقال : إن ما يجوز وجوده في ذاته يجب وجوده بغيره، قيل: وهذا أيضا باطل، فإن الوجوب بالغير إنما يكون بإيجاب الغير، والجايز إنما يحتاج إلى الواجب في وجوده لا في وجوبه، فيكون وجوده بإيجاد الغير ثم يلزمه من حيث النسبة وجوبه، وقد تبين هذا المعنى فيما قبل. وأما التناقض من حيث المعنى فهو أن الجايز وجوده مما لا يتناهى، فلو كان ما جاز وجوده وجب وجوده لوجد ما لا يتناهى دفعة واحدة وإذا شرط الترتيب في الذوات حتى حصل في الوجود أول ثم ثان ثم ثالث، ثم ينتهي إلى موجودات محصورة، كذلك يشترط الترتيب في الموجود حتى حصل موجود له أول ثم ثان ثم ثالث، فالترتيب في الذوات تقدمًا وتأخرًا كالترتيب في الموجودات أولا وآخرا، وهذا بناء على ما ذكرناه من إثبات قسم آخر في أقسام التقدم والتأخر، وذلك واجب الرعاية.
And as for the discussion on the second premise: that what may possibly exist [must exist]. We say: this statement is contradictory in wording and meaning. As for wording, possibility and necessity are contradictory unless it is said: what may possibly exist in itself must exist by another. It is said: This too is false, for necessity by another only occurs by the necessitation of the other, and the possible only needs the Necessary in its existence, not in its necessity. So its existence is by the bringing into existence of the other, and then its necessity follows from the aspect of relation. And this meaning has been clarified previously. As for the contradiction in meaning: it is that the possible of existence is of an infinite [regress], so if what is possible of existence were necessary of existence, then what is infinite would exist all at once. And if arrangement is required among essences such that there comes into existence a first, then a second, then a third, and then it ends with finite existents, similarly arrangement is required among the existent such that there comes to be an existent having a first, then a second, then a third. So arrangement in essences in priority and posteriority is like arrangement in existents as first and last. And this is based on what we mentioned of establishing another division in the divisions of priority and posteriority, and that must be observed.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage examines the opponent's premise that possible existents necessarily exist. It points out a verbal contradiction: 'possible' and 'necessary' are opposites. Even if reinterpreted as 'necessary by another', that fails because necessity by another comes from the other's causing, not from the thing itself. The meaning contradiction shows that if all possibles were necessary, they would all exist at once, but actual existence shows temporal order, which requires arrangement. So the premise is invalid.

Key Terms
مقدمة
جواز
وجوب
بالغير
ترتيب
تقدم وتأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'possible by itself, necessary by another' resolves the contradiction between possibility and necessity.
Correction: The author argues that necessity by another only applies to existence, not to the thing's own attribute, so the contradiction remains.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence if all possible things were necessary?
A) They would exist one after another
B) They would exist all at once
C) They would be contingent
D) They would need a specifying agent

Key Takeaway
The claim that 'what may possibly exist must exist' is invalid because it collapses the distinction between possibility and necessity and implies infinite simultaneous existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you hold two contradictory beliefs or actions (e.g., claiming trust in Allah but planning anxiously) and try to patch them with a superficial excuse instead of honestly confronting the contradiction?
?
The author argues that possibility and necessity cannot be merged. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes treat your own efforts (possible) as if they are necessary for outcomes, forgetting that only Allah is truly Necessary? How can you reorder your heart to rely on Him alone?
?
The argument about infinite regress shows that order implies a First. If you examine your daily priorities, what 'first' do you actually put before everything else? Is it your own will, your desires, or the command of the Necessary Being? Does your actual order reflect that belief?

Objection to the Philosophers' Limitation of Existents
The author questions why existents are limited in number and space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallim's key objection to the philosophers' emanation theory regarding the arbitrary limitation of existents in number and space.

Core Idea
If emanation has no particularity, why are the recipients limited to specific numbers?

The philosophers argued that existents are finite in number and space because emanation is measured by the capacity of the recipients. The mutakallimun, however, turn this reasoning back on itself: if the recipients determine the quantity, then why are those recipients themselves limited to a specific number? This passage captures that incisive objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the opening question uses rhetorical force.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and the opening question
The passage begins with the mutakallim's central question: what necessitates the limitation of existents? Note that emanation (fayḍ) is said to have no particularity.
3
Examine the hypothetical response and the counter-questions
The philosophers might say that limitation comes from the capacity of the bearers. The mutakallim then asks: why are those bearers themselves limited to specific numbers (e.g., seven heavens, four elements)?
These counter-questions are the core of the objection.
4
Reflect on the asymmetry between space and time
The mutakallim points out that the philosophers accept infinite time (motions of heavens) but reject infinite space (heavens extending endlessly). Ask yourself: is this arbitrary?
This highlights an inconsistency in the philosophers' system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ما الموجب لحصر الموجودات في هذه الذوات ولا خصوص في الفيض، وإن تناهي الموجودات عددًا ومكانا كتناهي الموجودات بداية وزمانًا ؟ فإن قلتم : إن الخصوص عندنا يرجع إلى قبول الحوامل فيقدر الفيض بمقدارها. قيل : وكلامنا في أصل الحوامل لِمَ انحصر في عدد معلوم فلم انحصرت السموات في سبع أو تسع ؟ ولم انحصرت العناصر في أربعة؟ ولم انحصرت المركبات في عدد معلوم؟ ولم تناهت هذه الموجودات فهلا ذهبت السموات إلى غير نهاية مكانًا ، كما ذهبت حركاتها إلى غير نهاية زمانًا.
We say: What necessitates the limitation of existents to these essences, with no particularity in emanation, and that existents are finite in number and place just as existents are finite in beginning and time? If you say: The particularity according to us goes back to the receptivity of the bearers, so the emanation is measured by their capacity. It is said: Our discussion concerns the origin of the bearers—why are they limited to a known number? Why are the heavens limited to seven or nine? Why are the elements limited to four? Why are the composites limited to a known number? And why are these existents finite? Why did the heavens not go on infinitely in place, just as their motions go on infinitely in time?
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an objection from the mutakallimun against the philosophers' emanation theory. The author presents a question: what necessitates the limitation of the number and spatial extent of existents? The philosophers claim that emanation (fayḍ) has no particularity in itself; limitation arises from the capacity of the recipients (al-ḥawāmil). The mutakallim turns the argument back: if the recipients determine the quantity, why are those recipients themselves limited to a specific number? Why are the heavens seven or nine? Why are the elements four? Why are composites limited? And why does the spatial series of heavens end, while their temporal motion is infinite? The argument highlights that the philosophers arbitrarily accept infinity in time but not in space, without offering a principled reason.

Key Terms
موجودات (existents)
فيض (emanation)
حوامل (bearers/receptacles)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallim is arguing that limitation itself is impossible.
Correction: No, the mutakallim is arguing that the philosophers' specific claims about the number of bearers and the finitude of space lack a necessary reason, not that limitation is impossible altogether.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the mutakallim's objection in this passage?
A) To prove that the heavens are infinite
B) To challenge the philosophers' claim that emanation has no particularity by questioning the limitation of the bearers
C) To argue that time is finite
D) To defend the philosophers' theory of emanation

Key Takeaway
The burden of proof falls on the philosophers to explain why the bearers of emanation are limited to specific numbers and why space is finite while time is infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun challenge the philosophers for accepting infinite time but finite space. In your own life, do you accept certain limitations (e.g., your circumstances, abilities, or lifespan) as simply 'given' without seeking the wisdom behind them? This lesson calls you to examine: are there beliefs or habits you hold inconsistently? Commit today to asking Allah for insight into the limits He has placed on you and to trusting that each one has a purpose.
?
You believe in Allah's infinite power and wisdom. But do you sometimes unconsciously limit His mercy or justice in your mind? For example, do you find it easier to trust His forgiveness than His justice, or vice versa? The philosophers' asymmetry between time and space mirrors a possible asymmetry in your own heart. Reflect: is there an attribute of Allah you struggle to reconcile? Repent of any limited view of His perfection and ask Him to strengthen your tawheed.

Reconciling Three Views on Acquisition
Al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Qadi on kasb and khalq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the classical scholars al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Qadi agreed on the substantive distinction between acquisition (kasb) and creation (khalq), differing only in terminology.

Core Idea
Three scholars, one distinction: acquisition vs. creation, only the words differ.

This passage from a work of kalam reconciles the definitions of three major Sunni theologians on the nature of human action. It shows that despite different phrasing, they all affirm that acquisition involves human power alongside divine creation, while creation is exclusive to God. The passage clarifies that the differences are merely terminological.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three scholars
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that 'al-Ustadh' refers to al-Ash'ari, 'al-Ustadh Abu Bakr' is al-Baqillani, and 'al-Qadi' is likely al-Qadi Abu Bakr or another.
These titles are respectful designations for leading scholars.
2
Compare the definitions of kasb and khalq
Notice that for al-Ash'ari, kasb involves the action occurring by human power but with impossibility of independence, while khalq allows independence. For al-Baqillani, kasb is power related in some aspect, not all. Both agree on the core idea.
The passage explicitly says al-Baqillani's view explains al-Ash'ari's.
3
Understand the reconciliation with al-Qadi
The author states there is no difference except in terminology: what the first two call 'aspect' (wajh) and 'consideration' (i'tibar), al-Qadi calls 'attribute' (sifa) and 'state' (hal). This confirms substantial agreement.
This resolves any apparent disagreement among the schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا تحقيق ما قاله الأستاذ أن كل فعل وقع على التعاون كان كسبا للمستعين، وحقيقة الكسب من المكتسب هو وقوع الفعل بقدرته مع تعذر انفراده به، وحقيقة الخلق هو وقوع الفعل بقدرته مع صحة انفراده به، وهذا أيضا شرح لما قاله الأستاذ أبو بكر أن الكسب هو أن تتعلق القدرة به على وجه ما، وإن لم تتعلق به من جميع الوجوه والخلق هو إنشاء العين وإيجاد من العدم، فلا فرق بين قوليهما وقول القاضي إلا أن ما سمياه وجها واعتبارا سماه القاضي صفة وحالا.
And this is a verification of what the teacher (al-Ash'ari) said: that every action that occurs through cooperation is an acquisition for the one seeking assistance, and the reality of acquisition from the acquirer is that the action occurs by his power while his being alone in it is impossible, and the reality of creation is that the action occurs by his power while his being alone in it is possible. And this is also an explanation of what the teacher Abu Bakr said: that acquisition is that the power is related to it in some manner, even if it is not related to it from all aspects, and creation is the bringing into existence of the entity and creation ex nihilo. So there is no difference between their two statements and the statement of al-Qadi except that what they called 'aspect' and 'consideration' he called 'attribute' and 'state'.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage verifies and explains the positions of al-Ash'ari, Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, and al-Qadi on kasb and khalq. Al-Ash'ari is reported as saying that any action occurring through cooperation (i.e., with divine assistance) is an acquisition for the human agent, meaning the action occurs by the human's power but cannot be done independently. Creation, by contrast, occurs by one's power with the possibility of independence. Abu Bakr describes acquisition as the power being related to the action in some aspect, not all aspects, and creation as bringing the entity into existence from nothing. The author concludes that there is no substantive difference between these views and that of al-Qadi; the only disagreement is terminological—what the first two call 'aspect' (wajh) and 'consideration' (i'tibar), al-Qadi calls 'attribute' (sifa) and 'state' (hal). Thus, all agree on the conceptual distinction between creation and acquisition.

Misconception: These scholars fundamentally disagreed on the nature of acquisition.
Correction: The passage shows they agreed on the core distinction; only the terms used to describe it differ.

B) Creation allows independent action, acquisition does not.
C) Acquisition involves partial knowledge, creation does not.
D) Creation changes the agent, acquisition does not.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference between creation and acquisition is not a matter of substantive disagreement among these scholars; it is a difference in terminology.

Al-Hayth as Mental Consideration
Defines al-hayth as mental consideration and lists ambiguous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'al-hayth' in kalam terminology refers to a mental consideration, not a real attribute, and that many key terms are ambiguous because they indicate such mental distinctions.

Core Idea
Al-hayth is a mental lens, not a real label.

This short but essential passage from Nihayat al-Aqdam clarifies a foundational point: when theologians speak of 'aspects' or 'considerations,' they are referring to mental distinctions, not actual divisions in reality. Understanding this prevents confusion when encountering terms like 'qa'im bi al-dhat' or 'qa'im bi al-ghayr.' Let's see how the author explains it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author uses 'hayth' in the example of the accident.
The Arabic uses 'الحيث' and 'الاعتبار العقلي' – mental consideration.
2
Identify the core definition
The author explicitly states what 'al-hayth' means. Find that sentence and understand that it is a mental consideration, not a physical attribute.
3
Review the list of ambiguous terms
The author concludes by listing several terms that are 'mushtaraka' (ambiguous). Note each pair: al-qa'im bi al-dhat/al-qa'im bi al-ghayr, al-dukhul/al-khuruj, al-hayth/al-jiha. Ask why each is open to different mental considerations.
These terms are often misinterpreted as referring to real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحيث أيضا قد اتسع حتى يقال: هذا في العرض من حيث هو موجود له حكم، ومن حيث هو عرض له حكم وليس يعني بإطلاق لفظ الحيث إلا الاعتبار العقلي والوجوه العقلية. والحاصل في هذا السؤال أن الألفاظ التي أوردوه كلها مشتركة، ولفظ القايم لبالذات والقايم بالغير، ولفظ الدخول والخروج، ولفظ الحيث والجهة.
The 'hayth' also has become broad such that one says: this is in the accident inasmuch as it exists has a ruling, and inasmuch as it is an accident has a ruling. And by the absolute use of the word 'al-hayth' nothing is meant except the mental consideration and the mental aspects. And the conclusion in this question is that the terms they mentioned are all common (mushtaraka): the term 'al-qa'im bi al-dhat' (subsistent by self) and 'al-qa'im bi al-ghayr' (subsistent by other), the term 'al-dukhul' (entering) and 'al-khuruj' (exiting), and the term 'al-hayth' (aspect) and 'al-jiha' (direction).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'al-hayth' (aspect/respect) in kalam terminology. The author explains that 'hayth' is used broadly to refer to the different rulings that apply to a thing from different considerations—for instance, an accident considered from its existence versus from its being an accident. He clarifies that by 'al-hayth' is meant only a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli) and mental aspects (wujuh 'aqliyya), not a real attribute. Then he concludes that the terms mentioned in the question—such as al-qa'im bi al-dhat vs. al-qa'im bi al-ghayr, al-dukhul vs. al-khuruj, and al-hayth vs. al-jiha—are all ambiguous (mushtaraka). The point is to avoid misunderstanding by recognizing that these terms refer to mental distinctions, not real divisions in the object.

Misconception: One might think that 'al-hayth' refers to a real, objective attribute or direction inherent in a thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'al-hayth' is merely a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli) and not a real attribute.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Order of Letters and Obligatory Consequences
The author states a principle of speech ordering and lists obligatory consequences for opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of letter ordering in speech and the kalām argument that opponents must accept temporal entities alongside eternal ones.

Core Idea
Speech requires order; temporal attributes demand eternal counterparts.

This passage from Niḥāyat al-Aqdām presents two interconnected points. First, the author establishes a fundamental rule for spoken language: letters must follow a specific order to form coherent speech. Second, he applies a similar logic to theological disputes, showing that opponents who accept temporal attributes must also affirm corresponding eternal entities. This argument is a classic example of kalām reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the principle of speech order and the list of obligatory consequences.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the speech ordering principle
The author states that no letter is inherently prior to another, but order is necessary for speech. Reflect on why this principle matters for language.
This is about the necessity of sequential order in spoken words.
3
Examine the obligatory consequences
The author lists three pairs: temporal atoms with eternal atom, temporal sciences with eternal science, temporal will with eternal volition. Why would opponents be forced to accept these?
These are 'ilzāmāt' – arguments that corner the opponent.
4
Reflect on the kalām method
Consider how the author uses a simple linguistic principle to build a theological argument. This reflects the mutakallimūn's approach of reasoning from common premises.
The argument assumes that speech order is agreed upon, then applies it to attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني، فليس الكاف بالتقديم أولى من النون، وكذلك كل حرف يجب أن يسبق حرفا في القول والتكلم حتى يكون بترتبه وتعاقبه كلامًا مسموعا. ومن الإلزامات إثبات أكوان حادثة مع الكون القديم، وإثبات علوم حادثة مع العلم القديم؛ كإثبات إرادة حادثة مع المشيئة القديمة، وقد ذكر المتكلمون ذلك في كتبهم.
The second: the kāf is not more deserving of precedence than the nūn, and likewise every letter must precede a letter in speech and utterance so that by its order and succession it becomes audible speech. And among the obligatory consequences is the affirmation of temporal atoms with the eternal atom, and the affirmation of temporal sciences with the eternal science, like the affirmation of a temporal will with the eternal volition. The mutakallimūn have mentioned this in their books.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct but related points. First, the author states a principle regarding the composition of speech: in any spoken utterance, letters must follow a specific order; no letter is inherently prior to another. This is a fundamental rule for language. Second, the author lists a series of 'obligatory consequences' (ilzāmāt) that opponents must accept if they hold certain views. These consequences include positing temporal atoms (akwān ḥāditha) alongside the eternal atom (al-kawn al-qadīm), temporal sciences with eternal science, and a temporal will with eternal volition. The author notes that the mutakallimūn have discussed these in their works, implying that these are established arguments in kalām.

Correction: In kalām terminology, 'أكوان حادثة' specifically refers to 'temporal atoms' – the accidents that arise and pass away, contrasted with the eternal atom (al-kawn al-qadīm). It is a technical term, not a general reference to beings.

The Five Forms of Ta'til (Divestment)
The author classifies the five forms of ta'til (divesting God of attributes).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five forms of ta'til (divestment) as classified by the author and the breadth of this concept in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Ta'til is not just atheism—it has five distinct forms.

The author begins the fifth principle by classifying the types of ta'til. Ta'til, often translated as divestment or denial, is not limited to rejecting God's existence. It includes denying His role in creation, His attributes, His names, and even the apparent meanings of scripture. This passage lays out these five forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the full list.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ومنها' which introduces each form.
2
Identify the five forms
List each of the five forms mentioned. Note the object being divested in each case.
The forms progress from external to internal aspects.
3
Understand the classification logic
Reflect on why the author distinguishes these five. What does each form deny?
They cover denial of God's existence, action, attributes, names, and scriptural meaning.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this classification broadens the concept of ta'til beyond mere atheism.
This shows the depth of Islamic theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة في إبطال مذهب التعطيل وبيان وجوه التعطيل. وقد قيل: إن التعطيل ينصرف إلى وجوه شتى؛ فمنها تعطيل الصنع عن الصانع، ومنها تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع، ومنها تعطيل الباري سبحانه عن الصفات الأزلية الذاتية القائمة بذاته، ومنها تعطيل الباري سبحانه عن الأسماء أزلا، ومنها تعطيل ظواهر الكتاب والسنة عن المعاني التي دلت عليها.
The fifth principle: On invalidating the doctrine of ta'til (divesting) and explaining the forms of ta'til. And it has been said: Divesting (ta'til) divides into several forms; among them is divesting the created world (as-san') of its Maker (as-sāni'), and among them is divesting the Maker of [the act of] creation, and among them is divesting the Blessed and Exalted of the eternal essential attributes subsisting in His Essence, and among them is divesting the Blessed and Exalted of the names from pre-eternity, and among them is divesting the outward expressions of the Book and the Sunna of the meanings they indicate.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of ta'til (denial or divestment) according to the author. It begins with the heading of the fifth principle (al-qā'ida al-khāmisa), which aims at abolishing the doctrine of ta'til and explaining its various forms. The author then states that ta'til branches into several distinct types. First: divesting the created world (as-san') of its Creator (as-sāni'), i.e., denying that the universe has a Maker. Second: divesting the Creator of the act of creation, meaning denying that God creates. Third: divesting the Blessed and Exalted of His eternal essential attributes that subsist in His Essence, i.e., denying the divine attributes. Fourth: divesting God of His names from pre-eternity, i.e., denying the divine names. Fifth: divesting the external meanings of the Qur'an and Sunna of the meanings they indicate, i.e., engaging in metaphorical or esoteric interpretations that strip texts of their apparent sense. This enumeration shows that ta'til is broader than simple atheism and includes various forms of denial within Islamic theology.

ظواهر الكتاب والسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'til only refers to denying God's existence.
Correction: Ta'til includes five distinct forms: denial of the Creator, denial of His creation, denial of His attributes, denial of His names, and denial of the apparent meanings of scripture.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Sixth Principle: Historical Context and Definition of States (Ahwal)
Historical context and division of states into caused and uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical background of the theory of states (ahwal) in Islamic theology and the foundational division of states into caused and uncaused.

Core Idea
The state (hal) defies real definition and is divided into caused and uncaused categories.

The concept of 'state' (hal) was introduced into kalam by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, sparking intense debate among theologians. This lesson sets the stage by reviewing the main positions and then explaining how states are classified. Understanding this context is essential before diving into the arguments for and against states.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical overview
Read the first part of the passage that recounts the views of key theologians on states. Notice who affirmed and who negated them.
This shows the significance of the issue.
2
Identify the author's plan
The author outlines what he will explain: definition of state, doctrines of affirmers and negators, proofs, and assessment.
This gives us a roadmap for the rest of this principle.
3
Examine the definition challenge
The author says a real definition of 'state' is impossible because it would lead to a regress. Understand why.
This is a key methodological point.
4
Learn the division of states
The author divides states into caused and uncaused. Note the examples: caused states are judgments of meanings, uncaused are attributes not derived from meanings.
This classification is central to the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة في الأحوال اعلم أن المتكلمين قد اختلفوا في الأحوال نفيًا وإثباتًا، بعد أن أحدث أبو هاشم بن الجباي رأيه فيها وما كانت المسألة مذكورة قبله أصلا فأثبتها أبو هاشم ونفاها أبوه الجباي، وأثبتها القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني رحمه الله بعد ترديد الرأي فيها على قاعدة غير ما ذهب إليه ، ونفاها صاحب مذهبه الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري وأصحابه رضي الله عنهم ، وكان إمام الحرمين من المثبتين في الأول والنافين في الآخر ، والأحرى بنا أن نبين أولا ما الحال التي توارد عليها النفي والإثبات؟ وما مذهب المثبتين فيها؟ وما مذهب النافين؟ ثم نتكلم في أدلة الفريقين، ونشير إلى مصدر القولين وصوابهما من وجه وخطأيهما من وجه. أما بيان الحال وما هو ؟ اعلم أنه ليس للحال حد حقيقي يذكر حتى نعرفها بحدها وحقيقتها على وجه يشمل جميع الأحوال، فإنه يودي إلى إثبات الحال للحال ، بل لها ضابط وحاصر بالقسمة وهي تنقسم إلى ما يعلل وإلى ما لا يعلل، وما يعلل فهي أحكام لمعان قايمة بذوات وما لا يعلل فهو صفات ليس أحكامًا للمعاني.
The Sixth Principle: On States. Know that the theologians have differed concerning states in negation and affirmation, after Abu Hashim ibn al-Jubba'i introduced his view about them, and the issue had not been mentioned before him at all. So Abu Hashim affirmed them, and his father al-Jubba'i negated them. And Judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani—may God have mercy on him—affirmed them after vacillating in opinion, on a basis different from what Abu Hashim held. And the leader of his school, Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his companions—may God be pleased with them—negated them. And Imam al-Haramayn was among the affirmers in the beginning and among the negators in the end. It is more appropriate for us to first explain what is the 'state' (hal) concerning which negation and affirmation have been applied? And what is the doctrine of the affirmers? And what is the doctrine of the negators? Then we will discuss the proofs of the two groups, and indicate the source of the two views and their correctness in one respect and their error in another. As for the explanation of the state and what it is: Know that the state has no real definition that we can mention so that we know it by its definition and reality in a way that includes all states, because that would lead to affirming a state for a state. Rather, it has a delimitation and a limit by division. It divides into what is caused (yua‘llal) and what is not caused. What is caused are judgments (ahkam) of meanings (ma‘ani) subsisting in essences, and what is not caused are attributes (sifat) that are not judgments of meanings.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new principle (qā‘ida) on the theory of states (al-aḥwāl) in kalām. It first provides historical context: the concept of 'state' was introduced by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, and then debated. His father al-Jubba'i negated states, while al-Baqillani affirmed them on a different basis. Al-Ash'ari and his followers negated them, and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni initially affirmed then later negated. The author then outlines his plan: first define what is meant by 'state', then present the positions, then discuss proofs. In defining the state, the author states that it cannot have a real definition that covers all instances, because that would lead to an infinite regress (a state for the state). Instead, it is delimited by a division: states are divided into those that are caused (by a meaning) and those that are not. The caused states are 'judgments' (aḥkām) of meanings that subsist in essences (e.g., the state of 'being knowing' caused by the meaning of knowledge), while the uncaused states are attributes that are not judgments of meanings (e.g., perhaps essential attributes that are not derived from a meaning). This sets up the technical framework for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
أحوال
حال
تعليل
معنى
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that a 'state' (hal) is an independently existing entity like a substance.
Correction: In kalam, a state is neither existent nor non-existent; it is a theoretical construct that cannot be defined in a real sense without regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say there is no real definition of 'state' (hal) that covers all states?
A) Because states are unknowable
B) Because it would lead to affirming a state for a state
C) Because the theologians never agreed on a definition
D) Because it is a merely linguistic term

Key Takeaway
The theory of states (ahwal) is a contentious topic in kalam, and its foundational definition reveals that states cannot be fully defined in a real sense but are divided into caused judgments and uncaused attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Knowing' or 'Powerful', do you unconsciously treat these as separate entities (meanings) that cause His attributes? How does the division of states into caused and uncaused challenge your mental habit of causally linking everything about God?
?
The author says a real definition of 'state' is impossible without circularity. Have you ever tried to define your faith in God in a way that ended up relying on the very thing you were defining? What does that reveal about the limits of human reason in theology?
?
If some states are uncaused and not judgments of meanings, then there are aspects of God's essence that simply are, without explanation. Can you accept that about your own understanding of God, or do you feel the need to rationalize every attribute? What would change if you surrendered the need to explain everything?

The Controversy Over States (Ahwal) Among Theologians
Introduction to the controversy over states and the author's roadmap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origins of the debate over states (ahwal) in kalam and the author's plan for addressing it.

Core Idea
The debate over states was introduced by Abu Hashim and split major theologians.

This passage opens the sixth principle concerning states. Before diving into definitions and proofs, the author first recounts who took which position and sets out a clear plan. Understanding this historical backdrop is essential for following the arguments that come later.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical introduction
Read the passage carefully, noting the names of the theologians and their positions on states. Pay attention to the timeline: Abu Hashim originated the idea.
2
Identify the unique contribution
Notice that Abu Hashim is credited with first raising the question. Before him, the topic was not discussed. This is a key historical point.
3
Understand the author's plan
The author lists four steps: (1) define 'state', (2) doctrines of affirmers, (3) doctrines of deniers, (4) proofs and evaluation. This outlines the structure of the upcoming discussion.
4
Reflect on significance
Ask yourself why the concept of states matters in kalam. It relates to how we understand Allah's attributes—are they identical to His essence or additional?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المتكلمين قد اختلفوا في الأحوال نفيًا وإثباتًا، بعد أن أحدث أبو هاشم بن الجباي رأيه فيها وما كانت المسألة مذكورة قبله أصلا فأثبتها أبو هاشم ونفاها أبوه الجباي ، وأثبتها القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني رحمه الله بعد ترديد الرأي فيها على قاعدة غير ما ذهب إليه ، ونفاها صاحب مذهبه الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري وأصحابه رضي الله عنهم ، وكان إمام الحرمين من المثبتين في الأول والنافين في الآخر ، والأحرى بنا أن نبين أولا ما الحال التي توارد عليها النفي والإثبات؟ وما مذهب المثبتين فيها؟ وما مذهب النافين؟ ثم نتكلم في أدلة الفريقين، ونشير إلى مصدر القولين وصوابهما من وجه وخطأيهما من وجه.
Know that the theologians have differed concerning states (al-ahwal) by denial and affirmation, after Abu Hashim ibn al-Jubba'i originated his opinion concerning them, and the question was not mentioned before him at all. So Abu Hashim affirmed them, and his father al-Jubba'i denied them. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, may Allah have mercy on him, affirmed them after vacillating in opinion concerning them, on a basis different from what he [Abu Hashim] had held. The master of his school, Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his companions, may Allah be pleased with them, denied them. And Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] was among the affirmers at first and among the deniers at the end. It is more appropriate for us to first explain what the state (al-hal) is which is the subject of denial and affirmation, and what is the doctrine of the affirmers, and what is the doctrine of the deniers, then to discuss the proofs of the two groups, and to indicate the source of the two views, their correctness in one respect and their error in another.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the sixth principle (al-qa'ida al-sadisa) concerning states (ahwal). The author begins by informing that theologians differed over states—whether they exist or not. This topic was first introduced by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i; before him, the question was not even mentioned. Then the author lists the positions: Abu Hashim affirmed states; his father Abu Ali al-Jubba'i denied them; al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani affirmed them but on a different basis than Abu Hashim; the founder of the Ash'ari school, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and his followers denied them; and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) initially affirmed then later denied. The author then states the plan: to first clarify what a 'state' is that is the subject of this dispute, then the doctrines of each group, then discuss the proofs, and finally point out the source of the two views and their correctness and error from different angles. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion.

The State: Definition and Division
Defines 'state' and divides it into caused and uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a 'state' cannot be truly defined and how it is divided into caused and uncaused categories.

Core Idea
A state has no real definition, only a controlling rule and a twofold division.

In this passage, the author tackles a foundational concept: what is a 'state' (hal)? He explains that a true definition is impossible because it would lead to circularity. Instead, we have a practical rule and a division into two types. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of states in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's cautious approach: he says there is no real definition but only a controlling rule and division.
The Arabic uses 'ضابط وحاصر' (controlling rule and comprehensive force).
2
Understand why no real definition
The author argues that a real definition would lead to affirming a state for a state (يثبت الحال للحال). Think about why this would be problematic: it would be circular or involve infinite regress.
This is a key subtlety in kalam methodology.
3
Identify the two divisions
The passage divides states into 'what is caused' (ما يعلل) and 'what is not caused' (ما لا يعلل). Note the definitions given for each: caused states are judgments for meanings, uncaused are simply attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask: Why does this distinction matter? It helps in analyzing attributes of Allah and created things, determining which are essential and which are relational.
This connects to later discussions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بيان الحال وما هو ؟ اعلم أنه ليس للحال حد حقيقي يذكر حتى نعرفها بحدها وحقيقتها على وجه يشمل جميع الأحوال، فإنه يودي إلى إثبات الحال للحال، بل لها ضابط وحاصر بالقسمة وهي تنقسم إلى ما يعلل وإلى ما لا يعلل، وما يعلل فهي أحكام لمعان قايمة بذوات وما لا يعلل فهو صفات ليس أحكامًا للمعاني.
As for the explanation of the state (al-hal) and what it is: know that there is no real definition (hadd haqiqi) for the state that can be mentioned so that we know it by its definition and its reality in a way that includes all states, for that would lead to affirming a state for a state. Rather, it has a controlling rule (dabit) and a comprehensive force by division, and it is divided into what is caused (ma yu'allal) and what is not caused (ma la yu'allal). What is caused: they are judgments (ahkam) for meanings (ma'ani) subsisting in selves (dhawat). What is not caused: they are attributes (sifat) that are not judgments for meanings.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage provides the definition and classification of 'state' (al-hal). The author states that a 'state' cannot be defined in a true definition (hadd haqiqi) because that would require another state, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. Instead, we have a controlling principle (dabit) and a comprehensive division (hasir bi al-qisma). The state is divided into two categories: (1) what is caused (ma yu'allal) - these are judgments (ahkam) that are consequences of meanings (ma'ani) subsisting in entities, and (2) what is not caused (ma la yu'allal) - these are attributes (sifat) that are not judgments of meanings. This sets up the detailed discussion to follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that states can be defined in the usual way, like substances or accidents.
Correction: The author explains that a real definition would lead to circularity; instead, we use a division-based approach.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A state cannot be truly defined; instead, it is understood through a controlling rule and a division into caused (judgments of meanings) and uncaused (attributes independent of meanings).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Are you comfortable with some attributes being uncaused, not dependent on any meaning? Why or why not, and what does your answer reveal about your view of God?

Two Definitions of Caused States
Explains the first type of state, with views of Abu Hashim and al-Qadi al-Baqillani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on caused states (Abu Hashim vs. al-Qadi al-Baqillani) and how they define the scope of states.

Core Idea
The definition of 'caused state' determines which attributes of beings count as states.

This passage introduces the first category of states (ahwal) — those that are caused. It contrasts the narrower definition of Abu Hashim, who requires life as a condition, with the broader definition of al-Qadi al-Baqillani, who includes any non-existential attribute. The text also touches on Ibn al-Jubba'i's nuanced view regarding motion and structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the three scholars' views. Pay attention to key terms like 'illa', 'mahall', and 'ma'na'.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's definition
Note that Abu Hashim limits caused states to judgments whose cause (illa) requires life. Examples: being alive, knowing, powerful.
These states are superadded attributes, not identical to the cause.
3
Contrast with al-Qadi's broader view
Al-Qadi defines a state as any attribute of an existent that is not itself existent, whether or not it requires life. Examples include being moving, stationary, black, white.
This expands the category significantly.
4
Understand Ibn al-Jubba'i's exception
Ibn al-Jubba'i differs on motion and possibly all modes (akwan), requiring structure (bunya) as a condition. For him, the whole structure is considered a single locus.
This shows the complexity of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فكل حكم لعلة قامت بذات يشترط في ثبوتها الحياة عند أبي هاشم ؛ ككون الحي حيًا عالماً قادراً مريداً سميعاً بصيراً ؛ لأن كونه حيًا عالماً يعلل بالحياة والعلم في الشاهد ، فتقوم الحياة بمحل ، وتوجب كون المحل حيا، وكذلك العلم والقدرة ،والإرادة وكل ما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة، وتسمى هذه الأحكام أحوالاً، وهي صفات زايدة على المعاني التي أوجبتها، وعند القاضي رحمه الله كل صفة الموجود لا تتصف بالوجود فهي حال سواء كان المعنى الموجب مما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة، أو لم يشترط ؛ يكون الحي حيا وعالماً وقادرًا، وكون المتحرك متحركا، والساكن ساكنا، والأسود والأبيض، إلى غير ذلك. ولا بن الجباي في المتحرك اختلاف رأي، وربما يطرد ذلك في الأكوان كلها، ولما كانت البنية عنده شرطًا في المعاني التي تشترط في ثبوتها الحياة، وكانت البنية في أجزائها في حكم محل واحد فتوصف بالحال لوعند القاضي أبي بكر رحمه الله لا يوصف بالحال إلا الجزء الذي قام به المعنى فقط.
As for the first [category]: every judgment (hukm) for a cause (illa) that subsists in a self (dhat), whose subsistence requires life according to Abu Hashim – such as a living being being alive, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing; because his being alive and knowing is caused by life and knowledge in the witnessed realm (al-shahid). So life inheres in a locus (mahall) and necessitates that the locus be alive. Likewise, knowledge, power, will, and everything whose subsistence requires life. These judgments are called states (ahwal), and they are attributes superadded to the meanings that caused them. According to al-Qadi (may Allah have mercy on him), every attribute of an existent (sifa al-mawjud) that is not qualified by existence is a state, whether the causing meaning requires life or not. Thus, the living being is alive, knowing, and powerful; the moving being is moving; the stationary is stationary; the black and white, etc. As for Ibn al-Jubba'i, concerning the moving there is a difference of opinion, and perhaps that applies consistently to all modes (akwan). Since, according to him, structure (al-bunya) is a condition for the meanings that require life, and the structure in its parts is considered a single locus, so it is described by the state. According to al-Qadi Abu Bakr (may Allah have mercy on him), only the part that subsists in the meaning alone is described by the state.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage details the first category of states: those that are caused (ma yu'allal). It presents the views of Abu Hashim and al-Qadi. For Abu Hashim, a caused state is a judgment that results from a cause (illa) that requires life. Examples: being alive, knowing, powerful, etc. These are states superadded to the meaning (e.g., life, knowledge). Then the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) is broader: any attribute of an existent that is not itself existent is a state, whether or not it requires life. He gives examples like being moving, stationary, black, white. Then it mentions Ibn al-Jubba'i's specific view on motion and structure (bunya). Finally, it notes that for al-Qadi, only the part that directly has the meaning is described by the state. This clarifies the scope of the first category.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all states require life as a condition, as per Abu Hashim, is the only view.
Correction: Al-Qadi al-Baqillani includes any non-existential attribute as a state, even without life (e.g., being moving or white).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first category of states includes all caused judgments, but kalam scholars disagreed on the scope: Abu Hashim restricts to those requiring life, while al-Qadi includes all non-existential attributes.

?

This lesson urges you to look beyond the surface. Take time today to reflect on one attribute you display and trace it back to its root meaning. Is it rooted in life (iman) or mere habit? Resolve to cultivate meaningful states.

The Second Category of States: Uncaused Attributes
Defines uncaused states with examples and a general rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second category of states (uncaused states) and their defining characteristic: they are attributes affirmed of a self without any additional cause.

Core Idea
Uncaused states are essential attributes that do not depend on additional causes beyond the self.

Sanusi now turns to the second category of states, which are those that are not caused by an additional cause (illa za'ida). These are essential attributes such as substantiality and existence. This lesson explains the definition, provides examples, and gives a general rule for identifying such states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the opening sentence that defines the second category: 'كل صفة إثبات لذات من غير علة زايدة'.
Notice the key phrase 'من غير علة زايدة' (without an additional cause).
2
Note the examples provided
The passage gives examples like substantiality (tahayyuz), existence, being an accident, color, and blackness. These illustrate what uncaused states are.
3
Understand the controlling rule
The author provides a general principle: every existent has a property (khassiya) by which it is distinguished, and that property is a state. Also, what makes similar things alike and different things differ is a state.
This rule helps identify states in general.
4
Relate to attributes of genera and species
These uncaused states are called 'attributes of genera and species' (sifat al-ajnas wa al-anwa'). Reflect on why they are essential and not dependent on additional causes.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the continuation likely elaborates further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني فهو كل صفة إثبات لذات من غير علة زايدة على الذات ؛ كتحيز الجوهر وكونه موجوداً ، وكون العرض عرضاً ولونا وسوادًا، والضابط أن كل موجود له خاصية يتميز بها عن غيره، فإنما يتميز بخاصية هي حال وما تتماثل المتماثلات به وتختلف المختلفات فيه فهو حال وهي التي تسمى صفات الأجناس والأنواع، والأحوال
As for the second category: it is every attribute of affirmation of a self (sifa ithbat li-dhat) without an additional cause (illa za'ida) beyond the self, such as the substantiality of a substance (tahayyuz al-jawhar) and its being existent, and an accident being an accident, color, and blackness. The controlling rule is that every existent has a property (khassiya) by which it is distinguished from others; it is only distinguished by a property that is a state. And that by which similar things are alike and different things differ is a state. These are called attributes of genera and species (sifat al-ajnas wa al-anwa'). And the states...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage explains the second category of states: those that are not caused (ma la yu'allal). These are attributes that are affirmed of a self without any additional cause beyond the self. Examples include the substantiality (tahayyuz) of a substance, its existence, an accident being an accident, color, blackness. The author then gives a general principle: every existent has a specific property (khassiya) by which it is distinguished from other things, and that property is a state. Moreover, that by which similar things are similar and different things differ is a state. These states are called attributes of genera and species (sifat al-ajnas wa al-anwa'). The passage ends with 'and the states...' suggesting continuation, but we only have this much.

Ahwal Conditioned by Life
Shahrastani defines the first type of ahwal conditioned by life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of ahwal (states) according to the Mu'tazili theologians, specifically those conditioned by life, and the disagreements about the scope of states.

Core Idea
Ahwal are attributes caused by meanings, but scholars disagreed on whether they require life.

This passage introduces the concept of 'states' (ahwal), which are attributes that are additional to the essential meanings in a thing. Shahrastani begins by discussing the first type: states that depend on life, according to Abu Hashim. He then presents a broader definition from al-Qadi al-Baqillani and notes further disagreements among the scholars. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the Mu'tazili theory of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the text and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'hal', 'ma'na', 'hukm'.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's definition
Look for the conditions that Abu Hashim sets for a judgment to be a state: it must be caused by a meaning that requires life. Note the examples given.
He mentions life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, seeing.
3
Contrast with al-Qadi's definition
Al-Qadi expands the definition: any attribute of an existent that is not itself qualified by existence is a state, regardless of the cause. This includes motion, rest, colors.
This is broader than Abu Hashim's definition.
4
Note the disagreements
Observe Ibn al-Jubba'i's differing view on motion and his condition of structure. Also note Qadi Abu Bakr's restriction that only the part containing the meaning is described by the state.
These nuances show the diversity of opinions within the Mu'tazili school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فكل حكم لعلة قامت بذات يشترط في ثبوتها الحياة عند أبي هاشم؛ ككون الحي حيًا عالمًا قادرًا مريدًا سميعًا بصيرًا؛ لأن كونه حيًا عالمًا يعلل بالحياة والعلم في الشاهد، فتقوم الحياة بمحل، وتوجب كون المحل حيًا، وكذلك العلم والقدرة، والإرادة وكل ما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة، وتسمى هذه الأحكام أحوالًا، وهي صفات زائدة على المعاني التي أوجبتها، وعند القاضي رحمه الله كل صفة الموجود لا تتصف بالوجود فهي حال سواء كان المعنى الموجب مما يشترط في ثبوته الحياة، أو لم يشترط؛ يكون الحي حيًا وعالمًا وقادرًا، وكون المتحرك متحركًا، والساكن ساكنًا، والأسود والأبيض، إلى غير ذلك. ولا بن الجباي في المتحرك اختلاف رأي، وربما يطرد ذلك في الأكوان كلها، ولما كانت البنية عنده شرطًا في المعاني التي تشترط في ثبوتها الحياة، وكانت البنية في أجزائها في حكم محل واحد فتوصف بالحال وعند القاضي أبي بكر رحمه الله لا يوصف بالحال إلا الجزء الذي قام به المعنى فقط.
As for the first: every judgment for a cause that subsists in the essence, its establishment requires life according to Abu Hashim; such as the living being being living, knowing, powerful, willing, hearing, seeing; because his being living, knowing is caused by life and knowledge in the witnessed world, so life subsists in a locus, and necessitates that the locus be living, and similarly knowledge, power, will, and everything whose establishment requires life, and these judgments are called states (ahwal), and they are attributes additional to the meanings (ma'ani) that caused them. According to al-Qadi (may Allah have mercy on him), every attribute of an existent that is not qualified by existence is a state, whether the causing meaning requires life or not; the living being is living, knowing, powerful, and the mover being moving, the stationary being stationary, the black and white, and so on. Ibn al-Jubba'i had a difference of opinion regarding the mover, and perhaps that applies to all modes (akwan). Since structure (al-bunyah) in his view is a condition for the meanings that require life for their establishment, and the structure in its parts is like a single locus, so it is qualified by the state. According to Qadi Abu Bakr (may Allah have mercy on him), only the part in which the meaning subsists is qualified by the state.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of al-ahwal (states) as attributes that depend on the prior existence of life in the subject. It begins with Abu Hashim's view that for a judgment to be a state, its cause must be conditioned by life. Examples include the living being being living, knowing, etc. These states are additional attributes over the meanings (like life, knowledge) that cause them. Then it mentions al-Qadi's (al-Baqillani) broader definition: any attribute of an existent that is not itself qualified by existence is a state, regardless of whether the cause requires life. This includes motion, rest, colors. Then it discusses Ibn al-Jubba'i's differing opinion on motion, and his requirement of structure (bunyah) as a condition for meanings that require life. Finally, it contrasts Qadi Abu Bakr's view that only the part containing the meaning is described by the state.

Key Terms
حال
معنى
حكم
علة
ذات
حياة
بنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that ahwal are separate existent entities that can exist independently.
Correction: This passage clarifies that ahwal are attributes that are not existent by themselves; they are additional to the meanings that cause them and cannot exist independently.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, what condition must be met for a judgment to be considered a state (hal)?
A) It must be caused by a meaning that requires life
B) It must be an attribute of an existent not qualified by existence
C) It must be a color
D) It must be a mode (kaw)

Key Takeaway
The first type of ahwal are attributes caused by meanings requiring life, but scholars differed on the scope and conditions of states.

The Second Type of Ahwal: Definition and Examples
Shahrastani defines the second type of states with examples and a general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what constitutes the second type of states (ahwal) according to Shahrastani, including their definition, the general rule, and examples like a substance's spatial occupancy and existence.

Core Idea
The second type of state is an attribute directly affirmed of an essence without an additional cause.

Shahrastani now focuses on the second division of states, which are essential attributes that do not require a separate cause. This includes fundamental characteristics like a substance's spatiality and an accident's color. In this passage, he provides a clear definition and a general rule for identifying such states.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition of the second type of state and the examples given.
Pay attention to the examples of substance and accident.
2
Identify the general rule
Find the sentence beginning with 'wa al-dabit' which states the general rule that every existent has a property by which it is distinguished.
3
Understand the terminology
Note terms like 'khassiyyah', 'ajnas', 'anwa'' and how they relate to states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني فهو كل صفة إثبات لذات من غير علة زائدة على الذات؛ كتحيز الجوهر وكونه موجودًا، وكون العرض عرضًا ولونًا وسوادًا، والضابط أن كل موجود له خاصية يتميز بها عن غيره، فإنما يتميز بخاصية هي حال وما تتماثل المتماثلات به وتختلف المختلفات فيه فهو حال وهي التي تسمى صفات الأجناس والأنواع، والأحوال عند المثبتين ليست موجودة ولا معدومة، ولا هي أشياء، ولا توصف بصفة ما، وعند ابن الجباي ليست هي معلومة على حيالها، وإنما تعلم مع الذات. وأما نفاة الأحوال فعندهم الأشياء تختلف وتتماثل لذواتها المعينة، وأما أسماء الأجناس والأنواع فيرجع عمومها إلى الألفاظ الدالة عليها فقط، وكذلك خصوصها، وقد يعلم الشيء من وجه ويجهل من وجه، والوجوه اعتبارات لا ترجع إلى صفات هي أحوال تختص بالذوات، وهذا تقرير مذهب الفريقين في تعريف الحال.
As for the second division: it is every attribute of affirmation for an essence without an additional cause beyond the essence; such as the substance's being in space (tahayyuz) and its being existent, and the accident's being an accident, a color, and blackness. The rule is that every existent has a property (khassiyyah) by which it is distinguished from others, and it is distinguished only by a property that is a state, and that by which similars are similar and by which differents differ is a state. These are called attributes of genera and species. The states according to the affirmers (al-muthbitun) are neither existent nor non-existent, nor are they things, nor are they described by any attribute. According to Ibn al-Jubba'i, they are not known by themselves, but only known with the essence. As for the deniers of states (al-nafah), in their view things differ and are similar by their specific essences (dhawat mu'ayyanah). As for the names of genera and species, their universality refers only to the words denoting them, and similarly their particularity. Sometimes a thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, and the aspects are considerations that do not refer to attributes that are states specific to essences. This is the exposition of the two schools regarding the definition of the state.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of states: attributes that are directly affirmed for an essence without requiring a separate cause. Examples include the substance's spatial occupation (tahayyuz) and existence, and the accident's being an accident or color. The general rule is that any distinguishing property is a state. Then it discusses the ontological status of states: they are neither existent nor non-existent, not things, not qualified. Ibn al-Jubba'i says they are only known with the essence. Finally, it presents the position of the deniers (nafah): they hold that things are similar and different by their essences, and universality and particularity are merely linguistic. The aspects are mental considerations, not real states. This concludes the definition of the two schools.

جوهر
عرض
خاصية

أنواع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That states are additional entities separate from the essence.
Correction: The definition clarifies that states are attributes of affirmation without an additional cause, meaning they are not separate entities but inherent characteristics.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the general rule for identifying a state?
A) It is an attribute with an additional cause
B) It is a property by which an existent is distinguished
C) It is the same for all existents
D) It is only for substances

Key Takeaway
The second type of state is a distinguishing property directly associated with an essence without requiring a separate cause.

The Ontological Status of Ahwal: Views of Affirmers and Deniers
Shahrastrani discusses the ontological status of states and the views of affirmers and deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological positions on the ontological status of states, including the affirmers' view that they are neither existent nor non-existent, and the deniers' view that universality is merely linguistic.

Core Idea
States are neither existent nor non-existent; they are known only with the essence.

After defining the second type of states, Shahrastani turns to the controversial question of their ontological reality. The affirmers of states (mainly the Mu'tazili scholar Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and his followers) hold that states are not existent entities but are known only with the essence. The deniers reject them entirely, arguing that similarity and difference are based on essences and that universal names are purely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmers' position
Read the part starting with 'wa al-ahwal 'inda al-muthbitin' to understand that states are neither existent nor non-existent.
Note the double negation: neither existent nor non-existent.
2
Note Ibn al-Jubba'i's addition
See that Ibn al-Jubba'i says states are only known with the essence.
3
Read the deniers' position
Read the part starting with 'wa amma nafat al-ahwal' to understand their rejection of states.
4
Understand the response
Note the deniers' argument that universality and particularity are just words, and aspects are mental considerations.

Sometimes a thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another, and the aspects are considerations that do not refer to attributes that are states specific to essences. This is the exposition of the two schools regarding the definition of the state.
