The Definition of State (Hal) in the Theory of States
Defines the state (hal) as per proponents and opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing definitions of the state (hal) according to the proponents and deniers in the theory of states.

Core Idea
The state is neither existent nor non-existent for proponents; for deniers, it is a mere mental consideration.

This passage from al-Shahrastani sets out the core disagreement about the ontological status of states (ahwal). Before we can discuss divine attributes, we need to understand whether states are real entities or merely conceptual. The passage defines the position of the proponents (muthbitun) and the deniers (nufat al-ahwal), clarifying what each group affirms or denies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct sections: one for proponents, one for deniers.
The passage is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the two groups and their core claims
Find where the proponents' view is stated (first part) and where the deniers' view is stated (from 'As for the deniers...'). Note the key terms: muthbitun, nufat al-ahwal.
3
Note the nuanced view of Ibn al-Jubba'i
Within the proponents' section, Ibn al-Jubba'i is mentioned separately. He adds that the state is not known by itself but only with the essence.
This is a subtle point within the proponents' stance.
4
Understand the deniers' alternative explanation
The deniers explain difference and similarity by essences, and they explain genus/species names as verbal. They reduce 'states' to mental considerations (i'tibarat).
5
Reflect on the importance for kalam
Consider why this debate matters: if states are real attributes, they can be used to discuss divine attributes without implying multiplicity or composition in God.
This connects to broader discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند المثبتين ليست موجودة ولا معدومة، ولا هي أشياء، ولا توصف بصفة ما، وعند ابن الجباي ليست هي معلومة على حيالها، وإنما تعلم مع الذات. وأما نفاة الأحوال فعندهم الأشياء تختلف وتتماثل لذواتها المعينة، وأما أسماء الأجناس والأنواع فيرجع عمومها إلى الألفاظ الدالة عليها فقط، وكذلك خصوصها، وقد يعلم الشيء من وجه ويجهل من وجه، والوجوه اعتبارات لا ترجع إلى صفات هي أحوال تختص بالذوات، وهذا تقرير مذهب الفريقين في تعريف الحال.
According to the proponents (al-muthbitun), it [the state] is neither existent nor non-existent, nor are they things, nor are they described by any attribute. According to Ibn al-Jubba'i, it is not known by itself, but is known along with the essence. As for the deniers of states (nufat al-ahwal), according to them, things differ and resemble each other by their specific essences (dhawat). As for the names of genera and species, their generality refers only to the words indicating them, and likewise their particularity. And a thing may be known from one aspect and unknown from another, and the aspects are mental considerations that do not refer to attributes that are states specific to essences. This is the presentation of the doctrine of the two groups regarding the definition of the state (hal).
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the state (hal) according to the two opposing groups in the theory of states. It begins by stating the view of the proponents (al-muthbitun): a state is neither existent nor non-existent, not a thing, and not described by any attribute. Then it mentions the specific view of Ibn al-Jubba'i that a state is not known by itself but only along with the essence. Next, it presents the view of the deniers (nufat al-ahwal): things differ and resemble by their own specific essences, not by additional states. According to them, the generality and particularity of genus and species names are merely verbal conventions. Knowledge and ignorance of a thing can be from different aspects, and these aspects are mental considerations (i'tibarat), not real attributes that are states. The passage concludes by stating that this exposition clarifies the respective positions on the definition of 'state'.

نفاة الأحوال
اعتبارات

Checkpoint

A) It is existent and known by itself.
B) It is neither existent nor non-existent, and not a thing.
C) It is an accident in the essence.
D) It is identical with the essence.

Key Takeaway
The state (hal) is defined differently: proponents see it as a real but non-existent attribute known with the essence, while deniers see it as a mere mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Futility of States for Definition and Inference
The author argues that affirming states, not denying them, blocks definition and inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that affirming states (ahwal) undermines the logical basis for definition, essence, inference, and deduction.

Core Idea
Affirming intermediate states destroys the binary logic needed for definition and proof.

In debates about states (ahwal), some claim that denying states cuts off the possibility of definition and inference. The author counters that the opposite is true: affirming states introduces an intermediary between existence and non-existence, which actually blocks definition and inference. This passage explains why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بالعكس أولي' which signals the author's reversal.
2
Identify the two opposing claims
Find the claim attributed to the negators of states and the author's counter-claim.
3
Understand the logical mechanism
The passage explains why a binary division between existence and non-existence is necessary for inference. See how an intermediary disrupts this.
Focus on 'غاية الناظر أن يأتي في نظره بتقسيم داير بين النفي والإثبات'.
4
Connect to definitions and essences
The final part ties the argument to the nature of definition and essence, emphasizing unique properties.
Note that for the negators, definition and essence are two expressions for the same thing.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument fits into the larger debate on states in kalam. Why is this logical point important?
This shows the intricate relationship between ontology and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن نفي الحال يؤدي إلى حسم باب الحد والحقيقة والنظر والاستدلال بالعكس أولي فإن إثبات الحال التي لا توصف بالوجود والعدم وتوصف بالثبوت دون الوجود حسم باب الحد والاستدلال، فإن غاية الناظر أن يأتي في نظره بتقسيم داير بين النفي والإثبات، فينفي أحدهما حتى يتعين الثاني، ومثبت الحال قد أتى بواسطة بين الوجود والعدم، فلم يفد التقسيم والإبطال علما ولا يتضمن النظر حصول معرفة أصلا، ثم الحد والحقيقة على أصل النفاة للأحوال عبارتان عن معبر واحد، فحد الشيء حقيقته وحقيقته ما اختص في ذاته عن ساير الأشياء، ولكل شيء خاصية بها يتميز عن غيره، وخاصيته تلزم ذاته ولا تفارقه، ولا يشترك فيها بوجه وإلا بطل الاختصاص.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying states (ahwal) cuts off the possibility of definition and inference.
Correction: According to the author, the opposite is true: affirming states introduces an intermediary between existence and non-existence, thereby blocking definition and inference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does affirming states block definition and inference?
A) Because states are too abstract to be used in definitions.
B) Because it introduces an intermediary between existence and non-existence, breaking the binary division needed for inference.
C) Because states are not real entities.
D) Because the negators of states reject all properties.

Key Takeaway
Affirming states as an intermediate category between existence and non-existence destroys the binary logical structure required for definition and inference, thus the problem lies with affirming states, not denying them.

The Reality of States: An Intermediate Category
Al-Shahrastani states the position of affirmers and gives the substance/accident example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani introduces the concept of states as real attributes distinct from substances and accidents.

Core Idea
States are intelligible attributes that are neither substance nor accident.

This lesson begins with al-Shahrastani's foundational claim: states are not an intermediate between existence and non-existence in the sense of a separate entity, but they are real for us. He uses the example of knowing a substance by its substantiality without knowing its location, to show that distinct knowledge indicates distinct real states.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Notice how the author clarifies that states are not an intermediate but are established real attributes.
The author emphasizes that states are not independent entities.
2
Analyze the substance/accident example
Observe how the same substance can be known by different attributes, suggesting real distinctions.
This example shows that knowledge of substantiality and location are distinct.
3
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'hal', 'jawhar', 'arad', and 'sifa ma'qula' and their roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المثبتون: نحن لم نثبت واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، فإن الحال ثابتة عندنا، ولولا ذلك لما تكلمنا فيها بالنفي والإثبات، ولم نقل على الإطلاق: إنه شيء ثابت على حياله موجود، فإن الموجود المحدث إما جوهر وإما عرض، وهو ليس أحدهما، بل هو صفة معقولة لهما، فإن الجوهر قد يعلم بجوهريته ولا يعلم بحيزه، وكونه قابلا للعرض والعرض يعلم بعرضيته ولا يخطر بالبال كونه لونا أو كونا، ثم يعرف كونه لونا بعد ذلك، ولا يعرف كونه سوادا أو بياضا إلا أن يعرف، والمعلومان إذا تمايزا في الشيء الواحد رجع التمايز إلى الحال، وقد يعلم ضرورة من وجه، ويعلم نظرا من وجه، كمن يعلم كون المتحرك متحركا ضرورة، ثم يعلم بالنظر بعد ذلك كونه متحركا بحركة، ولو كان المعنيان واحدا لما علم أحدهما بالضرورة والثاني بالنظر، ولما سبق أحدهما إلى العقل وتأخر الآخر، ومن أنكر هذا فقد جحد الضرورة. ونفاة الأعراض أنكروا أن الحركة عرض زائد على المتحرك، وما أنكروا كونه متحركا، وأنتم معاشر النفاة وافقتمونا على أن الحركة علة لكون الجوهر متحركا، وكذلك القدرة والعلم وجميع الأعراض والمعاني والعلة توجب المعلول لا محالة، فلا يخلو إما أن توجب ذاتها، وإما شيئا آخر وراء ذاتها، ويستحيل أن يقال: توجب ذاتها، فإن الشيء الواحد من وجه واحد يستحيل أن يكون موجبا وموجبا لنفسه، وإن أوجب أمرا آخر، فذلك الأمر إما ذات على حيالها أو صفة لذات، ويستحيل أن تكون ذاتا على حيالها فإنه يؤدي إلى أن تكون العلة بإيجابها موجدة للذوات، وتلك الذوات أيضا علل وهو محال، فإنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل فيتعين أنه صفة لذات، وذلك هو الحال التي أثبتناها، فالقسمة العقلية ألجأتنا إلى إثباتها، والضرورة حملتنا على أن لا نسميها موجودة على حيالها ومعلومة على حيالها، وقد يعلم الشيء مع غيره ولا يعلم على حياله؛ كالتأليف بين الجوهرين والمماسة، والقرب والبعد، فإن الجوهر الواحد لا يعلم فيه تأليف ولا مماسة ما لم ينضم إليه جوهر آخر، وهذا في الصفات التي هي ذوات وأعراض متصور، فكيف في الصفات التي ليست بذوات، بل هي أحكام الذوات. وأما قولكم: إنه راجع إلى وجوه واعتبارات عقلية. فنقول: هذه الوجوه والاعتبارات ليست مطلقة مرسلة، بل هي مختصة بذوات، فالوجوه العقلية لذات واحدة هي بعينها الأحوال فإن تلك الوجوه ليست مجردة قائمة بالمتكلم بل هي حقائق معلومة معقولة، لا أنها موجودة على حيالها ولا معلومة بانفرادها بل هي صفات توصف بها الذوات، فما عبرتم عنه بالوجوه عبرنا عنه بالأحوال فإن المعلومين قد تمايزا وإن كانت الذات متحدة، وتمايز المعلومين يدل على تعدد الوجهين والحالين وذلك معلومان محققان تعلق.
The affirmers said: We have not posited an intermediate between negation and affirmation, for the state (al-hal) is established for us, and were it not for that we would not speak about it in terms of negation and affirmation, nor would we say absolutely: it is a thing established by itself and existent. For the originated existent is either substance or accident, and it is neither, but rather an intelligible attribute for them both. For the substance may be known by its substantiality without being known by its location, and its being receptive of accident; and the accident may be known by its accidentality without its occurring to the mind that it is a colour or a mode, then it is known as a colour after that, and it is not known as black or white unless it is known. And if two known things are distinguished in a single thing, the distinction refers to the state. And something may be known necessarily from one aspect, and known by reasoning from another aspect, like one who knows that the moving thing is moving necessarily, then knows by reasoning after that that it is moving by motion. And if the two meanings were one, one would not be known necessarily and the second by reasoning, nor would one precede the intellect and the other follow. And whoever denies this has denied the necessary. And the deniers of accidents denied that motion is an accident superadded to the moving thing, but they did not deny that it is moving. And you, O group of deniers, have agreed with us that motion is a cause of the substance's being moving, and likewise power and knowledge and all accidents and meanings. And the cause necessarily necessitates the effect. So it is inevitable: either it necessitates its own essence, or something else beyond its essence. And it is impossible to say it necessitates its own essence, for a single thing from one aspect cannot be both necessitator and necessitated of itself. And if it necessitates another thing, then that thing is either a self-subsistent essence or an attribute of an essence. And it is impossible for it to be a self-subsistent essence, for that would lead to the cause by its necessitation causing essences, and those essences also would be causes—and that is impossible, for it leads to infinite regress. So it is determined that it is an attribute of an essence, and that is the state which we have affirmed. So the rational division compelled us to affirm it, and necessity impelled us not to call it existent by itself nor known by itself. And something may be known with something else and not known by itself, like composition between two substances and contiguity, proximity and distance. For a single substance is not known to have composition or contiguity unless another substance is joined to it. And this is conceivable in attributes that are essences and accidents, so how about attributes that are not essences but rather judgments of essences? And as for your saying: it reduces to rational aspects and considerations. We say: these aspects and considerations are not absolute and unbound, but rather are specific to essences. For the rational aspects of a single essence are the very states. For those aspects are not abstract subsisting in the speaker, but are known intelligible realities, not existing by themselves nor known in isolation, but rather attributes by which the essences are described. So what you have expressed as aspects we have expressed as states. For the two known things have been distinguished even though the essence is one, and the distinction of the two known things indicates the multiplicity of the two aspects and the two states, and those are two known realized things which relate.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive defense of the reality of 'states' (ahwal) as a third ontological category between existence and non-existence, neither substance nor accident but attributes of both. The author, likely al-Shahrastani, begins by stating the position of the affirmers (al-muthbitun): they do not posit an intermediate between negation and affirmation in the sense of a separate entity, but the state (hal) is established for them as a real attribute. If it were not real, they would not speak about it with affirmation or negation. They clarify that the originated existent is either substance (jawhar) or accident ('arad), but the state is neither—it is an intelligible attribute for both substances and accidents. To prove this, the author gives examples: a substance can be known by its substantiality (jawhariyya) without knowing its location, and an accident can be known by its accidentality without knowing its specific type (e.g., colour). The fact that two distinct pieces of knowledge can be had of one thing shows that the distinction refers to a real state in the thing, not merely a mental consideration. The author then provides a stronger argument from motion: one knows necessarily that a moving thing is moving, but then by reasoning one knows that it is moving by motion (i.e., the motion is distinct from the thing itself). If the meaning of 'moving' and 'motion' were identical, the two pieces of knowledge would not differ in mode (necessity vs. reasoning). This proves the reality of motion as an accident and, by extension, the reality of states as attributes that are not independently existing. Next, the author addresses the objection from those who deny accidents (nafat al-a'rad). They agree that motion is a cause of the substance's being moving, and that a cause necessitates its effect. The cause either necessitates its own essence (impossible, as a thing cannot cause itself) or something else. That something else cannot be a self-subsistent essence (leads to infinite regress of causes causing essences), so it must be an attribute of an essence—precisely the state. The rational division (al-qisma al-'aqliyya) compels affirmation of states, and necessity prevents us from calling them 'existent by themselves' or 'known by themselves'. The author gives further examples: composition (ta'lif) and contiguity (mumassa) between two substances are not known in a single substance alone. This is evident for attributes that are themselves essences or accidents, so it is even more true for attributes that are not essences but judgments of essences. Finally, the author responds to the objection that states are merely rational aspects (wujuh) and considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya). He argues that these aspects are not arbitrary or free-floating; they are specific to essences. The rational aspects of a single essence are precisely the states—they are not abstract mental constructs but known intelligible realities (haqa'iq ma'luma ma'qula). They do not exist independently nor are they known in isolation; rather, they are attributes by which essences are described. Thus, what objectors call 'aspects' the author calls 'states', and the distinction of two known things in a single essence proves the multiplicity of aspects/states. The passage concludes by emphasizing that these states are real, known, and related to the essence.

Key Terms
حال
جوهر
عرض
صفة معقولة
تمايز المعلومين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think states are a third type of entity intermediate between existence and non-existence, like a separate substance.
Correction: The author clarifies that states are not an intermediate in that sense; they are attributes that do not have independent existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the distinction of two known things in a single thing indicate?
A) That the thing has two separate essences.
B) That the distinction refers to a real state.
C) That the known things are illusions.
D) That the thing is composed of two substances.

Key Takeaway
States are real intelligible attributes that are neither substances nor accidents, proven by the distinct knowledge we have of a single thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that we can know a substance's 'substantiality' independently of its location. In your own self-reflection, do you define yourself by your circumstances (location) or by your essence (your core as a servant of Allah)? What would change if you focused on your inner reality apart from your outer conditions?
?
The affirmers say that without states, we could not even discuss them. Consider your understanding of Allah's attributes: when you say He is 'Knowing' or 'Merciful', do you treat these as real states of His essence, or as mere labels? How does your view affect the sincerity of your worship and your reliance on His names?

Proof from Motion and Causality
Al-Shahrastani argues for states using the motion example and a causal argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of motion and the causal argument prove the reality of states.

Core Idea
The distinction between necessary and rational knowledge shows motion is distinct from the moving thing.

In this lesson, al-Shahrastani presents two powerful arguments. First, the motion example: we know necessarily that something is moving, but only by reasoning do we know it moves by motion. If the two meanings were identical, the modes of knowledge would not differ. Second, the causal argument: a cause must necessitate an effect; if it necessitates itself it's impossible, and if it necessitates another essence, that leads to infinite regress. Therefore, it necessitates an attribute—the state.

Guided Path
1
Study the motion example
Understand how necessary and rational knowledge differ, and why this implies two distinct meanings.
The example shows that 'moving' and 'motion' are not identical.
2
Work through the causal argument
Follow the division: a cause can only necessitate its own essence or something else. Both options are analyzed.
3
Identify the conclusion
The only coherent option is that the cause necessitates an attribute of an essence, which is the state.

Source Text

According to the causal argument, what does a cause ultimately necessitate?
A) Its own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know by necessity that you exist and that Allah exists. But have you ever reasoned through the proofs for His attributes, or do you rely on blind acceptance? Will you commit to studying one proof this week to move from necessary to rational certainty?
?
The causal argument shows that every cause necessitates an effect. In your life, what 'effects' are you producing? Are your actions causes for good or harm? Will you trace your daily habits back to their ultimate cause and repent if they lead away from Allah?
?
Infinite regress is impossible—everything traces back to a First Cause. When you feel anxiety about the future, are you forgetting that all causes are under Allah's control? Will you recite 'Hasbunallahu wa ni'mal wakeel' with full conviction today?

States as Real Attributes, Not Mere Aspects
Al-Shahrastani addresses the claim that states are rational aspects and provides the composition example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani defends states as real attributes, not merely mental considerations, using examples like composition and contiguity.

Core Idea
States are not arbitrary mental constructs but objective attributes grounded in essences.

The author concludes by addressing a key objection: that states are merely rational aspects (wujuh) and considerations (i'tibarat). He argues that these aspects are not free-floating but are specific to essences. The example of composition between two substances shows that a relation is known only when two things are considered together, yet it is a real attribute. Thus, what objectors call aspects are precisely the states al-Shahrastani defends.

Guided Path
1
Examine the composition example
Notice how composition is not known in a single substance alone, showing it is a real relation.
2
Analyze the response to rational aspects
See the author's claim that these aspects are not abstract but real attributes of essences.
3
Understand the final conclusion
The distinction of two known things in one essence proves the multiplicity of states.

قال المثبتون: نحن لم نثبت واسطة بين النفي والإثبات، فإن الحال ثابتة عندنا، ولولا ذلك لما تكلمنا فيها بالنفي والإثبات، ولم نقل على الإطلاق: إنه شيء ثابت على حياله موجود، فإن الموجود المحدث إما جوهر وإما عرض، وهو ليس أحدهما، بل هو صفة معقولة لهما، فإن الجوهر قد يعلم بجوهريته ولا يعلم بحيزه، وكونه قابلا للعرض والعرض يعلم بعرضيته ولا يخطر بالبال كونه لونا أو كونا، ثم يعرف كونه لونا بعد ذلك، ولا يعرف كونه سوادا أو بياضا إلا أن يعرف، والمعلومان إذا تمايزا في الشيء الواحد رجع التمايز إلى الحال، وقد يعلم ضرورة من وجه، ويعلم نظرا من وجه، كمن يعلم كون المتحرك متحركا ضرورة، ثم يعلم بالنظر بعد ذلك كونه متحركا بحركة، ولو كان المعنيان واحدا لما علم أحدهما بالضرورة والثاني بالنظر، ولما سبق أحدهما إلى العقل وتأخر الآخر، ومن أنكر هذا فقد جحد الضرورة. ونفاة الأعراض أنكروا أن الحركة عرض زائد على المتحرك، وما أنكروا كونه متحركا، وأنتم معاشر النفاة وافقتمونا على أن الحركة علة لكون الجوهر متحركا، وكذلك القدرة والعلم وجميع الأعراض والمعاني والعلة توجب المعلول لا محالة، فلا يخلو إما أن توجب ذاتها، وإما شيئا آخر وراء ذاتها، ويستحيل أن يقال: توجب ذاتها، فإن الشيء الواحد من وجه واحد يستحيل أن يكون موجبا وموجبا لنفسه، وإن أوجب أمرا آخر، فذلك الأمر إما ذات على حيالها أو صفة لذات، ويستحيل أن تكون ذاتا على حيالها فإنه يؤدي إلى أن تكون العلة بإيجابها موجدة للذوات، وتلك الذوات أيضا علل وهو محال، فإنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل فيتعين أنه صفة لذات، وذلك هو الحال التي أثبتناها، فالقسمة العقلية ألجأتنا إلى إثباتها، والضرورة حملتنا على أن لا نسميها موجودة على حيالها ومعلومة على حيالها، وقد يعلم الشيء مع غيره ولا يعلم على حياله؛ كالتأليف بين الجوهرين والمماسة، والقرب والبعد، فإن الجوهر الواحد لا يعلم فيه تأليف ولا مماسة ما لم ينضم إليه جوهر آخر، وهذا في الصفات التي هي ذوات وأعراض متصور، فكيف في الصفات التي ليست بذوات، بل هي أحكام الذوات. وأما قولكم: إنه راجع إلى وجوه واعتبارات عقلية. فنقول: هذه الوجوه والاعتبارات ليست مطلقة مرسلة، بل هي مختصة بذوات، فالوجوه العقلية لذات واحدة هي بعينها الأحوال فإن تلك الوجوه ليست مجردة قائمة بالمتكلم بل هي حقائق معلومة معقولة، لا أنها موجودة على حيالها ولا معلومة بانفرادها بل هي صفات توصف بها الذوات، فما عبرتم عنه بالوجوه عبرنا عنه بالأحوال فإن المعلومين قد تمايزا وإن كانت الذات متحدة، وتمايز المعلومين يدل على تعدد الوجهين والحالين وذلك معلومان محققان تعلق.
The affirmers said: We have not posited an intermediate between negation and affirmation, for the state (al-hal) is established for us, and were it not for that we would not speak about it in terms of negation and affirmation, nor would we say absolutely: it is a thing established by itself and existent. For the originated existent is either substance or accident, and it is neither, but rather an intelligible attribute for them both. For the substance may be known by its substantiality without being known by its location, and its being receptive of accident; and the accident may be known by its accidentality without its occurring to the mind that it is a colour or a mode, then it is known as a colour after that, and it is not known as black or white unless it is known. And if two known things are distinguished in a single thing, the distinction refers to the state. And something may be known necessarily from one aspect, and known by reasoning from another aspect, like one who knows that the moving thing is moving necessarily, then knows by reasoning after that that it is moving by motion. And if the two meanings were one, one would not be known necessarily and the second by reasoning, nor would one precede the intellect and the other follow. And whoever denies this has denied the necessary. And the deniers of accidents denied that motion is an accident superadded to the moving thing, but they did not deny that it is moving. And you, O group of deniers, have agreed with us that motion is a cause of the substance's being moving, and likewise power and knowledge and all accidents and meanings. And the cause necessarily necessitates the effect. So it is inevitable: either it necessitates its own essence, or something else beyond its essence. And it is impossible to say it necessitates its own essence, for a single thing from one aspect cannot be both necessitator and necessitated of itself. And if it necessitates another thing, then that thing is either a self-subsistent essence or an attribute of an essence. And it is impossible for it to be a self-subsistent essence, for that would lead to the cause by its necessitation causing essences, and those essences also would be causes—and that is impossible, for it leads to infinite regress. So it is determined that it is an attribute of an essence, and that is the state which we have affirmed. So the rational division compelled us to affirm it, and necessity impelled us not to call it existent by itself nor known by itself. And something may be known with something else and not known by itself, like composition between two substances and contiguity, proximity and distance. For a single substance is not known to have composition or contiguity unless another substance is joined to it. And this is conceivable in attributes that are essences and accidents, so how about attributes that are not essences but rather judgments of essences? And as for your saying: it reduces to rational aspects and considerations. We say: these aspects and considerations are not absolute and unbound, but rather are specific to essences. For the rational aspects of a single essence are the very states. For those aspects are not abstract subsisting in the speaker, but are known intelligible realities, not existing by themselves nor known in isolation, but rather attributes by which the essences are described. So what you have expressed as aspects we have expressed as states. For the two known things have been distinguished even though the essence is one, and the distinction of the two known things indicates the multiplicity of the two aspects and the two states, and those are two known realized things which relate.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive defense of the reality of 'states' (ahwal) as a third ontological category between existence and non-existence, neither substance nor accident but attributes of both. The author, likely al-Shahrastani, begins by stating the position of the affirmers (al-muthbitun): they do not posit an intermediate between negation and affirmation in the sense of a separate entity, but the state (hal) is established for them as a real attribute. If it were not real, they would not speak about it with affirmation or negation. They clarify that the originated existent is either substance (jawhar) or accident ('arad), but the state is neither—it is an intelligible attribute for both substances and accidents. To prove this, the author gives examples: a substance can be known by its substantiality (jawhariyya) without knowing its location, and an accident can be known by its accidentality without knowing its specific type (e.g., colour). The fact that two distinct pieces of knowledge can be had of one thing shows that the distinction refers to a real state in the thing, not merely a mental consideration. The author then provides a stronger argument from motion: one knows necessarily that a moving thing is moving, but then by reasoning one knows that it is moving by motion (i.e., the motion is distinct from the thing itself). If the meaning of 'moving' and 'motion' were identical, the two pieces of knowledge would not differ in mode (necessity vs. reasoning). This proves the reality of motion as an accident and, by extension, the reality of states as attributes that are not independently existing. Next, the author addresses the objection from those who deny accidents (nafat al-a'rad). They agree that motion is a cause of the substance's being moving, and that a cause necessitates its effect. The cause either necessitates its own essence (impossible, as a thing cannot cause itself) or something else. That something else cannot be a self-subsistent essence (leads to infinite regress of causes causing essences), so it must be an attribute of an essence—precisely the state. The rational division (al-qisma al-'aqliyya) compels affirmation of states, and necessity prevents us from calling them 'existent by themselves' or 'known by themselves'. The author gives further examples: composition (ta'lif) and contiguity (mumassa) between two substances are not known in a single substance alone. This is evident for attributes that are themselves essences or accidents, so it is even more true for attributes that are not essences but judgments of essences. Finally, the author responds to the objection that states are merely rational aspects (wujuh) and considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya). He argues that these aspects are not arbitrary or free-floating; they are specific to essences. The rational aspects of a single essence are precisely the states—they are not abstract mental constructs but known intelligible realities (haqa'iq ma'luma ma'qula). They do not exist independently nor are they known in isolation; rather, they are attributes by which essences are described. Thus, what objectors call 'aspects' the author calls 'states', and the distinction of two known things in a single essence proves the multiplicity of aspects/states. The passage concludes by emphasizing that these states are real, known, and related to the essence.

Key Terms
وجوه واعتبارات عقلية
تمايز المعلومين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that states are merely mental considerations or rational aspects with no real basis in the object.
Correction: The author argues that these aspects are not free-floating but are rooted in essences; they are real attributes, not mere constructs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the example of composition between two substances illustrate?
A) That composition is a substance.
B) That composition is not known in a single substance alone.
C) That composition is an accident.
D) That composition is a mental illusion.

Key Takeaway
States are real, not mere mental aspects; they are objective attributes that account for real distinctions in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that some of your own attributes (like being a student, a sibling, a believer) only make sense in relation to others. When was the last time you felt your identity was shaped not by what you are alone, but by a relationship? Does that make that attribute less real in your eyes? How does this lesson challenge you to see your own states before Allah as real, even though they are known only in relation to Him?
?
The passage argues that two known things can be distinct in a single essence. Reflect on a moment when you struggled to hold two truths about yourself that seemed to conflict (e.g., being both weak and strong in faith). How might the reality of states help you accept that both are real attributes of your soul, not just mental contradictions? What must you stop dismissing in yourself?
?
Al-Shahrastani insists that rational aspects are not abstract mental constructs but known intelligible realities. Think about a time when you reduced someone else's actions to mere 'interpretation' or 'perspective' to avoid the weight of their reality. How does this lesson call you to take the states of others—their pains, their virtues—as objective realities that demand a response from you, not just as your own mental considerations?

intermediate
8 min
The Negators' Second Objection Against States
The negators' second objection against the reality of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' argument that if existence were a single state, it would lead to absurd consequences like unifying different genera and making them interchangeable without changing the state itself.

Core Idea
A single state across different genera is like requiring matter to change forms without changing itself.

The second objection from the negators targets the affirmers' claim that states (aḥwāl) are real. They argue that if existence were a single state shared by all things, then distinct genera and species would have to be unified, forcing them to change without any change in the underlying state. This leads to contradictions like substance becoming accident and vice versa. They also question how such a universal undifferentiated existence could even be conceived—if it has a locus, it loses universality; if it has no locus, it is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage that presents the negators' argument. Notice the premise and the two consequences they draw.
The objection is structured as a conditional: if existence is a single state, then...
2
Identify the problems raised
The negators list two main problems: (1) a single existence permeating different genera and species would unify them, and (2) genera would change without any change in existence itself, which they equate to converting genera.
They illustrate with the analogy of forms changing in matter.
3
Examine the analogy with matter and forms
The negators compare the situation to the Aristotelian concept of matter (hayūlā) receiving different forms. They then critique that analogy, noting that matter is never without form, so the parallel is not exact.
They argue that some forms are necessary (like the three dimensions) while others change.
4
Reflect on the final challenge
The negators end with a critical question: how can an absolute, general, undifferentiated existence have a locus? If it inheres in substance, it loses its universality over accidents; if it is an accident, it cannot inhere in substance; if it has no locus, that is impossible.
This challenge puts pressure on the affirmers to explain the ontological status of the state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكر الثاني إذا كان الوجود حالا واحدا واجتمعت فيه أجناس وأنواع وأصناف فيلزم أن تتحد المتكثرات المختلفة فيه، ولزم تبدل الأجناس من غير تبدل وتغير في الوجود أصلا، وذلك كتِبدل الصورة بعضها ببعض في الهيولي عند الفلاسفة من غير تبدل في الهيولي، وذلك التمثيل أيضا غير سديد، فإن الهيولي لا تعري عن الصورة إلا أن من الصور ما هو لازم للهيولي كالأبعاد الثلاثة، ومنها ما يتبدل كالأشكال والمقادير المختلفة والأوضاع والكيفيات، وبالجملة وجود مجرد مطلق عام غير مختلف كيف يتصور وجوده وما محله؟ فإن كان الجوهر محلا له فقد بطل عمومه العرض، فإن عنيتم العرض فقد بطل عمومه الجوهر، وإن لم يكن ذا محل وهو من أمحل المحال.
وعن هذه المطالبة الحاقة يلوح الحق ولا نبوح به ؛ لأنا بعد في مدارج أقوال الفريقين، فيقول النافي : كيف يتصور وجود على الحقيقة التي لا تتبدل ولا تتغير، وإنما تتغير أنواعه بعضها إلى بعض فيصير الجوهر عرضا والعرض جوهرا، والوجود لا يختلف وذلك تجويز قلب الأجناس، فالأول سريان الوجود الواحد في أجناس وأنواع مختلفة، والثاني اتحاد أنواع وأجناس مختلفة في وجود واحد.
وقال المثبتون : إلزام الحال علينا نقضا غير متوجه، فإن العموم والخصوص في الحال كالجنسية والنوعية في الأجناس والأنواع، فإن الجنسية في الأجناس ليس جنساً حتى يستدعي كل جنس جنساً ويؤدي إلى التسلسل، وكذلك النوعية في الأنواع ليست نوعًا حتى يستدعي كل نوع نوعًا، فكذلك الحالية للأحوال لا حالا فيؤدي إلى التسلسل، وليس يلزم على من يقول: الوجود عام أن يقول للعام عام، وكذلك لو قال : العرضية جنس، فلا يلزمه أن يقول للجنس جنس، وكذلك لو فرق فارق بين حقيقة الجنس والنوع وفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بأخص وصف لم يلزمه أن يثبت اعتبارًا عقليًا في الجنس هو كالجنس، ووجها عقليا هو كالنوع فلا يلزم الحال علينا بوجه لا من حيث العموم والخصوص، ولا من حيث الاعتبار والوجه.
وأنتم معاشر النفاة لا يمكنكم أن تتكلموا بكلمة واحدة إلا وإن تدرجوا فيها عموماً وخصوصاً، ألستم تنفون الحال ولم تعينوا حالا بعينها هي صفة مخصوصة مشار إليها ؟ بل أطلقتم القول وعممتم النفي وأقمتم الدليل على جنس شمل أنواعًا أو على نوع عم أشخاصاً حتى استمر دليلكم، ومن نفى الحال لا يمكنه إثبات التماثل في المتماثلات، وإجراء حكم واحد فيها جميعا، فلو كانت الأشياء تتمايز بذواتها المعينة بطل التماثل وبطل الاختلاف، وذلك خروج عن قضايا العقول.
And the second denier: if existence is a single state and genera, species, and varieties are gathered in it, then it is necessary that the multiple different things become united in it, and it is necessary that the genera change without any change or alteration in existence at all, like the replacement of some forms by others in matter according to the philosophers without change in matter, and that analogy is also not sound, for matter is never free from form, but among forms are those that are necessary to matter like the three dimensions, and among them are those that change like different shapes, quantities, positions, and qualities. In sum, an absolute general undifferentiated existence: how is its existence conceived and what is its locus? For if substance is its locus, then its universality of accident is invalid; and if you mean accident, then its universality of substance is invalid; and if it has no locus, that is among the most impossible of impossibilities. And from this rightful demand the truth appears but we do not declare it because we are still within the schools of the two groups. So the negator says: how can existence be conceived in the reality that does not change or alter, but its types change into each other so that substance becomes accident and accident becomes substance, while existence does not differ? And that is permitting the conversion of genera. The first is the permeation of a single existence into different genera and species, and the second is the unification of different species and genera in a single existence. And the affirmers said: the imposition of the state upon us as a refutation is not relevant. For universality and particularity in the state are like genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. For genus-ness in genera is not a genus such that every genus would require a genus and lead to infinite regress. Likewise species-ness in species is not a species such that every species would require a species. So too the state-ness of states is not a state, leading to infinite regress. And it is not necessary for one who says 'existence is universal' to say that the universal has a universal. Likewise, if one says 'accidentality is a genus', it is not necessary for him to say that the genus has a genus. Likewise, if someone distinguishes between the reality of genus and species and separates one from the other by the most specific description, it is not necessary for him to posit an intellectual consideration in the genus that is like the genus, and an intellectual aspect that is like the species. So the state is not imposed upon us in any respect—neither in terms of universality and particularity, nor in terms of consideration and aspect. And you, O community of negators, cannot utter a single word without implicitly employing universality and particularity in it. Do you not deny the state without specifying a particular state that is a designated attribute? Rather you spoke generally, made the negation universal, and established the proof upon a genus that includes species or a species that includes individuals so that your proof continues. And whoever denies the state cannot establish similarity among similar things, nor apply a single judgment to all of them. For if things were distinguished by their specific essences, similarity would be invalid and difference would be invalid—and that is a departure from the dictates of intellects.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a dense theological and philosophical argument about the nature of existence and the concept of 'al-ḥāl' (the state). It begins with the second objection by the negators (al-nufāt) who deny the reality of states. Their objection is that if existence were a single state (ḥāl wāḥid) shared by all existents, then different genera, species, and varieties would have to be united in that single state, forcing a change of genera without any change in the existence itself. They compare this to the replacement of forms in matter (al-hayūlā) in Aristotelian philosophy, but then argue that analogy fails because matter always has some form, and some forms are necessary while others change. They then ask: how can an absolute, general, undifferentiated existence be conceived? If it has substance as a locus, then it cannot be an accident (since accidents inhere in substances), and if it is an accident, then it cannot inhere in substance. If it has no locus, that is impossible. The author then remarks that the truth appears from this rightful demand but he will not reveal it because they are still moving between the schools of the two groups. The negator then refines the objection: how can existence be real and unchanging while its types change into each other (substance becomes accident and vice versa)? That would permit converting genera. The two problems are: (1) a single existence permeating different genera and species, and (2) different species and genera united in a single existence. Then the affirmers (al-muthbitūn) respond. They argue that imposing the state upon them as a refutation is irrelevant. They make an analogy: universality and particularity in the state are like genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. Just as genus-ness in genera is not itself a genus (otherwise there would be infinite regress), and species-ness is not itself a species, so state-ness in states is not itself a state. Therefore, no infinite regress follows. Similarly, if someone says 'existence is universal', he is not required to posit a universal for that universal. If someone says 'accidentality is a genus', he does not need to posit a genus for that genus. If someone distinguishes genus from species by their most specific description, he is not forced to posit an intellectual concept in the genus that is like a genus, or an aspect in the species like a species. Thus, the state is not imposed on them in any way—neither by universality/particularity nor by intellectual consideration. The affirmers then turn the tables on the negators, arguing that even the negators cannot speak without using universality and particularity. When the negators deny the state, they do so without specifying a particular state—they generalize their negation and base their proof on a genus that includes species or a species that includes individuals. This implies that the negators themselves rely on the very concepts of generality and particularity that they deny when applied to states. Moreover, if one denies states altogether, one cannot account for similarity among similar things or apply a single judgment to them. If things were distinguished solely by their individual essences (dhawāt muʿayyana), then both similarity and difference would become impossible—a conclusion that contradicts reason. This passage thus defends the reality of states (aḥwāl) as necessary for rational discourse and for explaining similarity and difference in the world.

Key Terms
الحال (al-ḥāl)
الأجناس (al-ajnās)
الأنواع (al-anwāʿ)
الهيولي (al-hayūlā)
الوجود (al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the negators are denying the existence of objects themselves, rather than denying the reality of the state (ḥāl) that is supposed to unite them.
Correction: The negators are not denying that things exist; they are denying that existence is a single state shared by all things. They argue that such a state leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the negators use to illustrate their objection?
A) The analogy of substance and accident
B) The analogy of forms changing in matter
C) The analogy of necessary and possible existence
D) The analogy of universals and particulars

Key Takeaway
The negators argue that a single state for all existence leads to unification of distinct genera and change without alteration, and that such a state cannot have a coherent locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The negators' argument forces you to ask: if existence cannot be a single state common to all things, then how do you understand the relationship between God's existence and His attributes? Are you content to accept a mystery, or do you have a rational explanation that avoids such objections?
?
The passage argues that a concept without a coherent locus is impossible. In your own spiritual life, where does your faith 'inhere'? Is it merely an accident of your soul, or does it define your very substance? What would it mean if your faith had no locus at all?
?
The analogy of matter and forms is criticized as flawed because matter is never without form. In your own life, do you ever try to separate your 'essence' from your actions or states? Are you acting as if your character can change without affecting your core self?

The Affirmers' Rebuttal and Counter-Argument
The affirmers' rebuttal of the second objection and their counter-argument against the negators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the affirmers defend the reality of states by analogy with genus/species, avoiding infinite regress, and then turn the argument against the negators by showing that even they must use universals in their reasoning.

Core Idea
State-ness in states is not a state, just as genus-ness is not a genus — no infinite regress follows.

After the negators' objection, the affirmers respond by showing that the charge of infinite regress is unfounded. They draw an analogy between state-ness (al-ḥāliyya) and genus-ness (al-jinsiyya): just as genus-ness is not itself a genus, so state-ness is not itself a state. They also argue that if someone says 'existence is universal', they are not required to posit a universal for that universal. Then they go on the offensive, pointing out that the negators themselves rely on generality and particularity in their own reasoning—when they deny states, they do so without specifying a particular state, thus using a universal negation. Finally, they argue that without states, similarity and difference among things would be inexplicable.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmers' rebuttal
Start with the Arabic passage from 'وقال المثبتون' to the end of the lesson. Notice the structure: first they defend, then they counter-attack.
The rebuttal uses an analogy to show that infinite regress does not apply.
2
Understand the analogy
The affirmers compare state-ness (al-ḥāliyya) in states to genus-ness (al-jinsiyya) in genera. Just as genus-ness is not a genus, state-ness is not a state. This stops the infinite regress objection.
Similarly, species-ness is not a species.
3
See the additional examples
They give examples: saying 'existence is universal' does not require a universal for that universal; saying 'accidentality is a genus' does not require a genus for that genus.
These reinforce that the demand for a higher-order state is unwarranted.
4
Follow the counter-argument
The affirmers then point out that the negators themselves use universality and particularity in their own denial of states. For example, they deny 'the state' in general without specifying a particular state.
Thus, the negators implicitly rely on the concepts they reject.
5
Reflect on the final point
The affirmers argue that without states, one cannot explain similarity among similar things or apply a single judgment to them. Denying states leads to the collapse of rational distinctions.
This shows that states are necessary for rational discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنكر الثاني إذا كان الوجود حالا واحدا واجتمعت فيه أجناس وأنواع وأصناف فيلزم أن تتحد المتكثرات المختلفة فيه، ولزم تبدل الأجناس من غير تبدل وتغير في الوجود أصلا، وذلك كتِبدل الصورة بعضها ببعض في الهيولي عند الفلاسفة من غير تبدل في الهيولي، وذلك التمثيل أيضا غير سديد، فإن الهيولي لا تعري عن الصورة إلا أن من الصور ما هو لازم للهيولي كالأبعاد الثلاثة، ومنها ما يتبدل كالأشكال والمقادير المختلفة والأوضاع والكيفيات، وبالجملة وجود مجرد مطلق عام غير مختلف كيف يتصور وجوده وما محله؟ فإن كان الجوهر محلا له فقد بطل عمومه العرض، فإن عنيتم العرض فقد بطل عمومه الجوهر، وإن لم يكن ذا محل وهو من أمحل المحال.
وعن هذه المطالبة الحاقة يلوح الحق ولا نبوح به ؛ لأنا بعد في مدارج أقوال الفريقين، فيقول النافي : كيف يتصور وجود على الحقيقة التي لا تتبدل ولا تتغير، وإنما تتغير أنواعه بعضها إلى بعض فيصير الجوهر عرضا والعرض جوهرا، والوجود لا يختلف وذلك تجويز قلب الأجناس، فالأول سريان الوجود الواحد في أجناس وأنواع مختلفة، والثاني اتحاد أنواع وأجناس مختلفة في وجود واحد.
وقال المثبتون : إلزام الحال علينا نقضا غير متوجه، فإن العموم والخصوص في الحال كالجنسية والنوعية في الأجناس والأنواع، فإن الجنسية في الأجناس ليس جنساً حتى يستدعي كل جنس جنساً ويؤدي إلى التسلسل، وكذلك النوعية في الأنواع ليست نوعًا حتى يستدعي كل نوع نوعًا، فكذلك الحالية للأحوال لا حالا فيؤدي إلى التسلسل، وليس يلزم على من يقول: الوجود عام أن يقول للعام عام، وكذلك لو قال : العرضية جنس، فلا يلزمه أن يقول للجنس جنس، وكذلك لو فرق فارق بين حقيقة الجنس والنوع وفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بأخص وصف لم يلزمه أن يثبت اعتبارًا عقليًا في الجنس هو كالجنس، ووجها عقليا هو كالنوع فلا يلزم الحال علينا بوجه لا من حيث العموم والخصوص، ولا من حيث الاعتبار والوجه.
وأنتم معاشر النفاة لا يمكنكم أن تتكلموا بكلمة واحدة إلا وإن تدرجوا فيها عموماً وخصوصاً، ألستم تنفون الحال ولم تعينوا حالا بعينها هي صفة مخصوصة مشار إليها ؟ بل أطلقتم القول وعممتم النفي وأقمتم الدليل على جنس شمل أنواعًا أو على نوع عم أشخاصاً حتى استمر دليلكم، ومن نفى الحال لا يمكنه إثبات التماثل في المتماثلات، وإجراء حكم واحد فيها جميعا، فلو كانت الأشياء تتمايز بذواتها المعينة بطل التماثل وبطل الاختلاف، وذلك خروج عن قضايا العقول.
And the second denier: if existence is a single state and genera, species, and varieties are gathered in it, then it is necessary that the multiple different things become united in it, and it is necessary that the genera change without any change or alteration in existence at all, like the replacement of some forms by others in matter according to the philosophers without change in matter, and that analogy is also not sound, for matter is never free from form, but among forms are those that are necessary to matter like the three dimensions, and among them are those that change like different shapes, quantities, positions, and qualities. In sum, an absolute general undifferentiated existence: how is its existence conceived and what is its locus? For if substance is its locus, then its universality of accident is invalid; and if you mean accident, then its universality of substance is invalid; and if it has no locus, that is among the most impossible of impossibilities. And from this rightful demand the truth appears but we do not declare it because we are still within the schools of the two groups. So the negator says: how can existence be conceived in the reality that does not change or alter, but its types change into each other so that substance becomes accident and accident becomes substance, while existence does not differ? And that is permitting the conversion of genera. The first is the permeation of a single existence into different genera and species, and the second is the unification of different species and genera in a single existence. And the affirmers said: the imposition of the state upon us as a refutation is not relevant. For universality and particularity in the state are like genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. For genus-ness in genera is not a genus such that every genus would require a genus and lead to infinite regress. Likewise species-ness in species is not a species such that every species would require a species. So too the state-ness of states is not a state, leading to infinite regress. And it is not necessary for one who says 'existence is universal' to say that the universal has a universal. Likewise, if one says 'accidentality is a genus', it is not necessary for him to say that the genus has a genus. Likewise, if someone distinguishes between the reality of genus and species and separates one from the other by the most specific description, it is not necessary for him to posit an intellectual consideration in the genus that is like the genus, and an intellectual aspect that is like the species. So the state is not imposed upon us in any respect—neither in terms of universality and particularity, nor in terms of consideration and aspect. And you, O community of negators, cannot utter a single word without implicitly employing universality and particularity in it. Do you not deny the state without specifying a particular state that is a designated attribute? Rather you spoke generally, made the negation universal, and established the proof upon a genus that includes species or a species that includes individuals so that your proof continues. And whoever denies the state cannot establish similarity among similar things, nor apply a single judgment to all of them. For if things were distinguished by their specific essences, similarity would be invalid and difference would be invalid—and that is a departure from the dictates of intellects.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a dense theological and philosophical argument about the nature of existence and the concept of 'al-ḥāl' (the state). It begins with the second objection by the negators (al-nufāt) who deny the reality of states. Their objection is that if existence were a single state (ḥāl wāḥid) shared by all existents, then different genera, species, and varieties would have to be united in that single state, forcing a change of genera without any change in the existence itself. They compare this to the replacement of forms in matter (al-hayūlā) in Aristotelian philosophy, but then argue that analogy fails because matter always has some form, and some forms are necessary while others change. They then ask: how can an absolute, general, undifferentiated existence be conceived? If it has substance as a locus, then it cannot be an accident (since accidents inhere in substances), and if it is an accident, then it cannot inhere in substance. If it has no locus, that is impossible. The author then remarks that the truth appears from this rightful demand but he will not reveal it because they are still moving between the schools of the two groups. The negator then refines the objection: how can existence be real and unchanging while its types change into each other (substance becomes accident and vice versa)? That would permit converting genera. The two problems are: (1) a single existence permeating different genera and species, and (2) different species and genera united in a single existence. Then the affirmers (al-muthbitūn) respond. They argue that imposing the state upon them as a refutation is irrelevant. They make an analogy: universality and particularity in the state are like genus-ness and species-ness in genera and species. Just as genus-ness in genera is not itself a genus (otherwise there would be infinite regress), and species-ness is not itself a species, so state-ness in states is not itself a state. Therefore, no infinite regress follows. Similarly, if someone says 'existence is universal', he is not required to posit a universal for that universal. If someone says 'accidentality is a genus', he does not need to posit a genus for that genus. If someone distinguishes genus from species by their most specific description, he is not forced to posit an intellectual concept in the genus that is like a genus, or an aspect in the species like a species. Thus, the state is not imposed on them in any way—neither by universality/particularity nor by intellectual consideration. The affirmers then turn the tables on the negators, arguing that even the negators cannot speak without using universality and particularity. When the negators deny the state, they do so without specifying a particular state—they generalize their negation and base their proof on a genus that includes species or a species that includes individuals. This implies that the negators themselves rely on the very concepts of generality and particularity that they deny when applied to states. Moreover, if one denies states altogether, one cannot account for similarity among similar things or apply a single judgment to them. If things were distinguished solely by their individual essences (dhawāt muʿayyana), then both similarity and difference would become impossible—a conclusion that contradicts reason. This passage thus defends the reality of states (aḥwāl) as necessary for rational discourse and for explaining similarity and difference in the world.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the affirmers are positing an infinite regress of states by saying 'state-ness is a state'.
Correction: The affirmers explicitly deny this: state-ness is not a state, just as genus-ness is not a genus. They use this analogy to block the regress objection.

Key Takeaway

First Error of the Deniers: Denying Universals
Author identifies two errors and elaborates the first: reducing universals to mere words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that universals are not mere words but real concepts perceived by the intellect, even by animals.

Core Idea
Universals are not just words; they are real concepts that even animals use.

In this passage, the author responds to the deniers who claim that universals and particulars are reducible to mere words. He identifies two errors in their position and elaborates the first: reducing generality and particularity to bare language. Using the example of animals, he shows that universal concepts are innate and independent of language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical style: the author starts by quoting the deniers and then refutes them.
2
Identify the two errors mentioned
At the beginning, the author states that the deniers erred in two ways. Find both errors. The lesson focuses on the first error.
The first error is reducing universals to mere words; the second error is judging that existents differ only by essences and existence.
3
Examine the animal example
The author uses the example of beasts eating grass to show that even without language, animals recognize edibility as a universal. This refutes the claim that universals are only words.
Pay attention to the author's reasoning: if the second grass were not conceived as edible (like the first), the animal would not eat it.
4
Reflect on the intellect's perception
The author argues that the intellect perceives universals like 'humanity' and 'accidentality' necessarily. This proves universals are real concepts in the mind, independent of expressions.
The author concludes that denying universals removes one from humanity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد) وأفحمناكم بنفي الواحد في الاثنين والدليل متعارض، والدست قايم فمن الحاكم؟ قال الحاكم المشرف على نهاية إقدام الفريقين أنتم معاشر النفاة أخطأتم من وجهين: من حيث رددتم العموم والخصوص إلى الألفاظ المجردة، وحكمتم بأن الموجودات المختلفة تختلف بذواتها ووجودها فكلامكم هذا ينقض بعضه بعضا، ويدفع آخره أوله، وهذا إنكار لأخص أوصاف العقل، أليس هذا الرد حكما عاما على كل عام وخاص بأنه راجع إلى اللفظ المجرد؟ أليست الألفاظ لو رفعت من البين لم ترتفع القضايا العقلية حتى البهائم التي لا نطق لها ولا عقل لم تعدم هذه الهداية، فإنها تعلم بالفطرة ما ينفعها من العشب فتأكل، ثم إذا رأت عشبا آخر يماثل ذلك الأول ما اعتراها ريب في أنه مأكول كالأول، فلولا أنها تخيلت من الثاني عين حكم الأول وهو كونه مأكولا، وإلا لما أكلت وتعرف جنسها به وتعرف ضدها فتهرب منه ولقد صدق المثبتون عليكم أنكم حسمتم على أنفسكم باب الحد وشموله للمحدودات، وباب النظر وتضمنه للعلم. وأنا أقول: لا بل حسمتم على العقول باب الإدراك، وعلى الألسن باب الكلام، فإن العقل يدرك الإنسانية كلية عامة لجميع نوع الإنسان مميزة عن الشخص المعين المشار إليه، وكذلك العرضية كلية عامة لجميع أنواع الأعراض من غير أن يخطر بباله اللونية والسوادية وهذا السواد بعينه، وهذا مدرك بضرورة العقل، وهو مفهوم العبارة متصور في العقل لا نفس العبارة؛ إذ العبارة تدل على معنى في الذهن محقق هو مدلول العبارة والمعبر عنه لو تبدلت العبارة عربية وعجمية وهندية ورومية لم يتبدل المعنى المدلول ثم ما من كلام تام إلا ويختص معنى عاما فوق الأعيان المشار إليها بها، وتلك المعاني العامة من أخص أوصاف النفوس الإنسانية، فمن أنكرها خرج عن حدود الإنسانية ودخل في حريم البهيمة؛ بل هم أضل سبيلا.
And we have refuted you by denying the one in the two, and the evidence conflicts, and the constitution stands, so who is the judge? The governor overseeing the ultimate limit of the two groups said: You, O deniers, erred in two respects: in that you reduced generality and particularity to mere words, and you judged that different existents differ by their essences and existence, so your speech contradicts itself and its end pushes its beginning, and this is a denial of the most specific attributes of the intellect. Is not this refutation a general judgment upon every general and particular that it refers back to the bare word? Are not the intellectual propositions, even if words were removed, still present? Even beasts that have no speech nor reason are not deprived of this guidance, for they know by instinct what benefits them from grass and eat, then when they see another grass similar to the first, no doubt occurs to them that it is edible like the first, for if they did not conceive in the second the very judgment of the first—namely its edibility—then they would not eat. And they know their species by it and know their opposite and flee from it. And the affirmers have truly said of you that you have closed upon yourselves the door of definition and its covering of the defined, and the door of inference and its inclusion of knowledge. And I say: No, rather you have closed upon intellects the door of perception and upon tongues the door of speech. For the intellect perceives humanity as a universal general to all of the human species, distinguishing it from the particular individual indicated, and likewise accidentally as a universal general to all types of accidents, without the mind conceiving color-ness, blackness, or this specific blackness; and this is perceived by necessity of the intellect. It is the concept of the expression conceived in the intellect, not the expression itself; for the expression indicates a meaning in the mind that is actual, which is the referent of the expression and the thing expressed; if the expression changed—Arabic, non-Arabic, Indian, Roman—the indicated meaning would not change. Moreover, no complete utterance is without a general meaning above the particular individuals indicated by it, and those general meanings are among the most specific attributes of human souls. Whoever denies them departs from the limits of humanity and enters the sanctuary of beasts; rather, they are more astray in path.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to an objection from the deniers (النفاة) who claim that universals and particulars are reducible to mere words. The author begins by quoting a rhetorical question from the deniers, then responds by identifying two errors. The lesson focuses on the first error. The author accuses the deniers of reducing generality and particularity to bare language and of judging that different existents differ only by their individual essences and existence. He points out the self-contradiction in their statement—its end refutes its beginning. He then argues that this denial is itself an attack on the most specific attributes of the intellect. He asks: Is not their own refutation a general judgment (حكم عام) on all general and particular matters? He then uses the example of animals: even beasts without speech or reason possess an innate guidance (هداية) that enables them to recognize beneficial grass and avoid harm based on similarity, implying they grasp a universal concept (e.g., 'edible') without language. If they did not conceive the same judgment in the second instance, they would not eat. Thus, universal concepts are innate and not dependent on words. The affirmers (المثبتون) have rightly said that the deniers have closed upon themselves the door of definition (باب الحد) and inference (باب النظر). The author then adds that, in fact, the deniers have closed the door of perception upon intellects and the door of speech upon tongues. He explains: The intellect perceives 'humanity' (الإنسانية) as a universal general to all human individuals, distinct from any particular person. Similarly, it perceives 'accidentality' (العرضية) as a universal general to all types of accidents, without needing to conceive specific colors or forms. This is a necessary perception of the intellect. The concept of the expression (مفهوم العبارة) exists in the mind, not the expression itself; changing the language does not change the meaning. Every complete utterance has a general meaning above the particular individuals (الأعيان) it indicates. These general meanings are among the most specific attributes of human souls. Denying them removes one from humanity and reduces one to the level of beasts—indeed, they are more astray.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That universals are only linguistic conventions and do not exist in the mind independently of words.
Correction: The author shows that even animals without language use universal judgments, and the intellect necessarily perceives universals like humanity and accidentally. Therefore, universals are real mental concepts.

The Second Error: Distinguishing Types by Individual Essences
Author presents the second error and addresses an objection about distinguishing substances and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reducing the distinction between types to individual essences is an error and how the correct distinction is based on the most specific description of the type.

Core Idea
Types are distinguished by their specific attributes, not by properties of individual instances.

The second error of the deniers is to reduce the distinction between types (such as substance and accident) to the individual essences themselves. The author declares this a serious mistake and explains that a thing is known by its most specific description. He then responds to an objection about how substance is distinguished from accident, clarifying that the distinction is made by absolute individuation in a generic sense, not by particular individuation of individuals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: error, principle, objection, answer.
2
Identify the error and the principle
The error is reducing type distinction to individual essences. The principle is that a thing is distinguished by its most specific description. Understand why this is a 'heinous statement'.
3
Examine the objection and the author's answer
The objection claims that the most specific description of the type and of the individual differ. The author answers by distinguishing between absolute and particular individuation, using substance and accident as examples.
Focus on how substance is distinguished from accident by absolute individuation, not by particular individuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخطأ الثاني؛ وهو رد التمييز بين الأنواع إلى الذوات المعينة، وذلك من أشنع المقالات، فإن الشيء إنما يتميز عن غيره بأخص وصفه. قيل له: أخص وصف نوع الشيء غير، وأخص وصف الذات المشار إليها غير، فإن الجوهر يتميز عن العرض بالتحيز مطلقا إطلاقاً نوعيا لا معينا تعيينا شخصيا، والجوهر المعين إنما يمتاز عن جوهر معين بتحيز مخصوص لا بالتحيز المطلق، وقط لا يمتاز جوهر معين عن عرض معين بتحيز مخصوص؛ إذ الجنس لا يميز الوصف عن الموصوف والعرض عن الجوهر، والعقل إنما يميز بمطلق التحيز، فعرف أن الذوات إنما تتمايز بعضها عن بعض تمايزا جنسيا نوعيا لا بأعم صفاتها
And as for the second error, it is reducing the distinction between types to individual essences. That is among the most heinous statements. For a thing is distinguished from others only by its most specific description. It was said to him: 'The most specific description of the type of a thing is different, and the most specific description of the individual essence is different.' For substance is distinguished from accident by absolute individuation, in a general type sense, not in a specific individual determination. And the specific substance is distinguished from a specific substance by a particular individuation, not by absolute individuation. And never is a specific substance distinguished from a specific accident by a particular individuation; for the genus does not distinguish the description from the described nor the accident from the substance. And the intellect distinguishes only by absolute individuation. Thus know that essences are distinguished from each other by generic and specific distinction, not by their most general attributes.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the second error of the deniers: reducing the distinction between types (الأنواع) to individual essences (الذوات المعينة). The author declares this to be among the most heinous statements. He states that a thing is distinguished from others only by its most specific description (أخص وصفه). Then an objection is raised: 'The most specific description of the type of a thing is different from the most specific description of the individual essence itself.' In other words, the description that distinguishes a type (e.g., 'humanity') is not the same as that which distinguishes a particular individual (e.g., 'Zayd'). The author answers by explaining the distinction between substance (الجوهر) and accident (العرض). Substance is distinguished from accident by absolute individuation (التحيز مطلقا) in a generic sense, not by a particular individuation (تحيز مخصوص) that would apply to an individual. A specific substance is distinguished from another specific substance by a particular individuation (e.g., location), not by absolute individuation. And a specific substance is not distinguished from a specific accident by a particular individuation because the genus (الجنس) does not provide the distinction between the description and the described or between accident and substance. The intellect distinguishes only by absolute individuation. Therefore, essences are distinguished from each other by generic and specific distinction (تميزا جنسيا نوعيا), not by their most general attributes (أعم صفاتها).

Key Terms

Correction: The author clarifies that the most specific description is of the type, not the individual. For types like substance, absolute individuation (generic) applies, not particular individuation.

Checkpoint
