Refutation of Denial of Universal Concepts
The author refutes those who deny that the mind grasps universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity of universal concepts and why denying them undermines human intellect and speech.

Core Idea
Universals are real mental concepts, not just words—denying them strips one of humanity.

This passage from Nihayat al-Aqdam defends the reality of universal concepts against those who claim they are mere words. The author argues that the mind necessarily perceives universals like humanity and accidentality, independent of language. He warns that denying this leads to a loss of human distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the accusatory tone at the beginning.
2
Identify the main claim
Identify the author's central argument: that the mind grasps universals as a necessary perception, and that denying this closes the door of reason and speech.
The claim is contrasted with the opponent's error.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples of 'humanity' and 'accidentality' as universals. How do they show that the mind abstracts from particular instances?
The author emphasizes that these are perceived without reference to specific colors or individuals.
4
Understand the expression-meaning distinction
Focus on the distinction between the expression (lafẓ) and its concept (mafhūm). The author argues that meaning is independent of language, as it remains unchanged across different languages.
This refutes the nominalist position that universals are just words.
5
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the author's conclusion that denying universals removes one from humanity. Why is this such a strong condemnation?
It shows the importance of universals for human cognition and communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنا أقول : لا بل حسمتم على العقول باب الإدراك، وعلى الألسن باب الكلام، فإن العقل يدرك الإنسانية كلية عامة لجميع نوع الإنسان مميزة عن الشخص المعين المشار إليه، وكذلك العرضية كلية عامة لجميع أنواع الأعراض من غير أن يخطر بباله اللونية ،والسوادية وهذا السواد ،بعينه ،وهذا مدرك بضرورة العقل، وهو مفهوم العبارة متصور في العقل لا نفس العبارة ؛ إذ العبارة تدل على معنى في الذهن محقق هو مدلول العبارة والمعبر عنه لو تبدلت العبارة عربية وعجمية وهندية ورومية لم يتبدل المعنى المدلول ثم ما من كلام تام إلا ويختص معنى عاما فوق الأعيان المشار إليها بهذا، وتلك المعاني العامة من أخص أوصاف النفوس الإنسانية ، فمن أنكرها خرج عن حدود الإنسانية ودخل في حريم البهيمة ؛ بل هم أضل سبيلا.
And I say: No, but you have closed upon the minds the door of perception, and upon the tongues the door of speech. For the mind perceives humanity as a universal (kulliyya) common to all individuals of the human species, distinguished from the specific designated person; and likewise accidentality ('araḍiyya) as a universal common to all types of accidents without its mind being struck by color-ness (lawniyya) or blackness (sawādiyya) or this particular black. And this is perceived by necessity of the mind. And it is the concept of the expression (mafhūm al-'ibāra) conceived in the mind, not the expression itself; for the expression indicates a meaning (ma'nā) in the mind which is realized, which is the denotation of the expression and the expressed meaning, even if the expression changes—Arabic, Persian, Indian, or Roman—the indicated meaning does not change. Moreover, every complete utterance necessarily has a general meaning (ma'nā 'āmm) above the specific entities indicated by 'this'. And these general meanings are among the most specific attributes of human souls. So whoever denies them has left the bounds of humanity and entered the domain of beasts; rather, they are more astray.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage refutes the error of those who deny that the mind grasps universal concepts. The author begins by asserting that the objector's position actually closes the door of perception and speech. He then gives an example: the mind perceives the universal 'humanity' common to all individuals, and also the universal 'accidentality' common to all accidents, without needing to think of specific instances like 'color-ness' or 'blackness' or this particular black. This is a necessary perception of the mind. He distinguishes between the expression (lafẓ) and its concept (mafhūm): the expression points to a meaning in the mind, and that meaning is independent of the language used. Thus, the meaning is the true object of understanding. Finally, he argues that every complete utterance necessarily has a general meaning that goes beyond the specific entities. These general meanings are the most specific attributes of human souls; denying them is like leaving humanity for bestiality, and even more astray. The argument guards against the error of nominalism or the denial of universals.

Distinguishing Types by Most Specific Universal Properties
The author refutes a classification error and explains the correct method using substance/accident example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why types cannot be distinguished by individual essences but only by their most specific universal attributes.

Core Idea
A thing is known by its most specific universal attribute, not by its individual essence.

This passage tackles a fundamental error in classification: reducing types to individual essences. The author argues that true distinction comes from the most specific universal property of a thing. Using the example of substance and accident, he shows how generic and specific properties work differently. This lesson clarifies the correct method of distinguishing types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error
Read the first sentence to see the error being refuted: reducing distinction between types to individual essences. This is denounced as among the most detestable statements.
Notice the strong language used.
2
Grasp the correct principle
The author states the correct principle: a thing is only distinguished from another by its most specific attribute. Understand that this attribute must be universal, not individual.
3
Examine the example
Follow the objection and response using substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ). Substance is distinguished from accident by absolute occupancy, not by any particular occupancy. This shows the generic vs specific distinction.
Contrast with how a specific substance is distinguished from another specific substance by a particular occupancy.
4
Apply the conclusion
Understand that essences are distinguished generically and specifically by their most specific universal attributes, not by most general attributes like existence. This avoids the error.
The condition 'on condition that they be universal' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخطأ الثاني؛ وهو رد التمييز بين الأنواع إلى الذوات المعينة، وذلك من أشنع المقالات، فإن الشيء إنما يتميز عن غيره بأخص وصفه. قيل له: أخص وصف نوع الشيء غير، وأخص وصف الذات المشار إليها غير، فإن الجوهر يتميز عن العرض بالتحيز مطلقا إطلاقا نوعيا لا معينا تعيينا شخصيا، والجوهر المعين إنما يمتاز عن جوهر معين بتحيز مخصوص لا بالتحيز المطلق، وقط لا يمتاز جوهر معين عن عرض معين بتحيز مخصوص؛ إذ الجنس لا يميز الوصف عن الموصوف والعرض عن الجوهر، والعقل إنما يميز بمطلق التحيز، فعرف أن الذوات إنما تتمايز بعضها عن بعض تمايزا جنسيا نوعيا لا بأعم صفاتها كالوجود، بل بأخص أوصافها؛ بشرط أن تكون كلية عامة.
As for the second error—which is reducing the distinction between types to specific individual essences—that is among the most detestable statements. For a thing is only distinguished from another by its most specific attribute. It was said to him: The most specific attribute of the type of a thing is other than the most specific attribute of the designated essence. Indeed, substance is distinguished from accident by absolute occupancy (taḥayyuz muṭlaq) with a generic absoluteness, not a specific personal determination. And the specific substance is only distinguished from another specific substance by a particular occupancy, not by absolute occupancy. And a specific substance is never distinguished from a specific accident by a particular occupancy; for the genus does not distinguish the attribute from the described, nor the accident from the substance. The mind only distinguishes by absolute occupancy. So it is known that essences are distinguished from each other with a generic and specific distinction, not by their most general attributes like existence, but by their most specific attributes, on condition that they be universal.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage refutes the second error: that types are distinguished by individual essences. The author states that a thing is distinguished by its most specific attribute. An objection is raised: the most specific attribute of the type is different from that of the individual essence. The answer explains using the example of substance and accident. Substance is distinguished from accident by absolute occupancy (a generic distinction), while a specific substance is distinguished from another specific substance by a particular occupancy. But a specific substance is never distinguished from a specific accident by a particular occupancy, because that would mix genus and species. The mind distinguishes by absolute occupancy. Thus, essences are distinguished generically and specifically, not by most general attributes like existence, but by their most specific attributes, provided they are universal. This guards against the error of thinking that individual essences are the basis of classification.

تحيز (taḥayyuz)
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
نوع (nawʿ)
ذات (dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Types are distinguished by their individual essences (specific individual properties).
Correction: Types are distinguished by their most specific universal properties; individual essences only distinguish individuals within a type.

Checkpoint

A) By a particular occupancy
B) By absolute occupancy
C) By existence
D) By its individual essence

Key Takeaway
Types are distinguished by their most specific universal attributes, not by individual essences or most general attributes like existence.

The Logical Contradiction in Shared Specific Attributes
Refutes the first error of those who affirm states (ahwal) – shared specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming a specific attribute that is both exclusive to a thing and shared with others leads to a logical contradiction according to the author.

Core Idea
A specific attribute cannot be both particular and universal without contradiction.

This passage presents a core argument against the theory of states (ahwal) in kalam. The author demonstrates that if an attribute is truly specific to a particular thing, it cannot also be shared by another without losing its specificity. This exposes a fundamental logical flaw in the proponents' position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the compressed style and the logical progression.
2
Identify the proponents' claim
The author says proponents affirm both exclusive attributes and shared attributes for the same thing. Identify this as the target of refutation.
3
Follow the reduction chain
Trace the author's step-by-step reduction: existence → accidentally → colorness → blackness → particular blackness. Each is the same as the previous when specified.
This shows attributes are not independent realities.
4
Understand the contradiction
The author argues that if the same attribute existed in two loci, it would be both specific (particular to each) and universal (shared), which is logically impossible.
This is the core of the refutation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself why this argument undermines the very notion of states (ahwal) as real attributes distinct from the essence.
The passage shows states lead to incoherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجه خطأ المثبتين للحال فهو أنهم أثبتوا لموجود معين مشار إليه صفات مختصة به وصفات يشاركه فيها غيره من الموجودات، وهو من أمحل المحال، فإن المختص بالشيء المعين والذي يشاركه فيه غيره واحد بالنسبة إلى ذلك المعين فوجود عرض معين وعرضيته ولونيته وسواديته عبارات عن ذلك المعين المشار إليه، فإن الوجود إذا تخصص بالعرضية فهو بعينه عرض، والعرضية إذا تخصصت باللونية فهي بعينها لون وكذلك اللونية بالسوادية والسوادية بهذا السواد المشار إليه، فليس من المعقول أن توجد صفة لشيء واحد معين وهي بعينها توجد لشيء آخر، فتكون صفة معينة في شيئين كسواد واحد في محلين، وجوهر واحد في مكانين، ثم لا يكون ذلك في الحقيقة عموما وخصوصا، فإن مثل هذا ليس يقبل التخصيص؛ إذ يكون خاصا في كل محل، فلا يكون البتة عاما، وإذا لم يكن عاما لم يكن خاصا أيضا فيتناقض المعنى.
As for the aspect of error of those who affirm the state (al-ḥāl), it is that they affirm for a particular existing thing, pointed to, attributes specific to it and attributes in which other existents share with it – and this is among the most absurd of impossibilities. For that which is specific to a particular thing and that which others share with it are one with respect to that particular thing. So the existence of a particular accident, its accidentally, its color-ness, and its blackness are expressions for that particular thing pointed to. For if existence is specified by accidentally, then it is precisely accident; and if accidentally is specified by color-ness, then it is precisely color; and likewise color-ness with blackness, and blackness with this pointed-to blackness. So it is not rationally intelligible that an attribute exist for one particular thing and that same attribute exist for another thing, such that a specific attribute exists in two things, like one blackness in two loci, and one substance in two places; then that would not be, in reality, universal and particular. For such a thing does not admit of specification; since it would be specific in every locus, so it would never be universal, and if it is not universal, then it is not particular either – thus the meaning becomes contradictory.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first error committed by those who affirm the existence of 'states' (aḥwāl) as real attributes distinct from the essence. The author argues that they attribute to a particular existing thing both exclusive attributes and shared attributes, which is impossible. He explains that attributes like existence, accidentally, color-ness, and blackness all refer to the same particular thing; they cannot be separated such that one attribute is specific and another shared without contradiction. If existence becomes specified as accident, it is exactly that accident; similarly, accidentally specified as color is exactly color. Therefore, it is unintelligible for the same attribute to belong to two different things, like one blackness in two places or one substance in two locations, because then the attribute would be both particular and universal simultaneously, leading to a contradiction in terms.

Key Terms
حَال
صفة
عرض
جوهر
تماثل
اختلاف
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes can be shared across individuals without numerical identity, e.g., the same blackness in two objects.
Correction: The author shows this leads to a contradiction: the attribute would have to be both particular (specific to each locus) and universal (shared), which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for a specific attribute to exist in two different loci?
A) Because an attribute cannot be both specific and universal simultaneously.
B) Because attributes are mental constructs, not real.
C) Because existence and attributes are identical.
D) Because the proponents of aḥwāl deny causality.

Key Takeaway
The author's argument shows that any attribute claimed to be both specific to a thing and shared with others is logically incoherent.

The Contradiction of Existence as a State
Exposes the contradiction in claiming existence is a state while denying states existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that existence is a state leads to a self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Calling existence a state while denying states existence is a logical contradiction.

The author presents the second error of the proponents of states. They claim that a state is neither existent nor non-existent, yet they consider existence itself to be a state. This creates a direct contradiction: if existence is a state, then it cannot be described by existence, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the claim and contradiction
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition of a state and the claim that existence is a state.
Focus on the rhetorical question: 'How is it correct to say existence is not described by existence?'
2
Analyze the contradiction
Understand that if states cannot be described by existence, then existence itself, being a state, cannot be described by existence—a clear logical inconsistency.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider why the author calls this a contradiction in wording and meaning, and how it undermines the doctrine of states.
This contradiction shows the doctrine is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخطأ الثاني أنهم قالوا: الحال لا يوصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، والوجود عندهم حال، فكيف يصح أن يقال: الوجود لا يوصف بالوجود؟ وهل هو إلا تناقض في اللفظ والمعنى، أو ما لا يوصف بالوجود والعدم كيف يجوز أن يعم عندهم أصنافا وأعيانا؛ لأن العموم والشمول يستدعي أولا وجودًا محققًا وثبوتا كاملا حتى يشمل ويعم أو يعين ويخص، وأيضا فهم أثبتوا العلة والمعلول ثم قالوا: العلة توجب المعلول، وما ليس بموجود كيف يصير موجبا وموجبا، فالعلمية حال، والعالمية عندهم أيضا حال، فالموجب حال والموجب حال، والحال لا يوجب الحال؛ لأن ما لا يتصف بالوجود الحقيقي لا يتصف بكونه موجبا.
And the second error is that they said: the state is not described by existence nor by non-existence, while existence, according to them, is a state. So how is it correct to say: existence is not described by existence? And is this anything but a contradiction in wording and meaning? Or how can that which is not described by existence and non-existence, according to them, encompass kinds and individuals? For universality and inclusiveness require first realized existence and complete establishment so that it may include and encompass or specify and particularize. And also, they affirmed cause and effect, then they said: the cause necessitates the effect; and how can that which does not exist become a necessitator and necessitated? For causation (al-ʿilliyya) is a state, and effect-ness (al-maʿlūliyya) according to them is also a state. So the necessitator is a state and the necessitated is a state, and a state does not necessitate a state, because that which is not characterized by real existence is not characterized by being a necessitator.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage identifies the second error of the proponents of states. They claim that a state (ḥāl) is neither existent nor non-existent, yet they consider existence itself to be a state. This is a self-contradiction: if existence is a state, then it cannot be described by existence, leading to an absurdity. Furthermore, if a state lacks existence, it cannot have universality or particularity, as these require existence. The author also points out a contradiction in their causal theory: they affirm cause and effect, and say the cause necessitates the effect, but if causation (being a cause) is a state, and effect-ness is also a state, then one state would necessitate another, which is impossible because only something truly existent can be a necessitator.

Key Terms
حَال
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence as a state is a special case exempt from the definition, but the author shows no exemption can avoid the contradiction.
Correction: If existence is a state, it must be subject to the same rule, leading to self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction pointed out by the author?
A) Existence would not be described by existence.
B) States are non-existent by definition.
C) Existence cannot be a state.
D) States cannot be described at all.

Key Takeaway
Calling existence a state while denying states existence is a logical contradiction.

?

?
Are you willing to accept logical inconsistency in your own faith for the sake of comfort, or will you honestly pursue the truth even if it requires abandoning long-held assumptions?

States Cannot Have Universality Without Existence
Argues that universality and particularity require real existence, which states lack.

Core Idea
Universality demands existence; a non-existent state cannot encompass kinds.

The author continues his critique by addressing another consequence: if states are neither existent nor non-existent, they cannot have properties like universality or particularity, which require a foundation of existence.

1

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخطأ الثاني أنهم قالوا: الحال لا يوصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، والوجود عندهم حال، فكيف يصح أن يقال: الوجود لا يوصف بالوجود؟ وهل هو إلا تناقض في اللفظ والمعنى، أو ما لا يوصف بالوجود والعدم كيف يجوز أن يعم عندهم أصنافا وأعيانا؛ لأن العموم والشمول يستدعي أولا وجودًا محققًا وثبوتا كاملا حتى يشمل ويعم أو يعين ويخص، وأيضا فهم أثبتوا العلة والمعلول ثم قالوا: العلة توجب المعلول، وما ليس بموجود كيف يصير موجبا وموجبا، فالعلمية حال، والعالمية عندهم أيضا حال، فالموجب حال والموجب حال، والحال لا يوجب الحال؛ لأن ما لا يتصف بالوجود الحقيقي لا يتصف بكونه موجبا.
And the second error is that they said: the state is not described by existence nor by non-existence, while existence, according to them, is a state. So how is it correct to say: existence is not described by existence? And is this anything but a contradiction in wording and meaning? Or how can that which is not described by existence and non-existence, according to them, encompass kinds and individuals? For universality and inclusiveness require first realized existence and complete establishment so that it may include and encompass or specify and particularize. And also, they affirmed cause and effect, then they said: the cause necessitates the effect; and how can that which does not exist become a necessitator and necessitated? For causation (al-ʿilliyya) is a state, and effect-ness (al-maʿlūliyya) according to them is also a state. So the necessitator is a state and the necessitated is a state, and a state does not necessitate a state, because that which is not characterized by real existence is not characterized by being a necessitator.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage identifies the second error of the proponents of states. They claim that a state (ḥāl) is neither existent nor non-existent, yet they consider existence itself to be a state. This is a self-contradiction: if existence is a state, then it cannot be described by existence, leading to an absurdity. Furthermore, if a state lacks existence, it cannot have universality or particularity, as these require existence. The author also points out a contradiction in their causal theory: they affirm cause and effect, and say the cause necessitates the effect, but if causation (being a cause) is a state, and effect-ness is also a state, then one state would necessitate another, which is impossible because only something truly existent can be a necessitator.

حَال
وجود
عدم
عموم
خصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think universality is a purely logical relation independent of existence, but the author insists it requires real existence.
Correction: Universality and particularity require a real subject that exists; a non-existent state cannot have these attributes.

Checkpoint
What do universality and inclusiveness require according to the author?
A) A state
B) Non-existence
C) Realized existence and complete establishment
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Universality and particularity cannot be attributed to non-existent entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If universality requires existence, then your faith claims must be grounded in reality. Is your belief in God based on a mere mental concept or on the certainty of His existence? How often do you treat your beliefs as abstract ideas rather than living truths that demand action?
?
The author says non-existent states cannot encompass kinds. Are there areas in your life where you hold to abstract principles (like 'justice' or 'mercy') without the concrete existence of those virtues in your actions? How does existence precede applicability?
?
This argument shows that for something to be universal, it must exist. Consider your claim that Islam is a universal religion. Is that claim built on the actual existence of its truths in your life, or is it a mere assertion? What would it mean for Islam to be truly universal in your heart?

The Impossibility of Causality with States
Demonstrates the impossibility of causality if causation and effect-ness are states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why states cannot serve as causes or effects.

Core Idea
Causality requires real existence; a non-existent state cannot necessitate.

The author exposes a further inconsistency: the proponents affirm cause and effect but then treat causation and effect-ness as states. However, a state cannot necessitate another state because necessitation requires true existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting that the proponents affirm cause and effect and say the cause necessitates the effect.
2
Identify the reduction to states
The proponents consider 'being a cause' (al-ʿilliyya) and 'being an effect' (al-maʿlūliyya) as states.
This is a key step in their reasoning.
3
Understand the impossibility
Since states are not existent, a state cannot act as a necessitator. Thus, the causal relation is impossible if causation is a state.
The author concludes that only something with real existence can be a necessitator.

Third Error: Everything Becomes a State
Argues that on the aḥwāl view, every existent is a state, leaving nothing truly existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the aḥwāl theory, if consistently applied, eliminates all real existents by making everything a state.

Core Idea
If everything is a state, nothing is truly existent.

The third error builds on the previous two, showing that the proponents of aḥwāl cannot point to a single existent that is not a state. The author challenges them to find any entity—whether in the physical world or the divine realm—that escapes being classified as a state. By their own definitions, even existence itself becomes a state, along with all essential attributes. This forces the theory into a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage first; then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical question posed to the opponents.
2
Identify the main argument
The author demands an example of an existent that is not a state. Find the list of things that the opponents themselves consider states.
Existence, substantiality, spatial occupation, receptivity to accidents.
3
Understand the reductio
If everything is a state, then the term 'state' loses its meaning. The opponent's theory collapses.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Why does this argument target the aḥwāl theory specifically? Notice how it shows the theory cannot distinguish between states and real entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخطأ الثالث: إنا نقول: معاشر المثبتين كل ما أثبتموه في الوجود هو حال عندكم، فأرونا موجودًا في الشاهد والغائب هو ليس بحال لا يوصف بالوجود والعدم، فإن الوجود الذي هو الأعم الشامل للقديم والحادث عندكم حال، والجوهرية والتحيز وقبول العرض كلها أحوال، فليس على مقتضى مذهبكم شيء ما في الوجود هو ليس بحال، وإن أثبتم شيئًا وقلتم: هو ليس بحال فذلك محال.
The third error: we say: O company of affirmers (of states), everything you have affirmed in existence is, according to you, a state. So show us an existent, in the witnessed and the unseen, that is not a state, that is not described by existence and non-existence. For existence, which is the most general, encompassing the eternal and the originated, is according to you a state; and substantiality, spatial occupation, and receptivity to accidents are all states. So on the basis of your doctrine, there is nothing in existence that is not a state. And if you affirm something and say: it is not a state, that would be impossible.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the third error of the proponents of states (aḥwāl). The author challenges them to point to any existent, whether in the sensible world (shāhid) or the unseen (ghāʼib), that is not a state. According to their own theory, existence itself—the most universal attribute covering both the eternal and the originated—is a state. Likewise, substantiality (jawhariyya), spatial occupation (taḥayyuz), and receptivity to accidents (qubūl al-ʿaraḍ) are all states. Therefore, their doctrine leads to the conclusion that everything is a state, leaving no true reality. If they were to claim that something is not a state, that would be impossible within their framework.

Key Terms
حَال
وجود
جوهرية
تحيز
قبول العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the aḥwāl theory allows for a distinction between states and essences, preserving real existents.
Correction: The author shows that the theory collapses all distinctions: everything becomes a state, leaving no independent existent.

Checkpoint
What does the author demand from the proponents of aḥwāl in the third error?
A) To prove the existence of a state.
B) To show an existent that is not a state.
C) To define the term 'existence'.
D) To prove causality.

Key Takeaway
The aḥwāl theory, when pressed, reduces all real entities to mere states, leaving no independent existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever treat abstract concepts or attributes as if they were real entities, forgetting that they depend on a concrete subject? How might this argument reveal a tendency in your thinking that you need to correct?
?

?
The author argues that on the aḥwāl view, everything is a state and nothing is truly existent. Could it be that in your spiritual life, you have reduced your relationship with Allah to a set of states or emotions, rather than grounding it in a real, existent essence? What would it mean to ensure your faith is built on something truly exisent?

The Second Error: The Contradiction of Existence as a State
The author presents a logical contradiction in the theory of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that existence is a state leads to a self-contradiction and undermines the theory of states.

Core Idea
If existence is a state, then 'existence is not described by existence' is a flat contradiction.

The author continues his critique of the theory of states. The second error reveals a logical contradiction at the heart of the theory: the affirmers claim states are neither existent nor non-existent, yet they also hold that existence itself is a state. This creates a paradox that cannot be resolved without abandoning the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the contradiction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author points out that the affirmers say states are not described by existence or non-existence, yet they treat existence as a state.
Focus on the phrase 'الوجود لا يوصف بالوجود'.
2
Understand the argument about universality
The author argues that universality and generality require real existence. Ask yourself: why can't a non-existent state have types or individuals?
This shows the theory cannot account for general concepts.
3
Examine the causality point
The author notes that causality and knowledge are states, but a state cannot be a cause. Reflect on why a non-existent thing cannot necessitate an effect.
This reinforces the incoherence of the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخطأ الثاني أنهم قالوا : الحال لا يوصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، والوجود عندهم حال، فكيف يصح أن يقال : الوجود لا يوصف بالوجود؟ وهل هو إلا تناقض في اللفظ والمعنى، أوما لا يوصف بالوجود والعدم كيف يجوز أن أصنافا وأعيانا؛ لأن العموم والشمول يستدعي أولا وجودًا محققًا وثبوتا كاملا يعم عندهم حتى يشمل ويعم أو يعين ويخص، وأيضًا فهم أثبتوا العلة والمعلول ثم قالوا: العلة توجب المعلول، وما ليس بموجود كيف يصير موجبا وموجبا، فالعلمية حال، والعالمية عندهم أيضا حال، فالموجب حال والموجب حال، والحال لا يوجب الحال؛ لأن ما لا يتصف بالوجود الحقيقي لا يتصف بكونه موجبا. الخطأ الثالث : إِنَّا نقول : معاشر المثبتين كل ما أثبتموه في الوجود هو حال عندكم، فأرونا موجودًا في الشاهد والغائب هو ليس بحلل لا يوصف بالوجود والعدم، فإن الوجود الذي هو الأعم الشامل للقديم والحادث عندكم حال، والجوهرية والتحيز وقبوله العرض كلها أحوال، فليس على مقتضى مذهبكم شيء ما في الوجود هو ليس بحال، وإن أثبتم شيئًا وقلتم : هو ليس بحال فذلك الشيء يشتمل على عموم وخصوص، والأخص والأعم عندكم حال، فإذا الا شيء إلا لا شيء، ولا وجود إلا لا وجود، وهذا من أمحل ما يتصور.
The second error: they said: the state (al-hal) is not described by existence nor by non-existence, while existence according to them is a state. So how is it correct to say: existence is not described by existence? Is this not a contradiction in wording and meaning? Or that which is not described by existence and non-existence, how can it be permissible that it has types and individuals? Because universality and generality require first a realized existence and complete subsistence to encompass and include or to specify and particularize. Moreover, they affirmed the cause and the effect, then said: the cause necessitates the effect. How can that which does not exist become a necessitator? So causality is a state, and being a knower according to them is also a state. So the necessitator is a state, and the effect is a state. And a state does not necessitate a state, because that which is not attributed with true existence is not attributed with being a necessitator. The third error: we say: O community of affirmers, everything that you have affirmed in existence is a state according to you. So show us an existent in the witnessed and the absent that is not a state, not described by existence and non-existence. For existence, which is the most general encompassing the eternal and the temporal, is a state according to you. And substantiality, occupying space, and receptivity of accidents are all states. So, according to your doctrine, nothing in existence is not a state. And if you affirm something and say: it is not a state, that thing comprises universality and particularity, and the particular and the general are states according to you. Then there is nothing except nothing, and no existence except non-existence. And this is among the most absurd things conceivable.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents two errors committed by the proponents of the theory of states (ahwal). The author is critiquing the doctrine that states are neither existent nor non-existent. The second error is a logical contradiction: the affirmers define 'state' as something not described by existence or non-existence, but they also hold that existence itself is a state. This leads to the absurd claim that 'existence is not described by existence.' The author emphasizes that universality and generality require real existence to encompass particulars, and that causality and knowledge are also states, but a state cannot act as a cause because it lacks true existence. The third error is that everything affirmed as existent—including existence itself, substance, accidents—is reduced to a state under their theory, leaving nothing that is genuinely existent. Any attempt to posit something beyond a state collapses into generality and particularity, which are themselves states. The result is nihilism: nothing exists but nothingness. The author uses reductio ad absurdum to show the theory is untenable.

Key Terms
حال
وجود
عدم
علة
معلول
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author completely rejects the concept of states (ahwal) altogether.
Correction: The author only rejects the specific characterization that states are neither existent nor non-existent. He does not deny that attributes or states can be meaningfully discussed.

Checkpoint
What is the second error the author attributes to the affirmers of states?
A) They claim existence is a state, yet existence is not described by existence, creating a contradiction.
B) They deny the existence of causality.
C) They confuse universals with particulars.
D) They reject the concept of the 'witnessed and absent'.

Key Takeaway
The theory of states collapses because it cannot consistently describe even itself, as it makes existence both a state and not describable by existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals how even a small contradiction can unravel an entire system. Are there beliefs you hold that, if truly examined, would lead to a contradiction with your core commitment to tawhid? What would it cost you to face that contradiction honestly today?
?
The author insists that only what truly exists can be a cause. Where in your daily life do you unconsciously attribute causality to things that are themselves dependent and non-existent in themselves? How can you consciously redirect your trust to the only true Cause, Allah, starting with one decision you make today?
?
If existence is a state, then it is not described by existence—a flat contradiction. This is not merely academic. When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly mean He is the Necessary Existent, whose existence is not a state but His very reality? Or do you sometimes treat His existence as a mere attribute? How would your worship change if you meditated on this truth?

The Third Error: The Theory of States Leads to Nihilism
The author shows the theory of states collapses into nihilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the affirmers' theory reduces everything to states, leaving no genuine existence, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If everything is a state, then nothing truly exists.

The third error is a reductio ad absurdum of the theory of states. The author shows that, according to the affirmers' own principles, every existent thing is merely a state—including existence itself, substance, accidents, and all categories. This leaves nothing that is actually existent, leading to utter nihilism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the list of things deemed states
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the items the author lists as states according to the affirmers: existence, substantiality, occupying space, receptivity of accidents.
The list is meant to be exhaustive of all categories.
2
Understand the argument about generality and particularity
The author argues that if you try to posit something not a state, it will involve particular and general, which are themselves states. So there is no escape.
This shows the theory leaves no room for genuine existence.
3
Appreciate the force of the reductio
The conclusion is that on their view, everything is a state, so nothing truly exists. Reflect on why this is 'among the most absurd things.'
The theory self-destructs into nihilism.

Commentary
This passage presents two errors committed by the proponents of the theory of states (ahwal). The author is critiquing the doctrine that states are neither existent nor non-existent. The second error is a logical contradiction: the affirmers define 'state' as something not described by existence or non-existence, but they also hold that existence itself is a state. This leads to the absurd claim that 'existence is not described by existence.' The author emphasizes that universality and generality require real existence to encompass particulars, and that causality and knowledge are also states, but a state cannot act as a cause because it lacks true existence. The third error is that everything affirmed as existent—including existence itself, substance, accidents—is reduced to a state under their theory, leaving nothing that is genuinely existent. Any attempt to posit something beyond a state collapses into generality and particularity, which are themselves states. The result is nihilism: nothing exists but nothingness. The author uses reductio ad absurdum to show the theory is untenable.

Key Terms
وجود
حال
جوهرية
تحيز
عرض
عموم
خصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is presenting a straw man or that the affirmers could easily avoid this by distinguishing states from existents.
Correction: The author's reductio shows that given the affirmers' definitions, every category they accept (existence, substance, etc.) falls under 'state,' so the nihilism follows necessarily.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the third error according to the author?
A) The theory of states leads to nihilism: nothing exists except nothing.
B) The theory of states is merely incomplete and needs refinement.
C) The theory of states is the only correct view.
D) The theory of states contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
A theory that makes everything a state leaves nothing genuinely existent, culminating in the absurdity of nihilism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever become so absorbed in abstract theological reasoning that the living reality of Allah became distant, almost like a mental concept? What can you do today to turn your knowledge into heartfelt presence?
?
The author shows that pushing a theory to its logical conclusion can lead to denying existence itself. In your own life, have you held a position that, if fully consistent, would contradict something you know to be true by faith? Would you have the courage to abandon it?
?
This passage calls the logical outcome of the theory 'among the most absurd things conceivable'. When you encounter an argument that feels comfortable but leads to absurdity when pressed, are you willing to re-examine it with intellectual honesty, even if it challenges your school of thought?

Universals as Mental Existents
The author presents his own view on universals and critiques both negators and affirmers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's view that universals exist as real meanings in the human mind, and how he critiques both nominalism and naive realism.

Core Idea
Universals are real in the mind, not in the external world.

The author reconciles the debate over universals by locating them in the mind. After rejecting that universals are mere words or external attributes, he argues they are genuine meanings perceived by the intellect. This passage establishes the middle path between nominalism and realism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the author's argumentative flow.
Notice the author uses a process of elimination right at the start.
2
Identify the author's elimination method
The author first rules out two possibilities: universals as mere expressions and universals as external individuals. Identify where he does this.
The key phrase is 'إما أن ترجع إلى الألفاظ ... وإما أن ترجع إلى الأعيان'.
3
Examine the critique of both schools
The author evaluates negators of states (nominalists) and affirmers of states. Find what he says each got right and wrong.
Pay attention to the structure: 'فنفاة الأحوال أخطئوا ... وأصابوا ... ومثبتو الأحوال أخطئوا ... وأصابوا'.
4
Understand the final position
The author concludes that universals are 'meanings realized and established in the human mind'. Reflect on what this means for the status of universals.
He also reformulates the affirmers' statement from 'neither existent nor non-existent' to 'existent conceived in minds'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحق في المسألة إذًا أن الإنسان يجد من نفسه تصور أشياء كلية عامة مطلقة دون ملاحظة جانب الألفاظ، ولا ملاحظة جانب الأعيان، ويجد من نفسه اعتبارات عقلية لشيء واحد، وهي إما أن ترجع إلى الألفاظ المحددة وقد أبطلناه، وإما أن ترجع إلى الأعيان الموجودة المشار إليها وقد زيفناه، فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : معان هي موجودة محققة في ذهن الإنسان، والعقل الإنساني هو المدرك لها، ومن حيث هي كلية عامة لا وجود لها في الأعيان فلا موجود مطلقا في الأعيان، ولا عرض مطلقا، ولا لون مطلقا، بل هي الأعيان؛ بحيث يتصور العقل منها معنى كليا عاما فتصاغ له عبارة لتطابقه وتنص عليه، ويعتبر العقل منها معنى ووجها فتصاغ له عبارة حتى لو طاحت العبارات أو تبدلت لم تبطل المعنى المقدر في الذهن المتصور في العقل. فنفاة الأحوال أخطئوا من حيث ردها إلى العبارات المجردة، وأصابوا حيث قالوا ما ثبت وجوده معينًا لا عموم فيه ولا اعتبار ومثبتو الأحوال أخطئوا من حيث ردوها إلى صفات في الأعيان، وأصابوا من حيث قالوا : هي معان معقولة وراء العبارات، وكان من حقهم أن يقولوا: هي موجودة متصورة في الأذهان بدل قولهم : لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وهذه المعاني مما لا ينكرها عاقل من نفسه، غير أن
The truth in the matter, then, is that man finds in himself the conception of universal general absolute things without observing the aspect of expressions, and without observing the aspect of external individuals. He finds in himself rational considerations of one thing. They either go back to defined expressions, which we have invalidated, or go back to indicated existent external individuals, which we have refuted. Nothing remains except to say: they are meanings that are realized and established in the human mind, and the human intellect is the perceiver of them. Insofar as they are universal and general, they have no existence in external individuals. So there is no existence absolutely in external individuals, nor absolute accident, nor absolute color; rather they are the individuals, such that the intellect conceives from them a universal general meaning, then an expression is coined to correspond to it and indicate it. The intellect considers from them a meaning and an aspect, then an expression is coined for it, so that if the expressions fall away or change, the meaning estimated in the mind, conceived in the intellect, does not become void. So the negators of states were wrong insofar as they reduced them to bare expressions, and they were right where they said: what is established in existence is determinate, having no universality or consideration. And the affirmers of states were wrong insofar as they reduced them to attributes in external individuals, and they were right where they said: they are rational meanings beyond expressions. It was proper for them to say: they are existent conceived in minds instead of their statement: not existent nor non-existent. And these meanings are such that no rational person denies them from himself, except that
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own resolution to the problem of universals and states. He begins by stating that man finds within himself the conception of universal, general, absolute things independent of expressions and external particulars. He identifies two possible reductions for these rational considerations: to defined expressions (which he has already invalidated) or to external existent individuals (which he has refuted). The only remaining possibility is that they are meanings that are realized and established in the human mind, perceived by the intellect. As universals, they have no existence in external individuals; there is no absolute existence, absolute accident, or absolute color in the external world, only specific individuals. The intellect derives a universal meaning from them, and an expression is coined to correspond to that meaning. If the expressions are lost or changed, the mental meaning remains. The author then evaluates both schools: the negators of states (likely nominalists) erred by reducing universals to mere expressions, but they were correct that what exists externally is determinate and lacks universality. The affirmers of states erred by positing them as attributes in external individuals, but they were correct that they are rational meanings beyond expressions. Their proper formulation should have been that states are existent and conceived in minds, not 'neither existent nor non-existent.' These mental meanings are undeniable to any rational person. The passage ends with 'except that' indicating a qualification on the next page, which is not present in this window.

Correction: The author affirms universals as real meanings existing in the mind, not as mere words nor as external attributes. They are genuine objects of thought.

Existence vs. Subsistence: The Affirmers' Distinction
The affirmers respond by distinguishing existence from subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirmers distinguish between existence (wujud) and subsistence (thubut) to argue that non-existents have a mode of reality without being existent.

Core Idea
Existence and subsistence are not the same; the non-existent is subsistent but not existent.

In this short but crucial passage, the affirmers respond to the negators' charge that the non-existent is nothing. They accept the logical opposition of negation and affirmation, but they also note another opposition: between existence and non-existence. By distinguishing existence from subsistence, they carve out a middle ground: the non-existent is not existent but is still subsistent, thus preserving its 'thingness'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the parallel structure between two oppositions.
The affirmers start by conceding the first opposition but then introduce a second.
2
Identify the two oppositions
The passage mentions two oppositions: negation/affirmation (nafy/ithbat) and existence/non-existence (wujud/'adam). The affirmers then relate this to their distinction.
They claim they give each division its due.
3
Understand the key distinction
The affirmers state that existence and subsistence are not synonymous. Similarly, non-existent and negated are not synonyms. This allows them to say that a non-existent thing has subsistence (thubut) even though it lacks existence.
This is the core doctrinal move.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: it refutes the idea that the non-existent is absolutely nothing. Instead, it exists in a different sense (subsistence).
This is a key theological claim about the reality of mental or future things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال من أثبت المعدوم شيئًا : كما تقرر في العقل تقابل النفي والإثبات، فقد تقرر أيضًا تقابل الوجود والعدم، فنحن وفزنا على كل تقسيم عقلي حظه، وقلنا : إن الوجود والثبوت لا يترادفان على معنى واحد والمعدوم والمنفي كذلك.
He who affirmed the non-existent to be a thing said: Just as the opposition of negation and affirmation is established in the intellect, so too the opposition of existence and non-existence is established. So we have each rational division receive its due, and we said: Existence and subsistence are not synonymous in one meaning, and similarly, the non-existent and the negated are not synonymous.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the response of those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing (man athbata al-ma'dum shay'an). They accept the rational opposition between negation and affirmation, but they point out that there is also an opposition between existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam). They claim that they give each rational division its due: they distinguish between existence and subsistence (thubut), stating that these two terms are not synonymous. Similarly, 'non-existent' (al-ma'dum) and 'negated' (al-manfi) are not synonymous. This allows them to say that the non-existent is something that has subsistence (thubut) without having existence. Thus, the negators' argument that the non-existent is negated and therefore not subsistent is answered: the non-existent is not existent but is subsistent.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ثبوت (thubut)
عدم ('adam)
نفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The affirmers believe that non-existent things have actual existence.
Correction: They do not. They distinguish existence (wujud) from subsistence (thubut). The non-existent has subsistence without existence, a different mode of being.

Checkpoint
How do the affirmers distinguish existence (wujud) from subsistence (thubut)?
A) They are synonyms.
B) Existence is specific to the eternal, subsistence to the contingent.
C) Subsistence is broader and includes both existent and non-existent.
D) Existence is the same as subsistence for the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Existence and subsistence are distinct; the non-existent is subsistent but not existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your past sins are still 'subsistent', are you truly done with them, or are they still present before Allah? What will you do to erase their reality through sincere tawbah?

Proximity and Distance and Infinite Accidents
The author refutes the claim that proximity/distance are added accidents by showing it leads to infinite accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that proximity and distance are added accidents leads to an infinite regress, and how the author refutes that position.

Core Idea
Claiming proximity and distance are added accidents leads to an impossibility: infinite accidents.

In this passage, the author tackles a specific argument in kalām: whether proximity and distance are real additional attributes (accidents) that inhere in substances. He shows that this view leads to an absurd consequence—an infinite number of accidents—and thereby refutes it. As you read, pay attention to how he distinguishes proximity/distance from other categories and why the infinite regress is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the technical terms.
The passage uses terms like al-qurb wa al-bu'd, al-iḍāfa, al-waḍ‘, al-ayn.
2
Identify the three categories listed
The author says proximity/distance may be from relation, position, or location. Recognize that these are the categories of accidents in which they can inhere.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author argues that if proximity/distance were added accidents, then because distances are unlimited, accidents would be infinite. Since infinite accidents in finite substances is impossible, the claim is false.
Focus on the key phrase: 'leads to establishing accidents that do not end.'
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the example given by the opposing view actually refutes itself. Reflect on why this is a strong refutation.
The phrase 'lāzim ʿalayhim' indicates the argument backfires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخالف ما نحن فيه حال القرب والبعد بالإضافة إلى شيء دون شيء، فإن قرب الجوهر من جوهر قد يكون من باب الإضافة، وقد يكون من باب الوضع، وقد يكون من باب الأين، لكن لفظ القرب والبعد يطلق على كل جوهرين تقارباً أو تباعدًا بمقدار ما، والمقادير بينهما لا تنحصر في حد، فقيل : فعند ذلك تختلف بالنسب والإضافات وهي لا تنحصر فلو قلنا: إنهما أمران زائدان على وجودهما أدى ذلك إلى إثبات أعراض لا تتناهى الجوهرين متناهيين لكن ما فيه القرب والبعد متناهيين؛ أعني : الأين والوضع والإضافة، فهي أمور زائدة على الجوهرين، فعرف أن المثال الذي تمثلوا به لازم عليهم.
And the case of proximity and distance differs from what we are in, with respect to addition to something without something. For the proximity of a substance to a substance may be from the category of relation, and may be from the category of position, and may be from the category of location. But the term proximity and distance is applied to every two substances that are near or far by a certain amount, and the distances between them are not confined to a limit, so it was said: then at that point they differ according to relations and additions, which are not limited. So if we say that they are two matters additional to their existence, that leads to establishing accidents that do not end, while the two substances are finite, but that in which proximity and distance [subsist] are finite, namely: location, position, and relation. So they are matters additional to the two substances. Thus it is known that the example they gave is incumbent upon them.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument. The author first distinguishes the case of proximity and distance from other categories like relation, position, and location. He argues that the terms proximity and distance are used for any two substances that are near or far by some amount, and the distances between them are unlimited. If one claimed that proximity and distance are added accidents (أعراض) over and above the substances, then that would entail an infinite number of accidents, because the possible distances are unlimited. However, the things in which proximity and distance subsist (i.e., location, position, relation) are finite in number. Therefore, the example they proposed (that proximity/distance are added accidents) is actually self-defeating (lazim 'alayhim).

Key Terms
القرب والبعد (proximity and distance)
الإضافة (relation/addition)
العرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proximity and distance are necessarily accidents that are added to substances.
Correction: The author shows that proximity/distance can belong to categories like relation, position, or location, which are not necessarily added accidents. Moreover, if they were added accidents, it would lead to infinite accidents, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that proximity and distance cannot be added accidents?
A) Because they are essential properties of substances.
B) Because that would lead to an infinite number of accidents.
C) Because they are only relational and not real.
D) Because substances are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Proximity and distance cannot be added accidents because that would entail an infinite number of accidents, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a false premise leads to an impossibility. In your own life, is there a belief you hold about Allah or His creation that, if examined honestly, leads to a logical contradiction? How would you resolve it based on what you've learned here?
?
The categories of proximity and distance are finite, yet the distances themselves are not. This reminds you that creation is finite but points to an infinite Creator. Does your daily awe of Allah reflect the vastness of His attributes, or do you limit Him by your own finite reasoning? Commit to one specific change in how you think about Allah's transcendence.

Why Denying States Undermines Causal Attribution
Argues that denying states makes causal attribution meaningless

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari theory of states (الأحوال) is essential for a coherent account of causation and real attribute attribution.

Core Idea
Denying states reduces causal language to empty mental labels.

In this passage, the author presents a critical argument: if you deny the reality of 'states' — real but non-existent attributes — you cannot meaningfully speak of causes and effects. Without states, statements like 'the world is knowing' become mere words without causal grounding. This argument reveals why Ash'ari theologians insisted on the theory of states as a necessary metaphysical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author structures the argument: a conditional (man nafā...), a consequence (lam yasiḥ...), and an explanation (fa-innahū lam yabqa...).
The Arabic is dense; take time with each phrase.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that denying states (al-ḥāl) as fixed attributes in essences invalidates the proof of cause and effect (ʿilla and maʿlūl). Find this statement and mark it.
The author uses 'lam yasiḥ ithbāt' – 'the proof is not valid'.
3
Understand the consequence
What remains after denying states? The author says only 'bare entities' (aʿyān muḥḍa) or 'a mode of rational consideration' (wajh iʿtibār ʿaqlī). See how this makes causal statements empty.
Example: 'The world is knowing' becomes just 'it has knowledge' – no real causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن نفى الحال وكونها صفة ثابتة في الأعيان لم يصح إثبات العلة والمعلول على أصله تحقيقا، فإنه لم يبق إلا عبارة أعيانًا محضة أو وجه اعتبار عقلي، فلا معنى لكون العالم عالماً على أصله إلا أنه شيء ما له علم.
And whoever denies the state (al-ḥāl) and its being a fixed attribute in essences, the proof of cause and effect according to his principle is not valid in reality, for nothing remains except a phrase of bare entities or a mode of rational consideration. So on his principle there is no meaning to the world's being knowing except that it is a thing that has knowledge.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the necessity of affirming 'states' (الأحوال) as real but non-existent attributes. The author states that anyone who denies the reality of states – that is, who denies that attributes like 'being knowing' are real, distinct entities inhering in substances – cannot properly establish a system of cause and effect. According to such a denial, what remains are only bare entities (أعيان محضة) or merely mental considerations. In that framework, saying 'the world is knowing' has no real content beyond saying 'the world is a thing that has knowledge' – it does not express any real causal relation between knowledge and the state of being-knowing. Therefore, the proof of cause and effect (إثبات العلة والمعلول) cannot be soundly built on such a principle. The author is critiquing a position that rejects the Ash'ari theory of states, which was a compromise between affirming real attributes and avoiding multiplicity of eternals.

Key Terms
الحال
الصفة الثابتة
العين
العلة
المعلول
وجه الاعتبار العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think states (الحال) are imaginary or superfluous, so denying them does not affect causal reasoning.
Correction: The author argues that without states, causal claims become empty: 'the world is knowing' reduces to 'it has knowledge' without any real relation between the entity and its attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of denying states (الحال) according to the author?
A) Nothing remains except bare entities or mental considerations.
B) It becomes impossible to prove the existence of God.
C) The world becomes uncreated.
D) Knowledge becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The reality of states is necessary to give causal claims real content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does your answer affect your reliance on His wisdom in your daily life? Commit to examining your belief about God's attributes this week.

If the latter, what must change in your understanding so that your attribution carries real meaning? Write one concrete action to deepen your conviction.
?
If the reality of states is denied, then causal language becomes empty. Have you ever spoken of Allah's attributes or actions as if they were mere phrases without real content? Confess this to yourself and resolve to read the next verse about Allah's names with the intention of affirming their reality.

Definition and Essence in Kalam
The author discusses definition and essence and the debate between negators and affirmers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on definition and essence in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Definition and essence are disputed: synonymous or distinct with a state.

In this passage, the author introduces the concepts of definition and essence as common between the witnessed and unseen. He presents the dispute between negators and affirmers of states on whether definition is identical to the defined and whether it is the same as essence. This sets the stage for understanding how theologians approach definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two questions posed: is definition identical to the defined, and is it one with essence?
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the negators of states (Nufat al-Ahwal) and the affirmers of states (Muthbitat al-Ahwal). Note what each group says about definition and essence.
3
Understand the affirmers' view
The affirmers define definition as a statement clarifying the attribute shared by individuals. That attribute is a 'state' (hal) and the particularity (khasisa) distinguishes the defined.
This is the key technical concept.
4
Reflect on the author's remark
The author notes that most theologian definitions are just word replacements, and logicians go to extremes. Consider why that matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجوامع بين الشاهد والغائب الحد والحقيقة، جرى رسم المتكلمين بذكر الحد والحقيقة في هذا الموضع، وقد اختلفوا في أن الحد عين المحدود أو غيره، وأنه والحقيقة شيء واحد أم شيئان؟

قال نفاة الأحوال : حد الشيء وحقيقته وذاته وعينه عبارات عن معبر واحد. وقال مثبتو الأحوال : إن الحد قول الحاد المبين عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود، فإن المحدود عندهم يتميز عن غيره بخاصية شاملة لجميع آحاد المحدود، وتلك الخاصية حال، ويعبر عن تلك الحال بلفظ شامل دال عليه جامع مانع ما له من الخاصية ويمنع ما ليس له من خواص غيره، ثم من الأشياء ما يحد ومنها ما لا يحد على أصلهم، وأكثر حدود المتكلمين راجع إلى تبديل لفظ بلفظ أعرف منه، وربما يكون مثله في الخفا والجلا، وإنما أهل المنطق يبالغون في ذكر
And from the things that are common between the witnessed and the unseen are definition (al-hadd) and essence (al-haqiqa). The custom of the theologians (al-mutakallimin) has been to mention definition and essence in this context. They have differed as to whether the definition is identical to the defined or other than it, and whether it and essence are one thing or two.

The negators of states (al-ahwal) said: The definition of a thing, its essence, its self (dhat), and its 'ayn are expressions for a single meaning. The affirmers of states said: Definition is a statement of the limit (al-hadd) clarifying the attribute (al-sifa) in which the individuals of the defined share. For the defined, according to them, is distinguished from others by a particularity (khasisa) encompassing all individuals of the defined, and that particularity is a state (hal). And they express that state by a comprehensive word indicating it, including and excluding, including what belongs to it of particularity and excluding what does not belong to it of the particularities of others. Then, some things have a definition and some do not according to their principle. And most definitions of the theologians go back to replacing a word with a more known word, and perhaps it is similar in obscurity and clarity. And the logicians (ahl al-mantiq) go to extremes in mentioning
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage introduces the concepts of definition (hadd) and essence (haqiqa) as common between the witnessed and the unseen. The author notes that theologians customarily mention these terms in this context and have disputed two issues: whether the definition is identical to the defined or different, and whether definition and essence are the same or distinct. He then presents two opposing views. The negators of states (Nufat al-Ahwal) hold that definition, essence, self, and 'ayn are all synonymous. The affirmers of states (Muthbitat al-Ahwal) define definition as a statement clarifying the attribute shared by the individuals of the defined; that attribute is a state (hal). According to them, the defined is distinguished by a particularity (khasisa) that is a state, and the definition expresses that state with a comprehensive word that includes and excludes. They also hold that not all things have a definition. The author then remarks that most definitions by theologians are merely replacing a word with a better-known word, and that logicians exaggerate in this matter.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that definition and essence are always distinct concepts.
Correction: The negators of states argue that definition, essence, self, and 'ayn are all synonymous—they are the same thing.

Checkpoint

A) The same as the essence of the thing.
B) A statement clarifying the attribute shared by individuals of the defined.
C) A verbal substitution without meaning.
D) A logical fallacy.

Key Takeaway
The affirmers of states see definition as a statement of the distinguishing state, while negators equate definition with essence and self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The affirmers of states say that a definition is a comprehensive word that 'includes and excludes' — it includes only what belongs to the thing and excludes what does not. What 'definition' have you unconsciously accepted for yourself that includes sins or excludes righteous deeds? Are you willing to revise that definition based on the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The negators of states see definition, essence, self, and 'ayn as all one reality. In your own life, do you treat your actions (states) as separate from your core self, or do you see them as reflecting your true essence? How does your answer affect your urgency to repent?
?
The author notes that most definitions of theologians are just replacing a word with a more known word, often still obscure. When you learn a new Islamic term (like 'iman' or 'tawhid'), do you merely swap it with a familiar word, or do you seek to understand its precise meaning and its implications for your daily worship? This lesson challenges you to move beyond surface-level knowledge.

Defining Reality: The Mutakallimun on Limit and Reality
Presents the dispute on definition and reality among mutakallimun and critiques logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions among mutakallimun on whether definition (ḥadd) and reality (ḥaqīqa) are identical, and the author's critique of both mutakallimun and logicians in defining things.

Core Idea
Do words capture the true nature of things? The mutakallimun were divided.

This passage tackles a foundational question: is a thing's definition the same as its reality? The author presents two camps—negators of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl) and affirmers of states (muthbitāt al-aḥwāl)—with opposing views. He then critiques the definitions of the mutakallimun and contrasts them with logicians, ultimately arguing that grasping true essences is extremely difficult.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Let the flow of the dispute and critique register naturally.
This passage is somewhat technical; focus on the main contrast in views.
2
Identify the two groups and their positions
Find where the nufāt al-aḥwāl and muthbitāt al-aḥwāl are mentioned. Note what each says about limit and reality.
The negators say they are identical; the affirmers define limit as a statement about a shared attribute.
3
Analyze the critique of definitions
The author says most mutakallimun definitions just replace a word with a more familiar one. Then he compares logicians to a prosody expert without poetic talent. Understand the point: both groups struggle to define essences.
The key is that defining true essences is very difficult, as stated at the end.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by saying logicians may be excused because grasping common and specific essential properties is hard. Consider how this reflects the author's own stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجوامع بين الشاهد والغائب الحد والحقيقة، جرى رسم المتكلمين بذكر الحد والحقيقة في هذا الموضع، وقد اختلفوا في أن الحد عين المحدود أو غيره، وأنه والحقيقة شيء واحد أم شيئان؟ قال نفاة الأحوال : حد الشيء وحقيقته وذاته وعينه عبارات عن معبر واحد. وقال مثبتو الأحوال : إن الحد قول الحاد المبين عن الصفة التي تشترك فيها آحاد المحدود، فإن المحدود عندهم يتميز عن غيره بخاصية شاملة لجميع آحاد المحدود، وتلك الخاصية حال، ويعبر عن تلك الحال بلفظ شامل دال عليه جامع مانع ما له من الخاصية ويمنع ما ليس له من خواص غيره، ثم من الأشياء ما يحد ومنها ما لا يحد على أصلهم، وأكثر حدود المتكلمين راجع إلى تبديل لفظ بلفظ أعرف منه، وربما يكون مثله في الخفا والجلا، وإنما أهل المنطق يبالغون في ذكر شروط الحد، ويحققون في استيفاء جوانبه لفظاً ومعنى، غير أنهم إذا شرعوا في تحديد الأشياء وتحقيق ماهياتها يأتون بأبعد ما يأتي به المتكلمون؛ كمن يتقن علم العروض ولا طبع له في الشعر، أو كمن يكون له طبع ولا مادة له من النحو والأدب فيعود حسيرًا ويصبح أسيرًا، ولعلهم معذورون؛ لأن الظفر بالذاتيات المشتركة والذاتيات الخاصة جدا عسير.
And among the common terms between the visible and the unseen are limit (ḥadd) and reality (ḥaqīqa). The custom of the mutakallimūn was to mention limit and reality in this place. They differed on whether the limit is identical to the limited or other than it, and whether it and reality are one thing or two. The negators of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl) said: Limit, reality, essence, and identity of a thing are expressions for a single referent. And the affirmers of states (muthbitāt al-aḥwāl) said: The limit is a statement of the hadd [boundary] explaining the attribute that the individuals of the limited share. For the limited, in their view, is distinguished from others by a property covering all its individuals, and that property is a state (ḥāl). They express that state with a comprehensive term that is inclusive and exclusive of what it has of the property and excludes what is not of its other properties. Then among things, some can be limited and some cannot, according to their principle. Most definitions of the mutakallimūn reduce to replacing a word with a more familiar one, and it may be similar in clarity and obscurity. The logicians exaggerate in mentioning the conditions of definition and verify in covering its aspects verbally and in meaning, but when they begin to define things and realize their essences, they come with something more remote than what the mutakallimūn bring, like one who masters prosody but has no poetic talent, or one who has talent but has no material from grammar and literature, so he becomes exhausted and becomes a captive. Perhaps they are excused because grasping the common and specific essential properties is very difficult.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the concepts of 'limit' (ḥadd) and 'reality' (ḥaqīqa) as common terms between the visible and the unseen. The mutakallimūn customarily mention these terms. They differed on whether the limit is identical to the limited (maḥdūd) or distinct, and whether limit and reality are the same or two things. The negators of states (nufāt al-aḥwāl) hold that limit, reality, essence, and identity are expressions for a single referent. The affirmers of states (muthbitāt al-aḥwāl) define limit as a statement explaining the attribute shared by all individuals of the limited. For them, the limited is distinguished from others by a particular property covering all its individuals, and that property is a state (ḥāl). They express that state with a comprehensive term that is inclusive and exclusive. Some things can be limited, others cannot, according to their principle. Most definitions of the mutakallimūn reduce to replacing a word with a more familiar one, sometimes equally clear or obscure. The logicians, however, elaborate on the conditions of definition, but when they define, they often go farther afield than the mutakallimūn, like one who masters prosody but has no poetic talent. They may be excused because grasping the common and specific essential properties is very difficult.

Correction: The author argues that logicians, despite their rigorous methodology, often produce definitions that are more remote from the essence than those of mutakallimun, as they lack the intuitive grasp of the thing's reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your personal life have you assumed you truly understood a concept in Islam (like tawḥīd or taqwā) when your understanding was merely a surface-level replacement of one word for another? What would it take for you to go deeper?
?
