Existence as General Concept Suffices for Division
Continuation of argument: existence as general concept suffices for division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if existence is not a genus, it still functions as a general concept enabling division between necessary and possible beings.

Core Idea
Existence as a general concept suffices for division, even without being a genus.

In this continuation, the author assumes existence is not a genus but insists it remains a general concept that encompasses both necessary and possible beings. He argues that division is based on meaning, not mere words, and the general-special relationship works like genus-differentia in conveying distinct concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully and then its literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument: it starts with an assumption (sallamna) and then draws conclusions.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the argument's movement
The author concedes that existence is not a genus but argues it still serves as a general concept. Identify the key claim: division applies to meaning (ma'na), not mere words. Then see how the general-special relationship is compared to genus-differentia.
3
Reflect on the logical consequence
The author concludes that the same thing follows from general-special as from genus-differentia. Consider why this matters for the overall argument about existence and necessity/possibility.
This lesson shows that the theological division into necessary and possible does not depend on existence being a genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن سلم لكم أن الوجود ليس بجنس فلم يخرج عن كونه عاما شاملا للقسمين ؛ إذ لولا شموله وإلا لما صح التقسيم، فإن التقسيم إنما يرد على المعنى لا على مجرد اللفظة، فالمعنى العام للواجب والجائز غير المعنى الخاص بأحد القسمين، فإذا لم يكن التركيب تركبا من جنس وفصل كان تركبا من عام وخاص، فيفيد أحدهما من التصور ما لا يفيده الآخر، فيلزم منه عين ما يلزم من الفصل والجنس.
Then we say: Even if it is granted to you that existence is not a genus, it does not cease to be a general concept encompassing the two divisions; for if not for its comprehensiveness, then the division would not be valid. Indeed, division only applies to the meaning, not to the mere word. So the general meaning of necessity and possibility is different from the special meaning of one of the two divisions. If the composition is not a composition of genus and differentia, then it is a composition of general and special, and one of them conveys in conception what the other does not, and the same thing follows from it as follows from differentia and genus.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the previous argument. The author assumes (for the sake of argument) that existence is not a genus (jins). He argues that even so, existence remains a general concept (am) that encompasses the two divisions (necessity and possibility). Without this comprehensiveness, the division would be invalid. He points out that division is based on meaning (ma'na), not on the mere word (lafz). The general meaning of existence common to both necessary and possible beings is different from the specific meaning of either. Therefore, when we say 'necessary being' or 'possible being', the relationship between the general concept (existence) and the specific concept (necessity/possibility) is analogous to that between genus and differentia: one adds to the other in conception. Thus, the same logical consequences follow as in a genus-differentia composition, even if existence is not technically a genus.

Key Terms
جنس (jins)
فصل (fasl)
عام (am)
خاص (khass)
تقسيم (taqsim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if existence is not a genus, it cannot function as a basis for dividing beings into necessary and contingent.
Correction: The author shows that a general-special relationship is sufficient for division, and it yields the same conceptual benefit as genus-differentia.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the composition of existence with necessity or possibility?
A) It is a composition of genus and differentia
B) It is a composition of general and special that yields a similar benefit
C) It is invalid because existence is not a genus
D) It is a composition of two general concepts

Key Takeaway
Even if existence is not a genus, its role as a general concept enables meaningful division between necessary and possible beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the Necessary Being (God), do you merely label Him as 'existent' without reflecting on the unique necessity that sets His existence apart from all possibilities? How can you deepen your conceptual understanding to avoid reducing God to a category?
?
Are there truths in your faith that you have reduced to empty phrases, merely repeating them without grasping their weight? What would it take for you to dwell on the meaning behind the words, as the passage insists division must be based on meaning?
?
The passage shows that even a general concept adds something to conception. In your daily life, how often do you skim over general truths without letting them transform your specific actions? Commit to one general truth today and let it shape a specific decision.

Abstraction Determines Intellection
Explains how abstraction from matter determines the degree of intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the degree of abstraction from matter determines the level of intellection and why the First Intellect, though fully abstracted, is still inferior to the First Cause.

Core Idea
Abstraction from matter is the measure of intellectual perfection.

This passage from Imam al-Ash'ari's Nihayat al-Aqdam explores a fundamental principle: the more a being is abstracted from matter, the greater its capacity for intellection. Yet the author adds a crucial nuance—even the highest created intellect, the First Intellect, remains contingent and thus below the Creator. Let's examine how abstraction grades the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read and Identify the Principle
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the opening statement: every intellect or soul perceives according to its degree of abstraction from matter. This is the core principle.
This principle applies universally, not just to God.
2
Trace the Gradation
Observe the spectrum: from celestial bodies (subtle matter) to terrestrial bodies (dense matter), and finally to the First Intellect (completely immaterial). Notice how the 'veil' of matter affects perception.
The highest degree is complete abstraction, but even there a limitation remains.
3
Understand the Limitation of the First Intellect
The passage ends with a key point: the First Intellect, though fully abstracted, is still possible in existence (contingent). Thus its intellection does not reach the perfection of the First Cause (God).
Possibility implies a kind of privation, which is still a material-like limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعقول، وليس يختص ذلك بتعقل الباري تعالى بل كل عقل ونفسي إذا تجرد عن المادة عقل وأدرك بمقدار تجرده، ثم ذات العقل الأول لا يحتجب عن ذاته أبدا، فهو عاقل بنفسه أبدا، وكل عقل ونفس مفارق للمادة فحكمه كذلك، إلا أن من المواد ما يكون في ألطف ما يكون كالأجسام السماوية، فكان حجابها لعقولها ونفوسها أقل تأثيرًا إلى أن يصل في الدرجة العليا إلى ما لا يخالط مادة البتة، فيكون أكمل الأشياء تعقلا وإدراكًا، ومن المواد ما يكون في أكثف ما يكون كالأجسام الأرضية فكان حجابها لإدراكاتها أكثر تأثيرًا إلى أن ينزل في الدرجة السفلى إلى ما لا يفارق مادة البتة، فيكون أكثر الأشياء خمودًا وجمودا، والعقل الأول وإن فارق المادة من كل وجه إلا أن ذاته باعتبار ذاته ممكن الوجود، فلم يخرج عن الإمكان وطبيعة الإمكان عدمية مادية لم يصل تعقله إلى درجة العلة الأولى؛ إذ هو أجل الأشياء إدراكًا لا أجل الأشياء كمالا، إذ هو برئ عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم.
The intelligible, and that is not specific to the intellection of the Exalted Creator, but rather every intellect and soul, when it becomes abstracted from matter, intellects and perceives according to the extent of its abstraction. Then the essence of the First Intellect is never veiled from itself, so it is always intellect of itself by itself. And every intellect and soul separate from matter has the same rule, except that among matters, some are in the subtlest state, like celestial bodies, so their veil for their intellects and souls has less effect, until it reaches the highest degree to that which never mixes with matter at all, so it becomes the most perfect of things in intellection and perception. And among matters, some are in the densest state, like terrestrial bodies, so their veil for their perceptions has more effect, until it descends to the lowest degree to that which never separates from matter at all, so it becomes the most inert and stagnant of things. And the First Intellect, although it is separate from matter in every respect, yet its essence considered by itself is possible of existence, so it does not exit from possibility, and the nature of possibility is privative/material. Its intellection does not reach the degree of the First Cause, for it is the most excellent of things in perception, not the most excellent of things in perfection, since it is free from the nature of possibility and privation.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between abstraction from matter and the degree of intellection. The author begins by stating that it is not only the Creator who intellects when abstracted; any intellect or soul, when it becomes abstracted from matter, intellects and perceives according to its degree of abstraction. Next, the author describes the First Intellect, whose essence is never veiled from itself, so it is always intellect of itself by itself. All intellects and souls separate from matter follow the same rule, but there are gradations: celestial bodies are in the subtlest state of matter, so their veil has less effect on their intellects and souls, leading to the highest degree where matter is completely absent, resulting in the most perfect intellection. Conversely, terrestrial bodies are in the densest state, so their veil has more effect, descending to the lowest degree where matter is never separated, resulting in the most inert and stagnant state. Finally, the author notes that the First Intellect, though fully separate from matter, is still possible in its essence, so its intellection does not reach the degree of the First Cause (God). The First Intellect is the most excellent in perception but not in perfection, since it is free from the nature of possibility and privation, implying that God is beyond even that.

تجرد
مادة
عقل أول
إمكان
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All abstracted intellects are equal in perfection because they are immaterial.
Correction: The passage shows that degrees of abstraction vary: even the First Intellect, though fully abstracted, is still contingent and thus less perfect than the First Cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the degree of intellection relate to abstraction from matter?
A) They are unrelated.
B) The more abstracted from matter, the greater the intellection.
C) The more material, the greater the intellection.
D) They are inversely proportional.

Key Takeaway
Abstraction from matter is the key determinant of intellectual power, but even the highest created intellect remains contingent and thus below the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that attachment to matter veils your intellect. What is one material attachment or worldly distraction that you know is clouding your ability to perceive truth clearly? What concrete step can you take today to detach from it, even for a few minutes?
?
The First Intellect, despite its high rank, recognizes its contingency and dependence. When was the last time you genuinely felt your absolute need for Allah in every moment? How can you cultivate that awareness in your daily routine starting tomorrow?
?
The passage distinguishes between being 'most excellent in perception' and 'most excellent in perfection.' In your pursuit of knowledge or spirituality, are you more driven by the desire to appear knowledgeable or to attain true inner perfection? What change can you make to prioritize the latter?

Why Negative Differentiae Cannot Be Essential
Shahrastani refutes negative differentiae as essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative differentiae cannot serve as essential differentiae in logic and how this applies to the attribute of 'necessity of existence' in discussions about God.

Core Idea
Essential differentiae must be positive: negation alone cannot define.

This passage presents a logical objection against the use of negative attributes to define essences. Shahrastani refutes the claim that negation can serve as an essential differentia, grounding his argument in the nature of definition. Understanding this helps clarify why divine attributes like 'necessity of existence' cannot be purely negative.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the opening objection and the refutation, noting the structure: the objector claims negation can specify a thing without causing multiplicity.
The objection is a common argument from the philosophers.
2
Understand the logical requirement for a differentia
Focus on the proof: a differentia requires a prior shared genus (more general attribute) and must be the most specific positive attribute of the thing.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Examine the historical mistake
Learn why some philosophers, unable to express essential differentiae positively, introduced negative ones, leading to the error of treating 'necessity of existence' as a negative attribute.
This connects the logical point to theology.
4
Reflect on the application to divine attributes
Consider how this critique applies to the claim that 'necessity of existence' is a negative differentia distinguishing God from contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: قد يتخصص الشيء بالفصول السلبية والسلب لا يوجب الاثنينية. قيل: هذا خطأ وقع للمنطقيين منكم؛ حيث ظنوا أن السلب يجوز أن يكون فصلا ذاتيا. والبرهان على استحالة ذلك أن الفصل لا يتحقق ما لم يتحقق في المفصول اشتراك ما في أعم وصف فيكون الفصل؛ أعني ما به تميز عن غيره من المشتركات أخص وصف الشيء، والشيء إنما يتميز عن غيره بأخص وصفه وسلب صفة، وشيء آخر عنه، وبما يطلقه على أخص وصفه لا أن يكون نفسه أخص وصفه. فلما ضاق على بعض الفلاسفة التعبير عن الفصول الذاتية بعبارات ناصة عليها أوردوا فصولاً سلبية على أن تطلع الطالب على نفس المطلوب، ظن بعضهم أن السلوب تصلح أن تكون فصولا ذاتية، وذلك خطأ بين، ثم ارتكبوا على مقتضى ذلك أن جعلوا وجوب الوجود أمرًا سلبيا، وفصلوا به ذات واجب الوجود عن سائر
If it is said: A thing may be specified by negative differentiae, and negation does not necessitate duality. It is said: This is an error that occurred to the logicians among you, as they thought that negation could be an essential differentia. The proof of the impossibility of that is that the differentia is not realized unless there is realized in the thing differentiated a certain sharing in a more general attribute; so the differentia—that by which it is distinguished from others among the shared attributes—is the most specific attribute of the thing. And a thing is only distinguished from others by its most specific attribute, and by the negation of an attribute from something else about it. And by what one applies to its most specific attribute, not that it itself is its most specific attribute. When some philosophers found it difficult to express essential differentiae with precise expressions, they introduced negative differentiae, aiming to acquaint the student with the very thing sought. Some of them thought that privations (al-salub) could be essential differentiae, and that is clearly an error. Then they proceeded, based on that, to make the necessity of existence a negative matter, and they differentiated the essence of the Necessary Being from other [essences] by it.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a historical note. The objector claims that things can be specified by negative differentiae (al-fusul al-salbiyya), and negation does not necessitate duality (thus God's attributes could be negative without multiplicity). The respondent refutes this by explaining the logical requirement for an essential differentia: a differentia can only exist if there is a prior shared more general attribute; the differentia is the most specific positive attribute of the thing. A thing is distinguished by its most specific attribute, not by a negation. The author then notes that some philosophers, finding it hard to express essential differentiae positively, resorted to negative differentiae, mistakenly thinking privations can be essential. They then wrongly made 'necessity of existence' a negative attribute and used it to distinguish the Necessary Being. This critique targets the philosophers' (falasifa) logical approach.

Key Terms
فصل سلبي
فصل ذاتي
وجوب الوجود
أخص وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A negative description (e.g., 'not contingent') can serve as an essential definition or differentia.
Correction: An essential differentia must be the most specific positive attribute that distinguishes the thing from others sharing a genus; negation cannot fulfill this because it does not provide positive content.

Checkpoint
Why can a negative differentia not be an essential differentia?
A) Because negation is always ambiguous.
B) Because the differentia must be the most specific positive attribute of the thing.
C) Because negation implies multiplicity.
D) Because negation is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
Essential differentiae must be positive and most specific; negation cannot define essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God in your daily life, how often do you rely on negations (e.g., 'He is not limited') rather than positive affirmations? What is one positive attribute of God you can actively learn and use this week to transform your worship?
?
Think of a time you defined yourself by what you are not (e.g., 'I am not like them'). How does Shahrastani's insight about negative differentiae apply to your own identity? What would it mean to define yourself by your most specific positive attributes instead?
?
The philosophers' mistake about wujub al-wujud affected their entire understanding of God. Is there a negative belief about God that you hold without realizing it? What would change if you replaced that negation with a positive, essential description from the Quran or Sunnah?

A Proposed Classification of Intellects: Imprinting and Imprinted
An objection proposes a taxonomy of intellects to resolve the knowledge problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proposed taxonomy of intellects (imprinting/imprinted/neither) and its parallel in tasawwur/taswir, as an objection to Ibn Sina's earlier definition of intellect.

Core Idea
Not all intellects work the same way—some imprint, some are imprinted, some do neither.

Ibn Sina had argued that intellect is simply the imprinting of one essence by another. But an objector proposes a more nuanced classification to avoid contradictions. This passage presents a taxonomy that distinguishes different types of intellection, thereby challenging the original formulation. Let's examine this objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument.
Notice that the passage begins with a claim by Ibn Sina, then an objection.
2
Identify the three categories in the first classification
The objector proposes that among intellects, some are imprinted, some imprint, and some do neither. Find where these categories are applied to specific intellects.
Examples: God's intellection of the First Intellect is imprinting; His intellection of His essence is neither; the Intellect and Soul are imprinted.
3
Understand the second classification using tasawwur and taswir
The objector then uses 'representation' (tasawwur) and 'depicting' (taswir) to further categorize intellects. Note how our intellects are representations, the conjunctions are both, and the Creator's intellection of His essence is neither.
This parallels the first classification but with different terminology.
4
Reflect on how this taxonomy addresses the earlier problem
Ask yourself: Why does this classification avoid the refutation of Ibn Sina's earlier formulation? How does it preserve God's knowledge without reducing it to a passive imprint?
The key is that God's intellectual acts are active (imprinting, depicting) or beyond both, not passive reception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا : إنه تعالى عالم بالأشياء لا من الأشياء، بل الأشياء منه ومن علمه. قيل له : فإذا بطل تقريرك الثاني أن العقل لا معنى له إلا الارتسام ماهية بماهية ، فهلا قلت : العقل رسم ماهية في ماهية ، أو هلا قسمت الأمر فقلت : من العقول ما يرتسم ، ومن العقول ما يرسم ومنها ما لا يرسم ولا يرتسم، فعقله للعقل الأول رسم وليس بارتسام وعقله لذاته ليس برسم ولا ارتسام، وعقل العقل والنفس له ارتسام وليس برسم، فعقولنا مثلا تصورات وعقول المقارنات تصويرات وتصورات وعقل الباري تعالى لذاته ليس بتصور ولا تصوير، وعقله للعقل الأول تصوير لا تصور.
Ibn Sina said: He, exalted, is knowledgeable of things not from things, but things are from Him and from His knowledge. It was said to him: Since your second formulation [that intellect is nothing but the imprinting of an essence by an essence] has been refuted, why did you not say: Intellect is the imprinting of an essence in an essence? Or why did you not divide the matter and say: Among intellects, some are imprinted, some are imprinting, and some neither imprint nor are imprinted? Thus His intellection of the First Intellect is imprinting and not being imprinted; His intellection of His essence is neither imprinting nor being imprinted; and the intellection of the Intellect and the Soul is being imprinted and not imprinting. So for example, our intellects are representations (tasa[ww]urat), and the intellects of the conjunctions (al-muqaranat) are depictings (taswirat) and representations, while the intellection of the Creator of His essence is neither a representation nor a depicting, and His intellection of the First Intellect is a depicting not a representation.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to Ibn Sina's earlier claim that intellect is merely the imprinting of one essence by another. The objector suggests that a more nuanced classification is possible. He proposes that some intellects imprint (like God's intellection of the First Intellect), some are imprinted (like the intellection of the Intellect and Soul), and some do neither (like God's intellection of His own essence). The objector then elaborates: our human intellects are 'representations' (tasawwurat) that receive forms; the intellects of the conjunctions (possibly celestial intellects) both depict and represent; the Creator's intellection of His essence is neither representation nor depiction; and His intellection of the First Intellect is a depiction only, not a representation. This classification attempts to show that different types of intellection exist, thereby avoiding the strict dichotomy that led to the previous problems.

Key Terms
عقل
رسم
ارتسام
تصور
تصوير
عقل أول
عقل العقل والنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All intellects work the same way, either all imprinting or all being imprinted.
Correction: This passage shows that different intellects (divine, celestial, human) have different modes of intellection: some are active, some passive, some both, some neither.

Checkpoint
According to the proposed classification, what type of intellection does the Creator have of His own essence?
A) Imprinting only.
B) Being imprinted only.
C) Neither imprinting nor being imprinted.
D) Both imprinting and being imprinted.

Key Takeaway
This objection shows that a more nuanced classification of intellectual acts can rescue Ibn Sina's system from contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that your own way of knowing—through forming mental images or concepts—is the only way? How does this lesson challenge you to accept that God's knowledge is beyond your intellectual categories?
?
When you think about God's self-knowledge, do you secretly imagine it as a kind of self-awareness like your own? How can you train yourself to affirm that His knowledge is neither representation nor depiction?
?
Are you willing to let go of the need to fit God into a human framework of understanding, even when it feels uncomfortable or abstract? What will you do this week to remind yourself that He is beyond all mental images?

Evil as Privation and the Impossibility of Absolute Evil
Defines evil as privation and argues absolute evil has no existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam view that evil is not a positive entity but a privation, and why absolute evil cannot exist.

Core Idea
Evil is the absence of perfection, not a thing that exists.

This passage from Sanusi's Nihayat al-Aqdam tackles a deep theological question: what is the nature of evil? The author argues that evil is not a positive reality but rather the lack of certain perfections. This distinction is crucial for defending God's goodness and power, as it shows that evil is not a created entity but a deficiency. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the concise kalam style.
2
Focus on the definition of evil in itself
Identify the statement: 'evil in itself is non-existence'. This is the foundational claim.
3
Understand the qualification of non-existence
The author specifies that not every non-existence is evil, but only the lack of what perfects a thing's nature.
This distinguishes metaphysical evil from mere absence.
4
Follow the argument against absolute evil
Trace the conditional reasoning: if absolute evil existed, it would have to exist, and existence is good, so it wouldn't be purely evil.
Key step: existence entails goodness.
5
Reflect on the implication for divine transcendence
Consider how this argument protects God from being the creator of evil as a positive entity.
Evil is not created; it is only privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزنا والسرقة، وبالجملة الشر بالذات هو العدم، ولا كل عدم بل عدم منقص طباع الشيء من الكمالات الثابتة النوعية والطبيعية، والشر المطلق لا وجود له، وكذلك الشر بالذات ليس بأمر حاصل إلا أن يخبر عن لفظه، ولو كان له حصول لكان شراً مطلقاً عاماً ذاتياً موجوداً، وإذا تحقق له وجود فقد حصلت فيه خيرية من جهة وجوده؛ إذ الوجود من حيث هو وجود خير فتحقق أن الشر المطلق لا وجود له إلا في اللفظ والذهن والوجود على كماله الأقصى أن يكون بالفعل وليس فيه أمر ما بالقوة أصلا، فلا يلحقه شر.
And adultery and theft, and in sum, evil in itself is non-existence, but not every non-existence, rather it is the non-existence of what is proper (that which perfects) the nature of a thing from the specific and natural perfections. And absolute evil has no existence. Likewise, evil in itself is not an actual thing except that one speaks of its word. And if it had actuality, it would be absolute, universal, essential, existing evil. And if existence is realized for it, then some goodness has occurred in it by virtue of its existence; since existence insofar as it is existence is good. So it is established that absolute evil has no existence except in utterance and the mind. And existence in its utmost perfection is to be actual and not have any potentiality in it at all, so evil does not attach to it.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof regarding the nature of evil. The author begins by stating that evil in itself (al-sharr bi al-dhat) is non-existence (al-‘adam), but he immediately qualifies this: not any non-existence, but the privation of the perfections that are proper to a thing's nature—both specific and natural. Absolute evil (al-sharr al-mutlaq) therefore has no existence, because if it did, it would be an existing thing, and existence as such is good. Hence absolute evil is merely a word or a mental concept. The author then adds that existence in its most perfect form is purely actual, containing no potentiality, and such existence is immune to evil.

Key Terms
الشر بالذات
العدم
الشر المطلق
الوجود
الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think evil is a positive entity with independent existence.
Correction: The author clarifies that evil is only privation, not a positive being.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does absolute evil have no existence?
A) Because evil is always accidental in nature.
B) Because if it existed, it would contain goodness from the aspect of existence.
C) Because evil is synonymous with matter.
D) Because God does not create evil.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not a positive reality but the absence of specific perfections; absolute evil is impossible because existence itself is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil is merely the absence of good, then when you sin, what good are you neglecting? What specific perfection are you failing to actualize in that moment, and what will you do today to pursue it instead?
?
You have witnessed or experienced profound evil. Has your belief that it is only a lack of good made you passive or more active in seeking good? Which response does the truth require, and what step will you take right now to cultivate good where evil has reigned?
?
Absolute evil does not exist, yet you fear certain evils as if they were ultimate. What fear is controlling you, and how does knowing that existence itself is good disarm that fear? What concrete act of trust in the Good will you perform today?

The Homonymy of 'Kalam'
Explains the homonymous nature of 'kalam' and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'kalam' is homonymous between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila and how this prevents genuine agreement about divine speech.

Core Idea
'Kalam' is a homonym; both sects talk past each other.

The Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya appear to debate divine speech, but a closer look reveals they use the word 'kalam' in different senses. This passage exposes the semantic confusion that underlies the disagreement, showing that the opponents cannot genuinely argue about the attribute itself—they only pose objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the conditional statement at the beginning.
2
Identify the conditional scenario
The passage begins with 'if our opponents agreed...' What would happen if they agreed? Observe the two possibilities.
The conditional reveals the key to the argument.
3
Understand the consequence of homonymy
The passage states that because 'kalam' is homonymous, the opponents cannot truly debate; they only raise objections and difficulties (ilzam and irad al-ishkal).
This explains the impasse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصومنا لو وافقونا على أن الكلام في الشاهد معنى في النفس سوى العبارات القائمة باللسان، وأن الكلام في الغائب معنى قائم بذات الباري تعالى سوى العبارات التي نقرؤها باللسان ونكتبها في المصاحف، لوافقونا على اتحاد المعنى، لكن لما لفظاً مشتركا في الإطلاق لم يتوارد على محل واحد فإن ما يثبته كلامًا فالأشعرية تثبته وتوافقه على أنه كثير وأنه محدث مخلوق، وما يثبته الأشعري كلاما فالخصم ينكره أصلا، فكيف يصح منه كلامه في وحدته، وكونه أزليا قديما، ولكن ليس يتكلم الخصم في هذه المسألة إلا على سبيل الإلزام وإيراد الإشكال.
And if our opponents agreed with us that speech (al-kalam) in the witnessed world is a meaning in the soul (maʿnan fī al-nafs) apart from the expressions that occur on the tongue, and that speech in the unseen (al-ghaʾib) is a meaning subsisting in the essence of the Exalted God (maʿnan qāʾim bi-dhāt al-Bārī taʿālā) apart from the expressions we recite with the tongue and write in the musḥaf, they would agree with us on the unity of the meaning. However, because it is a homonymous expression (lafẓan mushtrakan) in its application, it does not apply to one thing. For what they affirm as speech, the Ashʿariyya affirm it and agree with them that it is multiple (kathīr) and that it is created (muḥdath makhluq). But what al-Ashʿarī affirms as speech, the opponent denies it entirely. So how can he (the opponent) correctly speak about its unity and its being eternal? However, the opponent does not speak about this issue except by way of obligation (ilzām) and posing a problem (īrād al-ishkāl).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage clarifies the methodological impasse between the Ashʿariyya and the Muʿtazila regarding divine speech. It states that if the opponents (al-khuṣūm, i.e., the Muʿtazila) agreed with the Ashʿari conception of speech as a meaning in the soul for the witnessed and as a meaning subsisting in God's essence for the unseen, they would agree on the unity of that meaning. However, the term 'kalam' is homonymous (lafẓ mushtarak): what the Muʿtazila mean by it—multiple, created expressions—is something the Ashʿariyya also affirm as created speech, but what al-Ashʿarī means by it—the eternal attribute—is denied by the Muʿtazila. Therefore, the Muʿtazila cannot genuinely argue about the unity of the divine attribute; they only pose difficulties and objections (ilzām and īrād al-ishkāl) without actual agreement on the subject matter.

Key Terms
الكلام
معنى في النفس
لفظ مشترك
الإلزام
إيراد الإشكال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari and Mu'tazila are debating the same thing about divine speech.
Correction: They use 'kalam' in different senses, so their arguments do not engage with the same subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Mu'tazila argue about God's speech?
A) By agreeing on the meaning of 'kalam'.
B) By way of ilzam and irad al-ishkal.
C) By affirming the eternal attribute.
D) By accepting the Ash'ari definition.

Key Takeaway
The term 'kalam' is homonymous, so the debate between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila about divine speech is not about the same subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that the opponent in the debate only engaged in ilzām and īrād al-ishkāl—forcing contradictions and posing problems—rather than seeking truth. When you discuss theological matters, are you more concerned with winning the argument or understanding the other person's position? What would change if you approached every disagreement as an opportunity to define terms first? Commit today to ask, 'What do you mean by that word?' before debating.
?
The passage says that if both sides agreed on the definition of 'kalam', they would agree on the unity of meaning. Is there a belief you hold that you have never questioned simply because you assumed everyone uses the same terms you do? Identify one theological term you use frequently (e.g., 'tawheed', 'sunna', 'sincerity') and ask yourself: Am I certain that the people I discuss it with define it the same way? What would it cost you to clarify before assuming agreement?
?
The term 'kalam' is homonymous, causing people to talk past each other. Think about a recent conversation where you felt frustrated because someone didn't 'get' your point. Could it be that you were using the same word but meaning different things? What would it look like for you to humbly admit that you might not even be addressing the same subject? Write down one step you will take this week to listen more carefully to the definitions others use, even if you think they are wrong.

Answering the Objection from al-Hal: Condition vs. Coexistence
The author distinguishes between a condition (musahhih) and actual contradictory attributes in responding to the objection from 'al-hal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a condition (musahhih) that enables opposites and the actual coexistence of opposites, and how this answers the objection from 'al-hal'.

Core Idea
A condition for opposites does not force them to coexist.

In responding to the objection from 'al-hal', the author clarifies a crucial distinction: the condition that makes contradictory attributes possible is not the same as the actual existence of those contradictories in a single subject. Using the analogy of life, he shows how a single condition can enable opposites without requiring them to coexist. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize that the objector claimed that if a single state (hal) can be a condition for contradictory attributes, then divine speech could be both command and prohibition. This sets up the problem.
2
Grasp the distinction
The author distinguishes 'musahhih' (condition) from the actual existence of contradictories. A condition enables opposites but does not require them to coexist.
This is the core of the answer.
3
Examine the life analogy
Life is a condition for both knowledge and ignorance, but a living being does not possess both at once. This shows that conditions and actual coexistence are separate.
4
Apply to the divine case
When the author posits a 'hal' as a condition, it only enables; it does not force contradictory attributes like command and prohibition to exist together in God's speech.
Thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إلزام الحال فالجواب عنه أن المصحح للمختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير وثبوت المختلفات المتضادات لشيء واحد غير، فإن الحياة مصححة للعلم والجهل والقدرة والعجز والإرادة والكراهة إلى غير ذلك من المعاني التي يشترط في ثبوتها الحياة، ولا يلزم ذلك ثبوت المختلفات المتضادات لحي واحد، ونحن إذا أثبتنا حالا فهي مصححة أو في حكم المصحح، ولم نثبت مختلفات متضادة لشيء واحد، فهذا هو الفرق.
As for the objection from al-ḥāl (state), the answer is that the condition (muṣaḥḥiḥ) for contradictory things for one thing is other than the existence of contradictory things for one thing. Indeed, life is a condition for knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity, will and aversion, and other meanings whose existence requires life. But that does not necessitate the existence of contradictory things for one living being. When we posit a state (ḥāl), it is a condition or in the position of a condition, and we do not posit contradictory things for one thing. This is the difference.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objector argued that if a single attribute (like life) can be a condition for both knowledge and ignorance (contradictories), then why cannot a single divine speech be both command and prohibition? The author distinguishes: the condition (muṣaḥḥiḥ) that makes contradictory attributes possible in a subject is different from the actual existence of those contradictories in that subject. For example, life is a necessary condition for both knowledge and ignorance, but a living being cannot possess both simultaneously. Likewise, the author says that if they posit a 'state' (ḥāl) as a condition, it only enables, but does not imply that both contradictories actually exist. Therefore, the objection fails: even if life allows both knowledge and ignorance, it does not force them to coexist. In the divine case, positing a single speech attribute does not force it to be both command and prohibition in reality.

Key Terms
حال
مصحح
حياة
اختلاف
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A condition that enables opposites necessarily leads to the actual coexistence of those opposites in the same subject.
Correction: The passage shows that a condition (like life) can enable opposites without requiring their simultaneous existence; the condition and the actual coexistence are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the function of life (ḥayāt) in the author's analogy?
A) It causes both knowledge and ignorance to exist together.
B) It is a condition that enables opposing attributes but does not necessitate their simultaneous existence.
C) It is an attribute of God that explains His speech.
D) It is a proof that divine speech is single.

Key Takeaway
A condition enabling opposites does not force them to actually coexist; this distinction undercuts objections based on the theory of states (ahwal).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because something is possible for God, He must do it? How does the distinction between condition and actuality in this lesson challenge that assumption, and how can you apply it to your trust in God's wisdom?
?
Think of a time when you confused having the ability to do something (condition) with actually doing it (actuality). How did that affect your actions? What will you do today to act on what you truly know, not just what is possible?
?
This lesson shows that life enables both knowledge and ignorance. Are you using the life God gave you to pursue knowledge or to remain in ignorance? Write one concrete step you will take today to increase your beneficial knowledge.

Reduction of All Speech to One Type
Author reduces all speech types to one unified speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians reduce all forms of divine speech to a single, unified attribute.

Core Idea
All divine speech reduces to one: command, report, and prohibition are ultimately the same.

This passage presents a fundamental argument in Islamic theology: that the apparent diversity of divine speech (command, prohibition, report) does not imply multiple attributes. Instead, the author demonstrates a logical reduction from many forms to a single, unified speech. Follow along as the argument unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author moves from specific terms (disapproval, prohibition) to broader categories (command, report) and finally to one.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Trace the reduction steps
Identify each reduction: from disapproval/prohibition to command/prohibition, then to command/report, and finally to a single speech. Ask: what justifies each step?
The author uses the logic that prohibition is a command not to do.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the author insists speech can be one. How does this support the oneness of God's attributes?
This is part of the broader Ash'ari argument against multiplicity in divine essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمي تكريها، وإن ورد سمي تحريما، ثم هما يشتركان في كونهما أمرا ونهيا، وإن الأمر والنهي إلى معنى واحد وهو الأمر كان ذلك أيضا له وجه، فإن النهي أمر بأن لا تفعل فرجع أقسام الكلام كلها إلى خبر وأمر، ثم كما أمكن رد أقسام الكلام إلى قسمين من غير نقصان في حقيقة الكلام، كذلك أمكن رد القسمين إلى قسم واحد حتى يكون كلامه على ما قررناه واحدا.
It is named disapproval, and if it appears it is named prohibition. Then they both share in being command and prohibition. And if command and prohibition are reduced to one meaning, which is command, that also has a justification. For prohibition is a command not to do. So all divisions of speech reduce to report and command. Then just as it was possible to reduce the divisions of speech to two divisions without any deficiency in the reality of speech, likewise it was possible to reduce the two divisions to one division so that His speech, as we have established, is one.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an argument for reducing all types of speech to a single kind, thereby establishing the oneness of divine speech. The author begins by noting that disapproval and prohibition are both commands, so all commands reduce to command, and all speech reduces to either command or report. He then states that even these two can be reduced to one, because a command is essentially a report that something should be done. The point is that divine speech, despite its many forms in revelation, is one unified attribute.

The Philosophers' Definition of Speech: Outer and Inner
Defines two meanings of speech: articulated words and inner psychological processes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' twofold definition of speech as both external articulated words and internal psychological processes, and how inner speech relates to logical reasoning.

Core Idea
For philosophers, speech is both outer words and inner reasoning.

The philosophers take a broader view of speech than common understanding. They distinguish between the speech of the tongue—audible, articulated language—and inner speech, which includes rational discrimination, thinking, and imagination. This passage from Nihayat al-Aqdam explains both meanings and shows how inner speech relates to logical deduction. It sets the stage for understanding how human speech differs from divine speech in later theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview. Notice the two main definitions presented.
The passage is dense; take time to absorb each part.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
Identify the first meaning (outer speech: articulated letters and sounds) and the second meaning (inner mental states: rational discrimination, thinking, imagination).
3
Understand inner speech characteristics
Note how inner speech is considered either intellectually (as correct discrimination) or psychologically (as thinking and deliberation).
The passage mentions the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) linking to syllogism.
4
Connect to logic
The mention of the middle term shows that inner speech is the basis for logical reasoning and discovering proofs.
This highlights the philosophical view of speech as rational activity.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider why philosophers include inner processes as speech—this expands the concept beyond mere vocalization.
Contrast with common understanding that speech is only audible words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فالنطق عندهم يطلق على نطق اللسان، وهو حروف منظومة بأصوات مقطعة في مخارج مخصوصة نظما يعبر عن المعنى الذي في النفس بحكم الاصطلاح والمواضعة أو بحكم التوقيف والمصادرة ويطلق النطق على التمييز العقلي والتفكير النفساني والتصوير الخيالي ، وهو معان في ذهن الإنسان مختلفة الاعتبار، فإن اعتبرت بمجرد العقل كان تمييزاً صحيحًا بين الحق والباطل، ويلزمه أن يكون معاني كلية مجردة متحدة متفقة، فإن اعتبرت بمجرد النفس كان تفكيراً وترديدا بين الحق والباطل حتى يظفر بالحد الأوسط، فيطلع على الدليل المرشد والعلة والسبب، ويلزمها أن تكون ذاتية كلية أو جزئية بسيطة أو مركبة، ويلزمها أن تكون ذاتية أو عرضية موجبة أو سالبة موجهة أو مطلقة يقينية أو غير يقينية
As for the philosophers, 'speech' (al-nuṭq) in their view applies to the speech of the tongue (nuṭq al-lisān), which is arranged letters with segmented sounds from specific articulatory positions, arranged to express the meaning in the soul by convention and agreement (al-iṣṭilāḥ wa al-muwāḍaʿa) or by prescriptive institution and stipulation (al-tawqīf wa al-muṣādara). And speech applies to rational discrimination (al-tamyīz al-ʿaqlī), psychological thinking (al-tafkīr al-nafsānī), and imaginative representation (al-taṣwīr al-khayālī). They are meanings in the human mind of different considerations. If they are considered by the intellect alone, they are correct discrimination between truth and falsehood, and it entails that they be universal, abstract, unified, and consistent meanings. If they are considered by the soul alone, they are thinking and deliberation between truth and falsehood until one hits upon the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ), thereby arriving at the guiding proof, the cause, and the reason, and it entails that they be essential, universal or particular, simple or composite, and it entails that they be essential or accidental, affirmative or negative, modal or absolute, certain or uncertain.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage classifies the philosophers' definition of 'speech' (al-nuṭq) into two main types. First, speech of the tongue (nuṭq al-lisān): this is audible, articulated language—ordered letters with distinct sounds produced from specific places of articulation. This speech conveys meaning that is in the soul, and its relation to meaning is based either on convention (iṣṭilāḥ) and agreement (muwāḍaʿa) or on divine institution (tawqīf) and stipulation (muṣādara). Second, speech also applies to inner psychological processes: rational discrimination (al-tamyīz al-ʿaqlī), psychological thinking (al-tafkīr al-nafsānī), and imaginative representation (al-taṣwīr al-khayālī). These are meanings in the human mind that can be considered in two ways. When considered purely intellectually, they are the correct discrimination between truth and falsehood, and they necessarily take the form of universal, abstract, unified, and consistent meanings. When considered purely psychologically, they are thinking and deliberation (taraddud) between truth and falsehood, carried out until one discovers the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) of a syllogism, which then reveals the guiding proof, cause, and reason. These psychological meanings necessarily have certain characteristics: they can be essential or accidental, universal or particular, simple or composite, affirmative or negative, modal or absolute, certain or uncertain. The passage thus provides a comprehensive philosophical definition of speech encompassing both external expression and internal reasoning.

Key Terms
نطق
لسان
تمييز عقلي
تفكير نفساني
تصوير خيالي
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking speech refers only to audible words.
Correction: Philosophers include inner mental processes like rational discrimination and thinking as speech.

Checkpoint

D) The meaning of words alone.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define speech broadly to include both external language and internal cognitive processes essential for logical reasoning.

The philosophers consider even imaginative representation as speech. Have you ever pondered that your daydreams and fantasies are speech before Allah? What will you do to purify your imagination so that it aligns with truth?

The Necessity and Distinctness of Internal Speech
Shahrastani proves internal speech is self-evident and distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shahrastani proves the reality of internal speech (ḥadīth al-nafs) as a distinct faculty known by necessity, separate from knowledge and will.

Core Idea
Internal speech is a universal necessity, not a metaphor for knowledge or will.

Shahrastani begins by appealing to everyday human experience — every craftsman engages in silent deliberation before acting. He argues that to deny this inner dialogue is to reject self-evident truth and join the Sophists. The passage then carefully distinguishes internal speech from the faculties of knowledge (ʿilm) and will (irāda), showing that each has a distinct function. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the vivid imagery of the craftsman and the strong language against skeptics.
2
Identify the argument for necessity
Focus on the craftsman example and the claim that denial is sophistry. Understand why Shahrastani considers this knowledge self-evident.
He uses the term 'ḍarūra' (necessity) — meaning it is impossible to deny sincerely.
3
Understand the distinction from knowledge
Read how knowledge is described as 'pure representation' without mood, while internal speech includes questions, commands, etc.
This is a key conceptual difference.
4
Understand the distinction from will
Note that will involves intention to select among possibilities, whereas internal speech lacks such intention.
This further clarifies the unique nature of internal speech.
5
Reflect on the dream proof
Consider the argument that even in sleep, internal speech persists, sometimes manifesting in mumbled words. This reinforces its constant presence.
This serves as a powerful empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بترديد الفكر أنه هل يجوز أن يكون الشيء كذا أو يستحيل أو يجب؟ إلى غير ذلك من الأفكار حتى أن كل صانع يحدث مع نفسه أولا بالغرض الذي توجهت إليه صنعته، ثم ينطق نفسه في حال الفعل محادثة مع الآلات والأدوات والمواد والعناصر، ومن أنكر أمثال هذه المعاني فقد جحد الضرورة وباهت العقل، وأنكر الأوائل التي في ذهن الإنسان، وسبيله سبيل السوفسطائية. كيف وإنكاره ذلك مما لم يدر في قلبه ولا جال في ذهنه، ثم لم يعبر بالإنكار، ولا أشار إليه بالإقرار، فوجد أن المعنى معلوم بالضرورة، وإنما الشك في أنه هو العلم بنفسه أو الإرادة والتقدير والتفكير والتصوير، والتدبير والتمييز بينه وبين العلم هين؛ إذ العلم تبين محض تابع للمعلوم على ما هو به، وليس فيه إخبار ولا اقتضاء وطلب ولا استفهام ولا دعاء ولا نداء وعي أقسام معلومة وقضايا معقولة وراء التبيين والتمييز بينه وبين الإرادة أسهل وأهون، فإن الإرادة قصد إلى تخصيص الفعل ببعض الجائزات ولا قصد في هذه القضايا ولا تخصيص، وأما التقدير والتفكير والتدبير فكل ذلك عبارات عن حديث النفس وهو الذي يعنى به من النطق النفساني، ومن العجب أن الإنسان يجوز أن يخلو ذهنه عن كل معنى ولا يجد نفسه قط خاليًا عن حديث النفس حتى في النوم فإنه في الحقيقة يرى في منامه أشياء وتحدث نفسه بالأشياء، ولربما يطاوعه لسانه وهو في منامه حتى يتكلم وينطق متابعة لنفسه فيما يحدث وينطق.
By repeating thought as to whether something is possible or impossible or necessary? — to other such thoughts, such that every craftsman first occurs in himself the purpose toward which his craftsmanship is directed, then his soul speaks in the state of action conversing with the tools, instruments, materials, and elements. Whoever denies such meanings has denied necessity and defied reason, and denied the primary notions that are in the human mind, and his way is the way of the Sophists. How could that be? For his denial of that has not occurred in his heart nor passed through his mind, nor did he express denial nor indicate it by affirmation. So he finds that the meaning is known by necessity; rather, the doubt is whether it is knowledge itself or will, estimation, thinking, imaging, and management. Distinguishing it from knowledge is easy, for knowledge is pure representation, following the known as it is, and it contains no informing, no demanding, no asking, no supplication, no calling, and no awareness of known categories and intelligible propositions beyond representation. Distinguishing it from will is easier and simpler, for will is an intention to specify an action among some possibilities, and there is no intention in these propositions nor specification. As for estimation, thinking, and management, all of these are expressions of internal speech (ḥadīth al-nafs), which is what is meant by psychic speech (al-nuṭq al-nafsānī). It is surprising that it is possible for a person's mind to be empty of every meaning, yet one never finds his soul empty of internal speech, even in sleep, for in reality he sees things in his dream, and his soul speaks of things, and perhaps his tongue obeys him while he is asleep until he speaks and utters following his soul in what it relates and utters.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of internal speech (ḥadīth al-nafs / al-nuṭq al-nafsānī). The author begins by describing the inner deliberation of a craftsman who repeats thoughts about possibility, impossibility, and necessity. This internal dialogue is presented as a universal human experience. Denying it, he argues, is against necessity and sophistical. He then clarifies that what is known by necessity is this meaning, but one might confuse it with knowledge or will. The distinction is elaborated: knowledge is a pure representation of the known, lacking any imperative, interrogative, or other mood; will is a specific intention to select an action, whereas internal speech involves statements like questions and commands. The terms 'estimation,' 'thinking,' and 'management' are all synonyms for internal speech. The proof from dreams is given: even in sleep, a person has internal speech, and sometimes the tongue moves in accordance. Thus internal speech is always present.

النطق النفساني
العلم
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Internal speech is just a kind of knowledge or will.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge is pure representation without mood, and will involves intention to specify. Internal speech includes various propositional attitudes (questions, commands, etc.) and lacks the specific intention of will.

Key Takeaway
Internal speech is a distinct, ever-present reality known by necessity, not reducible to knowledge or will.

Expression and Internal Speech: Conventional vs. Rational Indication
Al-Shahrastani cites a verse to prove internal speech via conventional expression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani uses the conventional indication of expressions to prove the existence of internal speech as a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
Expressions point to internal speech by convention, not by reason or identity.

In this passage, Al-Shahrastani employs a well-known line of poetry to anchor a key distinction between two types of indication: conventional (dalālat al-muwaḍaʿa) and rational (dalālat al-ʿaql). By clarifying that expressions, gestures, and writings indicate internal speech through convention, he establishes that internal speech is not reducible to knowledge or will. This distinction is foundational for the Ashʿarī doctrine that the divine speech (kalām Allāh) is an eternal attribute, not a created sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the author begins with a poetic quote and then unpacks its implication.
The verse is often attributed to Al-Mutanabbī but is used here as a standard proof.
2
Identify the two types of indication
The passage distinguishes between the indication of expressions upon speech (conventional) and the indication of judgments upon knowledge (rational). Find both and note their differences.
Conventional indication varies by language; rational indication is universal.
3
Consider the purpose of this distinction
Ask yourself: Why does Al-Shahrastani need to emphasize that expressions indicate internal speech by convention rather than by identity or causality? This supports the idea that internal speech is a real attribute separate from knowledge.
This distinction is crucial for defending the orthodox Ashʿarī position on the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصدق من قال: إن الكلام لفي الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا فالعبارة والإشارة والكتابة دلالة بقرائنها تدل على أن لها مدلولاً خاصاً متميزاً عن العلم والإرادة، ولكل عبارة خاصة مدلول خاص متميز عن سائر المدلولات وهذا أوضح ما تقررة فإن دلالات العبارات على النطق دلالة المواضعة والتوقيف، ويختلف بالأمم والأمصار ودلالة الأحكام على العلم دلالة العقل
And truthful is the one who said: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue was only made a proof upon the heart.' So expression, gesture, and writing are an indication by their contexts that they have a specific meaning distinct from knowledge and will. For each specific expression there is a specific meaning distinct from all other meanings. This is the clearest point established. For the indications of expressions upon speech are the indication of convention and stipulation, and they differ among nations and regions, while the indication of judgments upon knowledge is the indication of reason.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents a classic proof for internal speech (kalām nafsī) by citing the well-known line: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue was only made a proof upon the heart.' Then the author explains: expressions (words), gestures, and writings are signs that carry a specific meaning (madlūl) distinct from knowledge and will. Each expression has a specific meaning. The relationship between expressions and internal speech is conventional (al-muwaḍaʿa wa al-tawqīf), varying between nations, whereas the relationship between judgments and knowledge is rational (dalālat al-ʿaql). This distinction underscores that internal speech is not mere knowledge but a real attribute indicated by conventional signs.

Key Terms

دلالة العقل
النطق النفساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Expressions are the same as the meaning they convey, or they produce knowledge directly.
Correction: The passage clarifies that expressions indicate internal speech by conventional agreement, not by identity or rational necessity. They are signs, not the thing itself.

Checkpoint

C) By being identical to internal speech.

Key Takeaway
Expressions indicate internal speech by convention, not by reason, thereby supporting the Ashʿarī doctrine of an eternal, uncreated divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Shahrastani highlights that conventional indication varies among nations, while rational indication is universal. In your own life, are there areas where you rely on cultural conventions rather than the universal truths of reason and revelation? Are there cultural practices you follow even when they contradict what you know to be right? Ask Allah for the courage to align your actions with His eternal speech.

Responding to the Objection and Defining the Soul
The author answers the objection and defines the soul with philosophical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the acceptance of rationally evident truths from philosophical sources and how he defines the soul in two distinct senses with speech as its substantial differentia.

Core Idea
Rational truths must be accepted even when they align with philosophy.

This passage tackles a common objection: that defining the soul as a spiritual substance aligns with the philosophers. The author dismisses this as an unworthy question and insists that rational insights, once evident, must be accepted regardless of their source. He then presents the philosophers' own definitions to clarify his view, showing that he is not blindly adopting philosophical ideas but recognizing rational truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's dismissive tone toward the objection and his immediate shift to a philosophical definition.
2
Identify the objection and the author's response
Find where the objection is stated (attribution to philosophers, soul as spiritual substance, address, reward/punishment). Then see how the author responds: he dismisses it as unworthy and insists rational meanings must be accepted when evident.
The author does not engage with the objection philosophically; he simply declares it invalid.
3
Analyze the two definitions of the soul
The author presents two definitions: (1) the broad sense shared with animals and plants: first perfection of a natural body with potential life; (2) the specific sense shared with angels: an incorporeal substance that is first perfection of a natural body, moving it by choice from a rational principle.
These definitions are from Aristotelian philosophy, showing the author's willingness to use them.
4
Understand the differentia: speech vs muteness
Speech (nuṭq) is the substantial differentia in all cases, whether potential or actual. If both are absent, the differentia would be muteness (like neighing, braying, roaring). This clarifies that speech is essential to the human soul.
The concept of 'muteness' contrasts with rational speech, emphasizing the distinctiveness of humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نسبة صاحب هذه المقالة إلى الفلاسفة والتشنيع عليه بأنه يؤدي إلى كون النفس جوهرا روحانيا غير جسم، وأن الخطاب يتوجه عليه، وأن الثواب والعقاب له، فهو سؤال لا يستحق الجواب، والمعاني العقلية إذا لاحت حسية كالشمس وجب اعتقادها ولزم اعتقالها من غير التفات إلى مذهب دون مذهب، وقد قالت الفلاسفة: النفس الإنساني عندنا بالمعنى الذي يشترك فيه الإنسان والحيوان والنبات أنه كمال أول الجسم الطبيعي متحرك الي ذي حياة بالقوة، وبالمعنى الذي يشترك فيه الإنسان والملك أنه جوهر غير جسم لهو كمال أول الجسم طبيعي محرك له بالاختيار عن مبدأ نطقي عقلي بالفعل وبالقوة، فالذي بالفعل هو خاصة النفس الملكية، والذي بالقوة هو فصل النفس الإنساني، فالنطق فصل ذاتي على كل حال سواء كان بالقوة والاستعداد، أو كان بالفعل والوجود ومن عدم المعنيين كان فصله الذاتي عجمة إما صاهل أو ناهق أو هادر أو فصل آخر.
As for the attribution of the author of this statement to the philosophers and the rebuke against him that it leads to the soul being a spiritual substance (jawhar rūḥānī) that is not a body, and that address is directed to it, and that reward and punishment belong to it, it is a question that does not deserve an answer. Rational meanings when they appear perceptible like the sun must be believed and held fast to without regard to one doctrine over another. The philosophers have said: The human soul (al-nafs al-insānī) in the sense in which human, animal, and plant share is that it is the first perfection (kamāl awwal) of the natural body that moves towards that which has life potentially. And in the sense in which human and angel share is that it is a substance not a body, it is the first perfection of the natural body, moving it by choice from a rational intellectual principle actually and potentially. That which is actual is the specific property of the angelic soul, and that which is potential is the differentia of the human soul. Thus speech (al-nuṭq) is a substantial differentia (faṣl dhātī) in any case, whether it is potential and dispositional, or actual and existent. If both meanings are absent, its substantial differentia would be muteness (‘ujma), either a neigh, a bray, a roar, or another differentia.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing an objection: someone might accuse the author of aligning with the philosophers by implying that the soul is a spiritual substance (جوهر روحاني غير جسم) that receives address and reward or punishment. The author dismisses this objection as unworthy of answer, insisting that rational meanings (المعاني العقلية) when they appear as evident as the sun must be accepted regardless of which school holds them. He then cites the philosophers' definition of the human soul in two senses: (a) in the broad sense shared with animals and plants: it is the first perfection of a natural body that potentially has life; (b) in the specific sense shared with angels: it is an incorporeal substance that is the first perfection of a natural body, moving it by choice from a rational intellectual principle (مبدأ نطقي عقلي) either actually or potentially. The actual aspect is specific to the angelic soul, while the potential aspect is the differentia (فصل) of the human soul. Thus speech (نطق) is a substantial differentia in all cases, whether potential or actual. If both potential and actual speech are absent, the differentia would be muteness (عجمة) – such as neighing, braying, roaring, or some other differentia. This shows that the author is not dogmatically rejecting philosophical views but is willing to accept rational truths.

Key Terms
جوهر روحاني
كمال أول
نطق بالقوة
نطق بالفعل
فصل ذاتي
عجمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is rejecting all philosophical definitions of the soul.
Correction: The author actually accepts philosophical definitions when they are rationally evident, as shown by his citation and explanation of the philosophers' own definitions.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that his view leads to the soul being a spiritual substance?
A) He denies that the soul is spiritual.
B) He says the objection is unworthy of answer and rational truths must be accepted.
C) He argues that the soul is actually a body.
D) He ignores the objection.

Key Takeaway
The author defends accepting rationally evident truths from philosophical sources, and defines the soul in two senses with speech as its substantial differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because it came from a source you considered problematic—be it a philosopher, a different sect, or a modern scientist? Are you willing to accept what is rationally evident even when it challenges your comfort zone?
?
This lesson shows that the human soul is defined by its rational potential (nutq bil quwwa). If your speech and reason are gifts that distinguish you from animals, do you actually use them to seek truth and worship Allah, or do you leave that potential dormant?
?
The author dismisses the objection as unworthy of a response. Is there a criticism you have received about your faith that you have dismissed too quickly out of pride, without examining whether it might contain a rational truth? What would it cost you to truly listen?

The Unlimited Power of the Soul Compared to Bodily Powers
The author contrasts finite bodily power with infinite soul power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's intellectual power is unlimited compared to bodily powers, and how this proves the soul's non-bodily nature.

Core Idea
The soul's capacity for infinite knowledge proves it is not a body.

This passage presents a key argument for the soul's immateriality. The author contrasts the finite scope of bodily powers with the infinite reach of the rational soul. By recognizing that the soul can know an unlimited number of things, we see that its essence and powers cannot be physical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the contrast presented.
The passage is one continuous argument.
2
Identify the two types of power
Locate the description of bodily power (finite) and intellectual psychic power (infinite). Understand why each is characterized that way.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that the soul cannot be a body because its powers are not limited. Reflect on how this conclusion follows from the premise.
This is a proof for the soul's immateriality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن خواص قواها وأفعالها أن كل قوة جسمانية فهي متناهية الأثر إلى حد معلوم، والقوة على ما لا يتناهى لا يتصور في الجسم، بخلاف القوة العقلية النفسانية فإنها تقوى على ما لا يتناهى إذ ما يمكن أن تدركه النفس الناطقة من المحسوسات والمعقولات ليست محصورة فيستحيل أن يكون ذاتها ذاتا جسمانيا وقواها قوى جسمانية.
And among the special properties of its powers and actions: every bodily power has a finite effect to a known limit, and power over the infinite is inconceivable in a body. In contrast, the intellectual psychic power is capable of the infinite, because what the rational soul can perceive of sensibles and intelligibles is not limited. Therefore, it is impossible that its essence be a bodily essence and its powers be bodily powers.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage states the third property: bodily powers are limited in effect and cannot handle infinite things. But the soul's intellectual power can handle infinite objects of knowledge—sensory and intelligible. Since the soul can know an unlimited number of things, it cannot be a body or have bodily powers, because bodies are incapable of infinite capacity. This proves the soul's non-bodily nature.

Key Terms
قوة جسمانية (bodily power)
قوة عقلية نفسانية (intellectual psychic power)
لا يتناهى (infinite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is weak
B) Because it can perceive infinite things
C) Because it is limited
D) Because it is made of atoms

Key Takeaway
The soul's unlimited capacity for knowledge shows it is not a bodily substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your soul's capacity is unlimited, or do you let physical limitations define what you believe you can learn or achieve? What would change if you fully embraced that your soul is not bound by your body?
?
The soul can perceive an infinite number of things, yet you may spend hours on trivial distractions. What is one area of knowledge—about Allah, yourself, or the universe—that you have neglected because you thought your mind was too weak or limited? How will you begin to explore it today?
?
If the soul is not a body, then your worth is not in your physical appearance or strength. How does this reshape what you truly value in yourself and others? What attachment to the physical world must you loosen to align with this truth?

The Soul's Self-Perception Without an Organ
The author describes the soul's ability to perceive itself directly without a bodily organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's immediate self-perception demonstrates its independence from the body.

Core Idea
The soul knows itself directly, without any organ.

This passage introduces a key property of the soul: its ability to perceive itself without any bodily instrument. The author argues that even in sleep or intoxication, the soul never loses awareness of its own essence, only of other things. This direct self-awareness shows the soul's non-physical nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the emphasis on the soul perceiving its own essence by itself.
Pay attention to the strong language: 'أخص إدراكاتها' (the most specific of its perceptions).
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two main claims: (a) the soul perceives itself by itself, and (b) this perception is not via an instrument. Locate these in the text.
The second claim is at the end: 'ثم المدرك من ذاته ليس بآلة'.
3
Reflect on the examples
The author uses sleep and intoxication as examples. Why? They show that the soul is always self-aware even when it is oblivious to everything else. This reinforces that self-awareness is not dependent on the body.
Even a drunkard or sleeper has awareness of their own essence, though they may be unaware of external things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما خواصها من جهة إدراكها فأخص إدراكاتها أنها تدرك ذاتها بذاتها، وأن ذاتها لا تعزب عن ذاتها ، وتراها تغفل عن كل شيء تقديراً سوى ذاتها، حتى النائم والسكران يغفل عن كل شيء سوى ذاته، ثم المدرك من ذاته ليس بآلة
And as for its properties from the perspective of its perception: the most specific of its perceptions is that it perceives its own essence by its own essence, and that its essence does not disappear from its essence. And you see it forgets about everything except its essence by estimation, even the sleeper and the drunkard forget everything except their essence. Then that which perceives from its essence is not by an instrument.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth property: the soul's self-awareness. The soul perceives itself directly, without an organ. It never loses awareness of its own essence, even when it is oblivious to everything else, as in sleep or intoxication. This self-perception is not mediated by any bodily instrument, further demonstrating its non-bodily nature.

Key Terms
إدراك (perception)
ذات (essence/self)
آلة (instrument)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-awareness is a bodily function dependent on the brain.
Correction: The soul perceives itself directly without any bodily organ, even when the body is inactive (asleep/drunk).

Checkpoint

A) External objects
B) Its own essence
C) Other souls
D) Bodily sensations

Key Takeaway
The soul's immediate and constant self-perception, independent of any bodily instrument, proves its non-material nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are deeply asleep or intoxicated, you still have a sense of 'I'—your soul remains aware of itself. Have you ever paused to recognize that this 'I' is not your body but something constant? How does that change the way you view your own identity?
?
If your soul perceives itself without any organ, then your true self is independent of your physical senses. In what ways do you currently live as if your body is your self? What would you change today to honor your soul's constant presence?
?
The author says the soul forgets everything except itself. Your soul never forgets you, even when you are distracted by the world. Are you conscious of your soul as it is conscious of you? What one habit could you adopt to become more aware of your own soul?
