The Soul's Self-Awareness Proves It Is Not a Body
Argument from self-awareness that the soul is not a body or bodily power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's direct self-awareness demonstrates that it is not a body or a bodily power.

Core Idea
The soul knows itself directly, unlike any bodily instrument.

This passage from al-Shahrastani presents a thought experiment: imagine the soul oblivious to everything except itself. In that state, it perceives itself directly—it is both subject and object. This unique self-awareness shows the soul cannot be a body or a bodily power, because bodily senses cannot perceive themselves, and bodily instruments have limitations that the soul does not. The author also warns that seeking a proof for such self-evident truths only obscures them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thought experiment
Read the opening lines where the author asks us to suppose the soul is oblivious to everything except its own essence. Notice that under this condition, the soul is both perceiver and perceived.
This is a conceptual isolation, not a literal claim about ordinary experience.
2
Contrast with senses and instruments
The author then compares the soul's self-perception to senses (which cannot perceive themselves) and instrumental perception (which fails when the instrument is damaged or overwhelmed). Observe each contrast.
The soul perceives the weak after the strong, unlike instruments that become saturated.
3
Recognize the self-evident nature
Finally, note that the author says we perceive these properties necessarily from ourselves, without need for proof. Reflect on why seeking a proof for such basic facts would only make them less clear.
This is a key principle: some truths are known directly and do not require demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جسمانية أو نفسانية ؛ لأنا فرضناها غافلة عن كل شيء سوى ذاتها، والمدرك ليس جسماً أو جزءاً من جسم، فإنا في الفرض المذكور أغفلناها عن كل شيء سوى ذاتها فهي مدركة ومدركة وعاقلة ومعقولة لا يحجبها عن ذاتها شيء البتة، ونفس إدراكها نفسها لا كالحواس فإنها لا تدرك ذاتها بذاتها ولا كالمدرك بالآلات إذا أصابت الآلة آفة بطل إدراكه أو ضعف، ولا كالمدرك بآلة إذا أدرك شيئًا قويا لم يمكنه إدراك الضعيف، بل هي تدرك الضعيف بعد القوي، والخفي بعد الجلي، أولا كما يضعف بامتداد الزمان، ويبليه ممر الدهور والحدثان، فإنه ربما يقوى في الكبير ويضعف في الصغير، فهذه كلها من خواص النفس الإنسانية نحس من أنفسنا ضرورة أن الأمر كذلك من غير احتياج إلى برهان يقام عليه، والمعاني الضرورية إذا احتال المرء فيها لطلب برهان علیها ازدادت خفاء وانتقصت جلاء.
Bodily or psychic; because we suppose it oblivious to everything except its essence, and the perceiver is not a body or a part of a body. For we, in the mentioned supposition, caused it to be oblivious to everything except its essence, so it is perceiving and perceived, rational and intelligible; nothing veils it from its essence at all. And its very perception of itself is not like the senses, for they do not perceive themselves by themselves, nor like the perceiver through instruments, for when the instrument is afflicted with a defect, its perception becomes invalid or weak, nor like the perceiver through an instrument when it perceives something strong and is unable to perceive the weak. Rather, it perceives the weak after the strong, and the subtle after the clear. It does not weaken with the extension of time, nor does the passage of ages and events wear it down; indeed, it may be strong in the old and weak in the young. All these are properties of the human soul; we perceive from ourselves necessarily that the matter is so, without need for a proof to be established upon it. And necessary meanings, if one attempts to seek a proof for them, become more hidden and decrease in clarity.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents a demonstration from self-awareness that the human soul is not a body or a bodily power. The author begins by assuming the soul is made oblivious to everything except itself (a thought experiment). Under that condition, the soul perceives itself directly—it is both perceiver and perceived, rational and intelligible, with nothing intervening. This direct self-perception differs from sense perception: senses do not perceive themselves, and instruments can fail or limit perception. The soul, however, perceives the weak after the strong, the subtle after the clear, and does not weaken over time (unlike bodily faculties that may decline with age or grow in youth). These observed properties are known directly from our own experience, without requiring a proof. The author cautions that seeking a proof for such self-evident matters only obscures them.

Key Terms
النفس
الحواس
المدرك
إدراك الذات
المعاني الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that self-awareness is a property of the brain or body, and that the soul's perception is just another bodily function.
Correction: The author argues that self-awareness is unique to a non-corporeal soul because bodily senses and instruments cannot perceive themselves directly, have limitations that the soul does not, and can weaken over time—unlike the soul.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the soul perceive directly without any instrument?
A) External objects
B) Other souls
C) Its own essence
D) Divine truths

Key Takeaway
The soul's direct self-awareness reveals its non-corporeal nature, a truth known necessarily without external proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience self-awareness every waking moment. Do you ever truly consider that this 'I' is not your brain or body? What practical difference would it make in your life right now if you deeply believed your soul is immaterial and eternal?
?
The author says these truths are known necessarily without proof. Do you sometimes rely too much on external arguments for your faith, neglecting what your own soul already knows? How can you cultivate a more immediate certainty from within, starting today?
?
Your soul perceives itself directly and does not weaken with time. Yet you spend most of your energy on temporary, bodily concerns. What one concrete change can you make this week to prioritize the reality of your soul over the distractions of the body?

The Abstraction of Universals Proves the Soul's Indivisibility
Shahrastani proves the rational soul is not composite through abstraction of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shahrastani uses the process of abstraction of universal concepts to prove that the rational soul is indivisible and non-material.

Core Idea
Only a non-material soul can conceive a universal free from all material attributes.

Shahrastani presents a tightly-woven argument from the nature of intellectual abstraction. He begins with a principle about universal meanings, then establishes the material characteristics of bodies, and finally traces how sensation, imagination, and intellect each abstract differently, culminating in a proof that the soul itself cannot be material or composite. This lesson walks through each step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial premise
Start with the claim that a unitary universal meaning does not inhere in a composite. This sets the stage for the proof.
This principle is foundational for the argument.
2
Recognize material attributes of bodies
Note that every body has position, quantity, shape, and space. Anything inhering in such a body also takes on these attributes.
This establishes the material context.
3
Follow the levels of abstraction
Examine how sensation, imagination, and intellect each abstract more completely from material attachments. Sensation excludes matter, imagination apprehends in absence, intellect conceives universals free of all material conditions.
Pay attention to the increasing degrees of abstraction.
4
See the conclusion about the soul
The ability to form such a universal concept indicates that the soul itself is indivisible, not composed of matter and form, and free from material attributes.
This is the final point of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنى الوحداني الكلي بالذات لا يحل في مركب، ونثبتها بناءً على أن كل جسم فهو ذو وضع وقدر وشكل وحيز، وكل ما حل في ذي قدر ووضع فيكون له نسبة وقدر وهيئة ووضع والمعنى المعقول المجرد في نفس الإنسان مجرد عن القدر والوضع، فتجرده إما أن يكون باعتبار محله أو باعتبار ما منه حصل، وباطل أن يكون تجرده بما منه حصل، فإن الإنسان يتلقى حد الإنسان وحقيقته من إنسان شخصي له قدر مخصوص، لكن الحس تجرده نوع تجريد عن المادة، فإن المادة لا تدخل في الحس، بل التماثل الذي يطابقه يرتسم فيه، والخيال يجرده تجريدا أشد، فإن الحس يناله وهو حاضر، والخيال يناله وهو غائب، والفكر يجرده تجريدا أشد، فإن الخيال يناله مع غيبوبته شخصيا جزئيا، والفكر العقلي يتصوره مجردا فيتصوره العقل كليًّا واحدا مبرأ من الوضع، والقدر مجردا عن المادة، والشكل فتجرده بما هو مجرد، وتفرده عن العلائق المادية بما هو مفرد، فدل إدراكه المعقول على هذا الوجه المذكور على أن ذات النفس الناطقة العاقلة لا تنقسم إلى جزء وجزء ولا تتركب من مادة وصورة ولا توصف بوضع ومقدار وشكل وحيز، وذلك ما أردنا بيانه.
And the unitary universal meaning does not inhere in a composite, and we prove it based on that every body has position, quantity, shape, and space, and everything that inheres in something possessing quantity and position has relation, quantity, form, and position. And the intelligible meaning abstracted in the human soul is abstracted from quantity and position. So its abstraction is either by consideration of its locus or by consideration of that from which it is obtained. And it is false that its abstraction is by that from which it is obtained, because the human receives the definition and reality of 'human' from a particular human possessing a specific quantity. But sensation abstracts it with a kind of abstraction from matter, for matter does not enter into sensation; rather, the similitude that corresponds to it is imprinted in it. And imagination abstracts it with a stronger abstraction, for sensation apprehends it while it is present, and imagination apprehends it while it is absent. And intellect abstracts it with an even stronger abstraction, for imagination apprehends it, in its absence, as particular and individual, while the rational intellect conceives it abstracted, so the intellect conceives it as one universal, free from position, quantity, abstracted from matter and shape. So its abstraction is by what is abstracted, and its separation from material relations is by what is separate. Thus, its intelligible apprehension in this described manner indicates that the essence of the rational, intellective soul does not divide into part and part, nor is it composed of matter and form, nor is it described with position, magnitude, shape, and space. And that is what we intended to explain.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the nature of abstraction that the rational soul is indivisible and non-material. The author begins by stating that a universal meaning does not inhere in a composite. He then establishes that every body has position, quantity, shape, and space, and anything inhering in such a body has these attributes. The intelligible meaning in the human soul is abstracted from quantity and position. The abstraction could be due to its locus or its source; it cannot be due to its source because we derive the definition of 'human' from a particular human. Sensation abstracts it somewhat by excluding matter; imagination abstracts more strongly by apprehending the absent; intellect abstracts most fully by conceiving a universal free of all material attributes. This demonstrates that the soul itself is not divisible, not composed of matter and form, and not characterized by position, magnitude, shape, or space. The argument proceeds stepwise through the levels of abstraction to show that only a non-material intellect could perform such complete abstraction.

Key Terms
المعنى الوحداني الكلي
التجريد
النفس الناطقة
الحس
الخيال
الفكر العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that universals are simply mental constructs without metaphysical significance.
Correction: The author argues that the very act of abstraction requires a soul that itself is not material. The soul's ability to form universals proves its own non-material, indivisible nature.

Checkpoint
What does the ability to conceive a universal meaning (like 'human') demonstrate about the soul according to Shahrastani?
A) That the soul is composed of matter and form
B) That the soul has shape and position
C) That the soul is indivisible and non-material
D) That the soul is a body with quantity

Key Takeaway
The soul's ability to form universal concepts free from material attributes proves its own indivisible and non-material nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can conceive of 'humanity' as a universal, free from any particular body. Yet you often identify yourself so strongly with your physical appearance and possessions. If your soul is truly indivisible and immaterial, why do you cling to what is temporary and material? What would it mean for you to live today as if your true self is that abstract, immortal soul?
?
This lesson argues that your intellect can separate a concept from all material attachments. But in your daily life, do you habitually attach your worth to material things—your job, your looks, your wealth? That attachment reveals a hidden belief that your soul is not truly immaterial. Will you examine one such attachment today and remind yourself that your soul, like the universal, is free from quantity and position?
?
The author says that only an indivisible soul can grasp a universal. Have you ever considered that every time you understand a universal concept (justice, mercy, truth), you are experiencing a proof that your soul is not like your body? If death only destroys the body, not the soul, then your actions now have eternal consequences. Are you living with that urgency? What is one concrete change you will make today to align your life with the reality of your immortal soul?

6 min
The First Objection: Defining the Soul as a 'Perfection'
The objector presents the first of three attacks on the definition of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problems with defining the soul as a 'perfection of a natural organic body', as argued by the first objector.

Core Idea
The definition's key term 'kamaal' is equivocal, so it cannot be a proper genus.

The objector does not reject the existence of the soul but questions whether the definition captures its essence. He first points out that the word 'perfection' (kamaal) is used in multiple senses, making it unsuitable as a genus. Then he argues that calling the soul a 'perfection of a body' creates a logical separation and contradicts the notion of perfection, especially when evil souls are considered. This lesson explores these two lines of attack.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the objector raises three objections; the first one is detailed here. Observe how he structures his criticism.
The passage begins by recalling previous findings before introducing the objector.
2
Analyze the criticism of 'kamaal'
Focus on the part where the objector questions the meaning of 'kamaal'. He argues it is an equivocal term (lafz mushtarak) with various meanings that cannot serve as a proper genus.
Consider why equivocal terms are avoided in definitions.
3
Examine the criticism of 'perfection of a body'
Next, the objector argues that if the soul is a perfection, it should not be separate from the body. The phrase 'perfection of a body' implies separation, and the notion of evil souls being perfections is contradictory.
Think about the implication of good and evil souls sharing the same definition.
4
Ask: what does this objection aim to show?
The objector is not denying the soul itself but showing that the philosophical definition is logically flawed. Understand the importance of precise language in definitions.
This ties back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرنا بحال الأنبياء أشبه وحال المنام بحال الأولياء أقرب، فقد تبين مما ذكرنا أن النفس الإنسانية ليست من شبح الأجسام التي تتناهى بالحدود وتنقسم بالقسم وأن النطق الذي لها ليس من جملة النطق اللساني الذي يختلف بالأمصار والأمم، وأنها الأصل في الإنسانية والمخاطبة بالأحكام التكليفية.

اعترض عليهم معترض من وجوه ثلاثة أحدها: اعتراض على الحدود والرسم الذي ذكروه والثاني: اعتراض على خواص أفعالها، والثالث: اعتراض على إدراكاتها وأحوالها وردها إلى مدرك آخر.

أما الأول فقولهم: حد النفس بالمعنى الذي يشاركه النفس الحيوانية والنباتية أنه كمال جسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة، قيل: أولا ما المعنى بالكمال الذي ذكرتموه؛ أهو جزء من جسم، أم عرض في جسم، أم أمر آخر وراء الجسم؟ فإنكم ذكرتم أنها بالمعنى الذي يشترك فيه الإنسان والملك وهو جوهر وراء الجسم، والكمال الذي وضعتموه موضع الجنس لفظ مشترك قد أطلق تارة بمعنى جزء وقوة أو عرض وأطلق تارة بمعنى آخر هو وراء الجسم، فمن الذي يشاركه الحيوان والنبات لم يشاركه الملك، ومن الوجه الذي يشاركه الملك لم يشاركه الحيوان، ولفظ الكمال واحد وقد أطلق إطلاق الجنس لا بالتواطؤ، بل بالاشتراك على أن الكمال من وجه آخر لفظ مشترك فإنه يطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله القوة ويعنى به الفعل والوجود ويطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله المبدأ ويعنى به الغاية، والتمام ويطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله النقص والعيب ويعنى به السلامة، والألفاظ المشتركة لا تصلح ولا تستعمل في الحدود والرسوم.

ثم قلتم: كمال جسم فقد أضفتموه إلى الجسم؛ إما إضافة على سبيل اللام، وإما إضافة على سبيل من وكمال الشيء ينبغي أن لا ينفصل عن الشيء، فإنه إن انفصل عنه بجوهر لم يكن كمالا له، فالنفس إذا مكمل الجسم الطبيعي لا كمال له ثم النفوس بجواهرها تنقسم على رأي إلى خيرة بالذات والجوهر، وإلى شريرة بالذات والجوهر، فالتي هي خيرة كمالات ومكملات للأجسام الطبيعية الآلية حتى تستعملها في جهات الخير، فكيف يسوغ لنا أن نحكم بأن الشريرة كمالات ومكملات للأجسام الطبيعية الآلية حتى تستعملها في جهات الشر، فإن الأمر يتناقض فيه، وإطلاق لفظ الكمال هاهنا سمج.
We have mentioned that the state of the prophets is more similar and the state of sleep is closer to the state of the saints, so it has become clear from what we have mentioned that the human soul (nafs) is not of the category of physical bodies that are delimited by boundaries and divided by division, and that the speech that it has is not of the category of linguistic speech that varies among regions and nations, and that it is the principle of humanity and the addressee of the legal obligations.

An objector objected to them from three aspects: one of them: an objection to the definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum) they mentioned; the second: an objection to the properties of its actions; and the third: an objection to its perceptions and states and their reduction to another sense.

As for the first: their saying: the definition of the soul in the sense that the animal and plant soul shares is that it is a perfection (kamaal) of a natural organic body (jism tabi'i aali) having life potentially (bi al-quwwa). It was said: first, what is meant by the perfection which you mentioned? Is it a part of a body, or an accident in a body, or another matter beyond the body? For you mentioned that it is in the sense that man and angel share, which is a substance (jawhar) beyond the body, and the perfection which you placed in the position of genus is an equivocal term (lafz mushtarak), sometimes used in the meaning of a part and a power or an accident, and sometimes used in another meaning which is beyond the body. So from the aspect that animal and plant share, they do not share with the angel, and from the aspect that the angel shares, the animal does not share. And the word 'perfection' is one, and it has been used as a genus not by univocality (tawatu') but by equivocation (ishtirak). Moreover, perfection from another aspect is an equivocal term, for it is used for the meaning opposite to potentiality (quwwa), meaning actuality (fi'l) and existence (wujud), and it is used for the meaning opposite to the beginning (mabda'), meaning the end (ghaya) and completion (tamam), and it is used for the meaning opposite to deficiency (naqs) and defect ('ayb), meaning soundness (salama). And equivocal terms are not suitable and are not used in definitions and descriptions.

Then you said: perfection of a body. So you added it to the body, either as an addition by way of (li) or as an addition by way of (min). And the perfection of a thing should not separate from the thing, for if it separates from it by a substance, it would not be a perfection for it. So if the soul is a perfecter (mukammil) of the natural body, it has no perfection. Then the souls by their substances are divided, according to opinion, into good by essence and substance, and evil by essence and substance. So those which are good are perfections and perfecters of natural organic bodies so that they use them in directions of good. So how is it permissible for us to judge that the evil ones are perfections and perfecters of natural organic bodies so that they use them in directions of evil? For the matter is contradictory in it, and the use of the word 'perfection' here is indecent.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three objections to the definition of the soul. It begins with a recap of previous conclusions: the human soul is not a physical body, its speech is not linguistic, and it is the essence of humanity addressed by divine obligations. Then an objector is introduced, raising three objections: first, against the definitions and descriptions given; second, against the properties of the soul's actions; third, against its perceptions and states, reducing them to another source. The passage then elaborates the first objection.

The objector quotes the definition under attack: the soul is 'a perfection (kamaal) of a natural organic body having life potentially.' He then raises two main criticisms. First, he questions the term 'kamaal' (perfection) itself: Is it a part of the body, an accident in it, or something else beyond the body? The objector notes that the defenders claim the soul is a substance beyond the body, yet they define it as a 'perfection' placed as a genus. But the word 'kamaal' is equivocal (lafz mushtarak): it can mean (a) a part or power or accident, or (b) something beyond the body. No single meaning covers both animals/plants and angels—the aspects that animal souls and angelic souls share do not overlap. Moreover, 'kamaal' is even more equivocal: it can mean actuality (opposite of potentiality), or completion/end (opposite of beginning), or soundness (opposite of deficiency). Such equivocal terms cannot serve as proper genus in definitions.

Second, the objector attacks the phrase 'perfection of a body.' The addition of 'of a body' implies either belonging (li) or origin (min). But a thing's perfection should be inseparable from it; if the soul separates from the body as a distinct substance, it cannot be the body's perfection. Thus the soul, if it perfects the body, would not itself be a perfection. Furthermore, souls are divisible into good and evil by essence. Good souls, being perfections, use bodies for good; but evil souls would then also have to be perfections using bodies for evil, which contradicts the notion that a perfection is inherently good. Applying 'perfection' to evil souls is indecent. Thus the definition fails.

The passage does not provide the answer to these objections; it only presents the objector's case.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
كمال (kamaal)
جسم طبيعي آلي (jism tabi'i aali)
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)
لفظ مشترك (lafz mushtarak)
قوة (quwwa)
فعل (fi'l)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector is rejecting the existence of the soul.
Correction: The objector does not deny the soul; he is critiquing the logical coherence of the definition used by the philosophers.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the objector gives against the definition of the soul as 'perfection'?
A) The definition uses an equivocal term that cannot serve as a genus.
B) The soul is not a substance.
C) The definition contradicts religious texts.
D) The soul does not have potentiality.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that the definition's key term 'kamaal' is too ambiguous to define the soul precisely, and the phrase 'perfection of a body' leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector says that calling evil souls 'perfections' is indecent. Reflect: Do you treat your own soul as something inherently good that perfects your body, or do you sometimes act as if your soul is corrupted and beyond perfection? What would change if you truly saw your soul as a perfection from your Creator?
?
If the soul is a perfection that must be inseparable from the body, what does that mean for how you care for your body? Are you treating your body as a mere machine, or as an organic vessel meant to be perfected by your soul? What one habit can you change to honor this connection?
?
The objector warns against using equivocal terms loosely. In your own speech about spiritual matters—like 'soul,' 'faith,' or 'heart'—do you use these words without precise meaning, or do you strive to define them clearly as the scholars have? What would it cost you to be more precise in your spiritual vocabulary today?

The Homonymy of 'Perfection' and Its Exclusion from Definitions
The author explains the homonymy of 'perfection' and lists its three meanings, concluding that homonymous terms cannot be used in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'perfection' is homonymous and why such terms cannot be used in theological definitions.

Core Idea
Perfection is not a single meaning; it is homonymous and thus unfit for definitions.

The author begins by clarifying a crucial linguistic point: the word 'perfection' is used in multiple senses, which makes it ambiguous. Before we can debate whether the soul is a perfection, we must recognise that perfection itself is not a univocal term. This passage lays out three distinct meanings and explains why homonymous words are excluded from formal definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on homonymy
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's description of perfection's multiple meanings.
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three distinct meanings: act/existence, end/completion, soundness.
3
Understand the implication for definitions
Recognize that homonymous words cannot be used in definitions because they lack a single, precise meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشاركه الحيوان ولفظ الكمال واحد وقد أطلق إطلاق الجنس لا بالتواطؤ بل بالاشتراك على أن الكمال من وجه آخر لفظ مشترك فإنه يطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله القوة ويعنى به الفعل والوجود ويطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله المبدأ ويعنى به الغاية والتمام ويطلق على المعنى الذي يقابله النقص والعيب ويعنى به السلامة والألفاظ المشتركة لا تصلح ولا تستعمل في الحدود والرسوم ثم قلتم كمال جسم فقد أضفتموه إلى الجسم إما إضافة على سبيل اللام وإما إضافة على سبيل من وكمال الشيء ينبغي أن لا ينفصل عن الشيء فإنه إن انفصل عنه بجوهر لم يكن كمالا له فالنفس إذا مكمل الجسم الطبيعي لا كمال له ثم النفوس بجواهرها تنقسم على رأي إلى خيرة بالذات والجوهر وإلى شريرة بالذات والجوهر فالتي هي خيرة كمالات ومكملات للأجسام الطبيعية الآلية حتى تستعملها في جهات الخير فكيف يسوغ لنا أن نحكم بأن الشريرة كمالات ومكملات للأجسام الطبيعية الآلية حتى تستعملها في جهات الشر فإن الأمر يتناقض فيه وإطلاق لفظ الكمال هاهنا سمج
Animals share with him, and the word perfection is one, and it has been used as a genus not by univocity but by homonymy, since perfection from another aspect is a homonymous word, for it is applied to the meaning that is opposite to potentiality and by it is meant act and existence, and it is applied to the meaning that is opposite to principle and by it is meant end and completion, and it is applied to the meaning that is opposite to deficiency and defect and by it is meant soundness. Homonymous words are not suitable nor used in definitions and descriptions. Then you said: Perfection of a body, so you added it to the body; either an addition of ownership or an addition of 'from'. And the perfection of a thing should not separate from the thing, for if it separates from it by a substance, it is not a perfection for it. So the soul, if it is a complement of the natural body, has no perfection. Then souls, by their substances, according to an opinion, divide into good by essence and substance, and evil by essence and substance. Those that are good are perfections and complements for natural instrumental bodies so that they use them in the ways of good. So how is it permissible for us to judge that the evil ones are perfections and complements for natural instrumental bodies so that they use them in the ways of evil? For the matter is contradictory in it. And the use of the word perfection here is repulsive.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'perfection' (kamāl) and critiques its use in describing the soul. The author first states that the word 'perfection' is used homonymously (ishtirāk), not univocally (tawāṭuʾ). He lists three meanings: (1) opposed to potentiality, meaning act and existence; (2) opposed to principle, meaning end and completion; (3) opposed to deficiency, meaning soundness. Because it is homonymous, it cannot be used in definitions (ḥudūd) or descriptions (rusūm). Then he addresses the opponent's claim that the soul is a perfection of the body. He points out that if perfection cannot be separated from its subject, but the soul is a separate substance, it cannot be a perfection. Moreover, if souls are good or evil by essence, evil souls would also be perfections, contradicting the meaning of perfection. Therefore, applying the term 'perfection' to the soul is repulsive (samij).

Key Terms
الكمال (kamāl)
الاشتراك (ishtirāk)
التواطؤ (tawāṭuʾ)
الحدود (ḥudūd)
الرسوم (rusūm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfection has a single, unambiguous meaning.
Correction: The author shows that perfection is homonymous with at least three distinct meanings, so it cannot be used univocally.

Checkpoint
How many distinct meanings does the author list for the word 'perfection' (al-kamāl)?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Homonymous terms like 'perfection' cannot be used in definitions because they lack a single, precise meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you use the term 'perfection' without realizing you are mixing its three different meanings (act/existence, end/completion, soundness)? How has this confusion affected your expectations of yourself or others?
?
The passage teaches that homonymous words cannot define anything precisely. What does that mean for your own pursuit of 'perfection' in your relationship with Allah? Are you chasing a single, clear goal, or are you conflating distinct meanings?
?
Think of a time you considered an act of worship 'perfect.' Which of the three meanings were you applying? Could the other meanings expose a hidden deficiency in your understanding, and how will you recalibrate?

Refutation of the Soul as a Perfection
The author refutes the claim that the soul is a perfection of the body, using separation and the division into good and evil souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments that refute the idea that the soul is a perfection of the body.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be a perfection because it separates from the body and because it would include evil souls.

Having established that 'perfection' is ambiguous, the author now directly attacks the claim that the soul is a perfection of the body. He presents two arguments: the first based on the inseparability of perfection, and the second on the absurd implication that evil souls would also be perfections. Both arguments show the inadequacy of applying the term to the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read how the author critiques the notion of soul as perfection.
2
Analyze the separation argument
Understand that if perfection cannot separate from its subject, but the soul is separate, then the soul is not a perfection.
3
Analyze the division argument
Note that souls are either good or evil by essence, yet calling evil souls 'perfections' is contradictory.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The author concludes that using 'perfection' for the soul is repulsive.

Source Text

?

?

?
The passage says that evil souls are perfections for bodies used in evil. Consider your own actions: are there things you do that you know are wrong, yet you still feel they 'complete' you or give you satisfaction? How can you break that attachment and see them as contradictions to your true perfection?

Refutation of the Third Objection: Accidents and Substrates
The author refutes the third objection that all accidents divide with their substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that all accidents divide with their substrate is not necessary, and how the unity of an accident implies the unity of its substrate, using the example of the point.

Core Idea
The division of an accident does not always follow the division of its substrate; unity implies indivisibility.

The opponent argues that every accident necessarily divides when its substrate divides. The author denies this as a universal necessity, pointing out that it was only imagined from a few cases. He then provides counterexamples and reverses the argument: if division of substrate implies division of accident, then unity of accident must imply unity of substrate, which leads to difficulties for the opponent's position, as illustrated by the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that every accident divides with its substrate. Note how the author refutes this by denying its necessity.
Look for 'ليست ضرورية'.
3
Follow the counterexamples
The author gives examples of accidents that do not divide (like contact) and those that subsist in parts. Understand the distinction.
Also note the mutakallim's view on knowledge.
4
Reverse the argument
The author shows that if division of substrate implies division of accident, then unity of accident implies unity of substrate. See how this leads to a problem with the point example.
The point is an accident that is indivisible but inheres in a divisible body.

الاعتراض الثالث فنقول: أولا أنتم مطالبون بأن كل عرض حل في محل انقسم بانقسام المحل ضرورة، فإن هذه القضية عندنا ليست ضرورية، بل لما توهم في الألوان والأشكال أنها تنقسم بانقسام المحل أجري حكم ذلك في الكل، فمن الأعراض ما ينعدم بانقسام المحال ( كالمماسة والتأليف، ومنها ما لا ينعدم فيقوم بجزء منه قيامه بالكل وعند المتكلم العلم الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد، وانعكس الأمر عليكم فنقول: إن كان انقسام المحل يوجب انقسام العرض، فاتحاد العرض يستدعي اتحاد المحل، وأنتم إذا سلمتم معنى وحداني الذات لا ينقسم بوجه فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم أليست النقطة عرض وهو شيء ما لا ينقسم بوجوه، فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا محلا له وحداني الذات لا ينقسم من الجسم ومحلها من الجسم، وجب أن لا ينقسم
The third objection: We say: First, you are required that every accident that inheres in a substrate divides with the division of the substrate of necessity, for this proposition is not necessary for us. Rather, because it was imagined regarding colors and shapes that they divide with the division of the substrate, the ruling of that was applied to everything. Among accidents are those that become non-existent with the division of the substrate (like contact and composition), and among them are those that do not become non-existent, and they subsist by a part of it just as they subsist by the whole. And according to the mutakallim, a single knowledge does not subsist except by a single part. And the matter is reversed upon you: We say: If the division of the substrate necessitates the division of the accident, then the unity of the accident necessitates the unity of the substrate. And you, if you have conceded a meaning that is one in essence, not dividing in any respect, it follows that you must posit a substrate for it that is one in essence, not dividing. Is not the point an accident? And it is something that does not divide in respects. So it follows that you must posit a substrate for it that is one in essence, not dividing from the body. And its substrate is from the body, so it must not divide.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a third objection. The opponent had claimed that every accident necessarily divides when its substrate divides. The author first denies this necessity, noting that the generalization was based on a few examples like colors and shapes. He then gives counterexamples: some accidents, like contact and composition, cease to exist when the substrate divides, and others, like certain qualities, persist in each part. The mutakallim holds that a single instance of knowledge subsists only in a single part. The author then reverses the argument: if division of substrate necessitates division of accident, then unity of accident must imply unity of substrate. Since the opponent concedes that there are immaterial entities (like the divine essence) that are one and indivisible, they would be forced to posit an indivisible substrate for them. He uses the example of the point (an accident in their view) that is indivisible, yet it inheres in a divisible body; this creates a contradiction in their position.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
محل (substrate)
انقسام (division)
وحدانية الذات (oneness of essence)
نقطة (point)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents necessarily divide when their substrate divides.
Correction: The author shows that this is not a universal necessity; some accidents become non-existent, and others persist in parts.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny that the proposition 'every accident divides with its substrate' is necessary?
A) Because the opponent has not proven it
B) Because it was only imagined from a few cases like colors and shapes
C) Because all accidents are indivisible
D) Because the substrate is not divisible

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the division of accidents with their substrate is not universal; the unity of an accident implies the unity of its substrate, which challenges the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the opponent assumed a universal rule based on a few examples. When it comes to your own faith, have you ever built a conviction on limited evidence, only to later find counterexamples that shook your certainty? What specific area of your practice or belief might need a more careful examination right now?
?
The author reverses the opponent's logic: if division of substrate necessitates division of accident, then unity of accident necessitates unity of substrate. Think of your own spiritual life: In what way might a small inconsistency in your beliefs (like accepting an accident that contradicts your premise) reveal a deeper flaw that you have been avoiding? What would it take to resolve that contradiction?
?
The point is an indivisible accident that inheres in a divisible body, exposing a tension in the opponent's worldview. Consider your own worldview: Is there a 'point'—a seemingly trivial detail in your understanding of Allah's attributes—that, if examined, would force you to reject a cherished assumption? Are you willing to let it challenge you?

The Dilemma of Universal Preservation
Dilemma on universal preservation and its alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dilemma about whether universals are perpetually actual in the intellect or can be forgotten, and the two possible means of preservation that follow from forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Universals are not always present; forgetfulness forces us to examine how they are preserved.

The author confronts the philosophers' claim that intellect apprehends universals by posing a simple but powerful dilemma: either the universal remains constantly present or it can be forgotten. Since we experience forgetfulness, the first option is false. This leads to the question of how forgotten universals are preserved, setting the stage for a critique of the separate active intellect theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dilemma structure and the two possibilities following forgetfulness.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author first presents a disjunction: either the universal remains actually in the intellect forever, or it may be neglected. Identify the rejection of the first option and the reason given.
The reason is empirical: humans find themselves neglecting acquired intelligibles.
3
Trace the argument after forgetfulness
If forgotten, two possibilities for preservation emerge: (a) in corporeal powers (like memory) or (b) in a separate intellect. The author uses a parity argument: if corporeal powers can preserve universals, then there is no need for a separate intellect.
The author implies that the philosophers' view is redundant.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Ask yourself why the author frames this as an insurmountable problem for the philosophers. The dilemma forces them to either accept human forgetfulness (which undermines their theory) or admit that preservation occurs in a way that does not require a separate intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نلزمهم أمرا لا جواب لهم عنه، وهو أن هذا المعنى الكلي الواحد الذي يدركه العقل أيبقى مع العقل محفوظا مذكورا بالفعل أبد الدهر، أو يجوز أن يغفل عنه العقل، ولا حاصل للقسم الأول، فإن الإنسان يجد من نفسه غفلته عن المعقولات المحصلة فيبقى متحفظا خزانة الحفظ وهي قوى جسمانية، إن جاز أن يحفظها كلية أفلم لا يجوز أن يكون قوة جسمانية يحصلها كلية، فإن حفظ المرتسم في النفس كنفس الارتسام أم تبقى محفوظة في غير خزانة الحفظ وهو العقل المفارق الفياض عليه، كما قيل: إنه يصير له كالخزانة الحافظة متى طالعه أشرف عليه ثانيا، وأفاده الصورة الأولة بعينها فيذكره ما قد نسي، ويذكر ثانيا ما قد أغفل فيلزم على مقتضى ذلك إشكالان: أحدهما: أن الصورة المفصلة كيف ترتسخ في ذات واهب الصور مفصلة حتى تكون لزيد عنده صورة ولعمرو صورة؟ وهو يحفظ الكل مفصلا منقسما متعددا متمايزا بعضها عن بعض بالكم والكيف، وهو أحدي الذات واسع الإفاضة الإضافة، وهذا من أمحل المحال. والإشكال الثاني: أن الذي تصورتم في جانب الحفظ فيتصور في جانب الإدراك الأول حتى تحكموا بأن المدرك للكليات هو العقل الفعال، كما أن الحافظ هو، وليس إلى النفس الإنسانية إلا تركيب القضايا وتأليف المقدمات ورفع الحجب، ثم يكون العقل مدركا كما كان حافظا، والإنسان يكون مدركا بإدراك في ذات العقل الفعال، كما كان حافظا بحفظ في ذات العقل الفعال، فيكون العقل الانفعالي حاصلا له كما كان العقل الفعلي حاصلا منه، وتكون نسبة العقول الجزئية إليه في العرض عليه نسبة الحواس الباطنة إلى العقل الإنساني في العرض عليه، وهذا موضع إشكال وشك عظيم، وربما يستوفي شرحه في المباحثات التي بيننا وبين الفلاسفة إن شاء الله.
Then we impose upon them a matter for which they have no answer, namely: This single universal meaning that the intellect apprehends—does it remain with the intellect preserved and actually remembered forever, or may the intellect neglect it? The first division is impossible, for man finds in himself his neglect of acquired intelligibles; then he remains preserving [them in] a treasury of preservation, which are corporeal powers. If it is possible that it preserves them as universals, then why is it not possible that a corporeal power acquires them as universals? For the preservation of the impression in the soul is like the impression itself; or are they preserved in [something] other than the treasury of preservation, namely the separate, effusive intellect, as it is said: It becomes for him like a preserving treasury; whenever he studies it, it bestows upon him a second time and gives him the very first form, so he remembers what he had forgotten, and he remembers a second time what he had neglected. There follow from this two problems: The first: How can the detailed form be impressed in the essence of the giver of forms in detail, such that for Zayd there is a form and for 'Amr a form? And He preserves all of it in detail, divided, multiple, distinguished from each other by quantity and quality, while He is one in essence, vast in effusion of relation; this is one of the most absurd impossibilities. The second problem: That which you have conceived on the side of preservation is conceivable on the side of the first apprehension, so that you judge that the apprehender of universals is the active intellect, just as the preserver is it; and the human soul has nothing except the composition of propositions, the arrangement of premises, and the lifting of veils. Then the intellect becomes apprehending as it was preserving, and man becomes apprehending by an apprehension in the essence of the active intellect, just as he was preserving by a preservation in the essence of the active intellect. Then the passive intellect becomes obtained for him just as the active intellect was obtained from him, and the relation of particular intellects to it in the bestowal upon it is like the relation of the internal senses to the human intellect in the bestowal upon it. This is a place of great difficulty and doubt, and perhaps its explanation will be completed in the discussions between us and the philosophers, if God wills.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a new argument against the philosophers. The author first poses a dilemma: does the universal concept remain perpetually actual in the human intellect, or can it be forgotten? The first is false because humans experience forgetfulness. The second leads to two possible means of preservation: either in corporeal faculties (like memory) or in a separate intellect. If corporeal faculties can preserve universals, there is no need for a separate intellect. The author then examines the philosophers' claim that a separate active intellect preserves universals and bestows them upon humans upon recollection. This leads to two problems: (1) How can a simple, one-in-essence being (the active intellect) contain detailed, multiple, and distinct forms for each knower? This is contradictory to simplicity. (2) If the active intellect is both the preserver and the apprehender of universals, then the human mind's role is reduced to merely arranging premises, and true cognition occurs via the active intellect. This would make the passive intellect identical to the active intellect in function, leading to a collapse of the epistemological roles. The author notes that this is a deep point requiring further discussion.

Key Terms
كلي (universal)
عقل مفارق (separate intellect)
حفظ (preservation)
قوى جسمانية (corporeal powers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some philosophers held that the active intellect actually preserves all forms and that the human mind never truly forgets them.
Correction: The author shows that forgetfulness is a real phenomenon, and the philosophers' explanation of a separate intellect as a 'treasury' is problematic, as it raises the question of why corporeal powers cannot suffice.

Checkpoint
What is the first option in the dilemma and why is it rejected?
A) The universal remains always actual; because humans are always conscious of it.
B) The universal is forgotten; because humans experience neglect.
C) The universal remains always actual; because humans experience forgetfulness.
D) The universal is forgotten; because the active intellect is separate.

Key Takeaway
Human forgetfulness forces a choice: either universals are preserved in corporeal faculties or a separate intellect, and the author challenges the necessity of the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you forget a crucial piece of Islamic knowledge—a verse, a ruling, an insight—do you immediately rush to an external source (a book, a teacher) or do you trust that the impression is still within you, awaiting reawakening? What does your automatic response reveal about your view of your own soul's capacities, and how does it align with or contradict the author's argument here?
?
The author implies that if your bodily faculties can preserve knowledge, they can also acquire it. Think of a time you suddenly understood something complex without external help, as if the knowledge had been there all along. Did you attribute that to your own mind or to a separate bestower? How does that moment challenge the philosophers' claim of a separate intellect, and what does it demand from you regarding your responsibility for your own learning?
?
This passage confronts you with the reality of your own forgetfulness. Consider one Islamic truth that you deeply believe but often fail to live by (e.g., reliance on Allah, patience, sincerity). Is that truth preserved only in your memory, or is it 'actually' present in your intellect right now? What would it take for you to move from neglect to actual presence—and what are you going to do about it today?

Two Problems with the Active Intellect
Two problems against the active intellect's role in preservation and intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical problems that arise from the philosophers' claim that the active intellect preserves universals: the impossibility of a simple being containing detailed multiple forms, and the collapse of the distinction between active and passive intellects.

Core Idea
A simple active intellect cannot hold detailed forms for each knower without contradiction.

Having shown that the philosophers' view of preservation leads to redundancy or absurdity, the author now raises two specific problems. The first targets the nature of the active intellect itself, arguing that it cannot contain multiple, distinct forms while being simple. The second problem extends the logic to the process of intellection, showing that if the active intellect is both the preserver and the apprehender, then the human mind's role is marginalized, and the passive intellect becomes unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two problems
The passage explicitly states 'two problems' (إشكالان). Read each one carefully and note how the author phrases them.
2
Analyze the first problem
The first problem is about the 'detailed form' (الصورة المفصلة). How can a simple, one-in-essence being (the active intellect) contain forms that are multiple, divided, and distinct by quantity and quality? The author calls this 'one of the most absurd impossibilities'.
3
Analyze the second problem
The second problem concerns cognition: if the active intellect is both the preserver and the apprehender of universals, then human intellectual cognition becomes merely a passive reception, and the passive intellect (عقل انفعالي) becomes identical in role to the active intellect. This collapses the epistemological hierarchy.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why these problems are devastating for the philosophers' theory. They undermine the coherence of a separate intellect that is both simple yet contains multiplicity, and they reduce human intellect to a passive receiver, contradicting the usual distinction between active and passive aspects.

Source Text

The Active Intellect as Preserver and the First Objection
Author explains the active intellect theory and raises the first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the active intellect's role in preserving forms and the first objection that a simple essence cannot contain multiple distinct forms.

Core Idea
A simple essence cannot hold a multitude of distinct forms.

The author begins by explaining the theory that the active intellect acts as a repository for forms, preserving them for the human soul. He then raises a fundamental problem: how can a single, simple essence contain multiple, detailed, and distinct forms? This lesson unpacks that very problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's presentation of the theory before the objection.
The theory posits the active intellect as a preservative repository.
2
Identify the theory presented
Find where the author describes the active intellect as a 'preserving repository' that shines upon the soul to remind it of forms.
3
Locate the first objection
Identify the first problem: how can detailed forms exist in the simple essence of the bestower of forms?
The author calls this 'among the most absurd impossibilities.'
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why a simple essence cannot contain multiple distinct forms differentiated by quantity and quality.
This objection targets the notion of a multi-form repository in a simple being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جسمانية يحصلها كلية (١) ، فإن حفظ المرتسم في النفس كنفس الارتسام أم تبقى محفوظة (٢) في غير خزانة الحفظ وهو العقل المفارق الفياض عليه، كما قيل : إنه يصير له كالخزانة الحافظة متى طالعه أشرف عليه ثانيًا، وأفاده الصورة الأولة بعينها فيذكره ما قد نسي ، ويذكر ثانيًا ما قد أغفل فيلزم على مقتضى ذلك إشكالان : أحدهما: أن الصورة المفصلة كيف ترتسخ (۳) في ذات واهب الصور مفصلة حتى تكون لزيد عنده صورة ولعمرو صورة ؟ وهو يحفظ الكل مفصلا منقسمًا متعددا متمايزا بعضها عن بعض بالكم والكيف، وهو أحدي الذات واسع الإفاضة الإضافة، وهذا من أمحل المحال. (٤) عام والإشكال الثاني : أن الذي تصورتم في جانب الحفظ فيتصور (٥) في جانب الإدراك الأول حتى تحكموا بأن المدرك للكليات هو العقل الفعال، كما أن الحافظ هو، وليس إلى النفس الإنسانية إلا تركيب القضايا وتأليف المقدمات ورفع الحجب، ثم يكون العقل مدركاً كما كان حافظا، والإنسان يكون مدركا بإدراك في ذات العقل الفعال، كما كان حافظا] (٦) بحفظ في ذات العقل الفعال، فيكون العقل الانفعالي حاصلا له كما كان العقل الفعلي حاصلا منه، وتكون نسبة العقول الجزئية إليه في العرض عليه نسبة الحواس الباطنة إلى العقل الإنساني في العرض عليه، وهذا موضع إشكال وشك ) عظيم، وربما يستوفي شرحه في المباحثات التي بيننا وبين الفلاسفة إن شاء الله. (۷)
Bodily? He obtains it totally (1), for the preservation of the imprinted image in the soul is like the imprinting itself, or it remains preserved (2) in another repository of preservation, which is the separate active intellect that overflows upon it, as it is said: it becomes for him like a preserving repository; whenever he examines it, it shines upon him a second time and provides him with the very first form, so he remembers what he had forgotten, and he recollects what he had neglected. So upon that premise two problems follow: One of them: How can the detailed form become imprinted (3) in the essence of the bestower of forms in a detailed manner, so that there exists for Zayd a form and for Amr a form? For He preserves the whole in a detailed, divided, multiple, distinct way, some from others by quantity and quality, while He is one in essence, vast in the abundance of relation? This is among the most absurd impossibilities. (4) The second problem: That which you have conceived on the side of preservation is conceived (5) on the side of primary perception, so that you rule that the perceiver of universals is the active intellect, just as it is the preserver, and nothing belongs to the human soul except composition of propositions, arrangement of premises, and removal of veils. Then the intellect becomes perceiver just as it was preserver, and the human becomes perceiver by a perception in the essence of the active intellect, just as he was preserver (6) by a preservation in the essence of the active intellect. So the passive intellect becomes acquired for him just as the active intellect was acquired from him, and the relation of the partial intellects to it in being presented to it is like the relation of the inner senses to the human intellect in being presented to it. And this is a place of great difficulty and doubt, and perhaps its full explanation will occur in the discussions between us and the philosophers, if God wills. (7)
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the theory that the active intellect (العقل الفعال) preserves forms and acts as a repository for the human soul. The author first explains the theory: the soul's preservation of an imprinted image is either identical to the imprinting or occurs in the active intellect, which shines upon the soul to remind it. Then two problems are raised. The first problem: how can a simple, one essence contain multiple detailed, distinct forms? This is impossible. The second problem: if the active intellect both preserves and perceives universals, then the human soul's role is reduced to merely combining propositions and arranging premises, and the human being perceives only through the active intellect. This makes the passive intellect a result of the active intellect, and the relation is like that of internal senses to the human intellect. The author concludes that this is a serious difficulty, to be resolved later in discussions with philosophers.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال
واهب الصور
الارتسام
الحفظ
العقل المفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first objection raised against the active intellect theory?
A) The active intellect cannot preserve forms because it is corporeal.
B) The simple essence of the bestower of forms cannot contain multiple distinct forms.
C) The active intellect is not intelligent.
D) The human soul does not need an active intellect.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that a simple essence cannot serve as a repository for multiple distinct forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author asserts that a simple essence cannot contain multiple distinct forms. Consider a belief you hold about God's attributes—are you guilty of assuming that a simple Divine essence can still 'contain' many attributes without logical tension? The Second Objection: Reducing Human Perception
Author presents the second objection and concludes with the difficulty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that if the active intellect both preserves and perceives universals, then human perception is merely derivative and the passive intellect becomes a product.

Core Idea
If the active intellect both preserves and perceives, humans only combine propositions.

The second objection targets the role of human perception. If the active intellect is both the preserver and the perceiver of universals, then the human soul is reduced to merely combining propositions and arranging premises. This lesson examines that objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Notice how the author draws a parallel between preservation and perception.
2
Identify the second objection
Find where the author says that what is conceived for preservation is also conceived for perception, leading to the active intellect being the perceiver of universals.
3
Understand the reduction of human role
See that the human soul is left only with composing propositions and arranging premises; true perception belongs to the active intellect.
4
Consider the conclusion
Note the analogy with inner senses and the passive intellect being derived from the active intellect. The author calls this a place of great difficulty.

Perception Resides in a Single Substance
Argument against the necessity of bodily structure for perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam thinkers argued that perception does not require a bodily structure and can reside in a single substance.

Core Idea
A single perception does not need a whole body; it can exist in one part.

In this passage, the author presents an argument from some rational thinkers (العقلاء) that challenges the common assumption that perception requires a composite body. They argue that if perception can occur in a single part of a larger whole, then it can equally occur in an isolated simple substance. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and its implications for understanding the soul and perception in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice the contrast between the initial claim and the refutation.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the premise that perception occurs in a single part and the conclusion that bodily structure is unnecessary.
The argument uses the independence of substances to support the conclusion.
3
Reflect on substance independence
Understand why the author insists that each substance is independent and that judgment does not change when it is alone or joined.
This is key to the logical move from connected to isolated substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال بعض العقلاء: وقرر أن جميع صفات الكمال تجتمع في كونه حيا، وتنتفي صفات النقص بقولنا: لا آفة به، وأما اشتراطهم البنية حسب اشتراط الحياة غير صحيح، فإن الإدراك الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد من الجملة، وإذا قام به أفاد حكما له، ولا أثر للجواهر المحيطة، فإن كل جوهر مختص بحيزه موصوف بأعراضه، وكما لا يؤثر جوهر في جوهر لا يؤثر حكم جوهر في جوهر آخر، ولا يختلف حكم جوهر بعينه في تفرده وانضمامه، وإذا جاز قيام الإدراك به مع اتصاله بالجواهر جاز قيامه به مع تفرده، فإنه لا يتحول عن صفته تفرد.
Some rational thinkers have said and established that all attributes of perfection are combined in his being living, and attributes of deficiency are negated by our saying 'without defect'. As for their condition of bodily structure on the basis of the condition of life, it is not correct. For a single perception only occurs in a single part of the whole, and when it occurs in it, it provides a judgment for it, and the surrounding substances have no effect. For every substance is specific to its location, described by its accidents, and just as one substance does not affect another, the judgment of one substance does not affect another. Nor does the judgment of a particular substance differ in its being alone or joined, and if it is permissible for perception to occur in it while connected to other substances, it is permissible for it to occur in it while alone, for it does not change from its characteristic when isolated.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from some rational thinkers about perception. It first notes that all perfections reduce to being alive, and defects are negated by 'no defect'. Then it refutes the requirement of bodily structure: a single perception only exists in a single part of the body, not the whole. When it resides in that part, it provides a judgment for that part alone. Surrounding substances (other parts of the body) have no effect. Each substance is independent with its own accidents. Therefore, if perception can occur in a part while it is connected to the whole, it can equally occur in that part when isolated, because the part's characteristic does not change. This supports the possibility of perception in a simple substance without a composite body.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الجوهر
البنية
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That perception requires a bodily organ or structure.
Correction: This passage shows that perception can reside in a single substance; a composite body is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the argument attributed to 'some rational thinkers'?
A) Bodily structure is necessary for perception
B) A single perception can reside in a single substance without requiring the whole body
C) Perception is impossible in isolated substances
D) All attributes are reducible to being alive

Key Takeaway
Perception can reside in a single substance; bodily structure is not necessary for its occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that perception can be in a single substance, independent of a body. Do you truly live as if your soul will retain perception after death? What one action tonight will prove you believe your perception does not end with your body?

What is the first step you will take to train your soul to perceive beyond the material?
?
Recall a moment when you felt something true in your heart without any physical evidence (e.g., a warning about a sin or a conviction about Allah's mercy). How does this lesson confirm that such inner perception is real and valid? Will you now trust it more than your eyes? What will you change about your daily decisions because of this trust?

Is Perception Direct or Mediated? The Mutakallimun's View on the Senses
Shahrastani raises two investigations on perception and presents the mutakallimun's initial reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key questions Shahrastani raises about the nature of perception and the mutakallimun's initial argument that the five senses are distinct in type and reality.

Core Idea
The five senses are distinct in reality, not just by objects, as we directly experience.

Shahrastani introduces further investigations into perception. He poses two main questions: whether perception involves an internal image matching the external object or direct contact, and whether the five external sense organs are mere instruments for a common sense. He then begins to present the mutakallimun's position that each sense is distinct in type and reality, grounded in self-awareness. This lesson explores these foundational debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the English translation. Notice how Shahrastani structures the questions.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the mutakallimun's argument is incomplete.
2
Identify the two main investigations
The passage begins with two 'investigations': one about the role of an image in perception, the other about the locus of perception (external senses vs. common sense).
3
Analyze the disagreement between mutakallimun and philosophers
The author notes that these questions are points of disagreement. Then the mutakallimun's answer begins: they argue for distinct perceptions based on self-evident experience.
The passage ends before the full argument is given, but the key point is that we find ourselves seeing with our eyes and hearing with our ears.
4
Reflect on the importance of self-awareness
The mutakallimun ground their position in internal self-awareness (wijdan). This is a key epistemological method in kalam.
Consider how this differs from purely rational or empirical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبقى هاهنا مباحثة أخرى وهي أن الإدراك هل هو إدراك الصورة في حاسة المدرك تطابق الصورة الخارجة، أم هو إدراك ما في الخارج من غير توسط صورة في الحاسة وشيء آخر، وهو أن محل الإدراك هو الحاسة الظاهرة من العين والأنف والفم والأذن، أم هي آلات وأدوات للحاسة المشتركة بينهما، فيكون الحس الحقيقي فيها حتى تجتمع المختلفات فيها، ويكون الإدراك إدراكا واحدا فيظن بعض العقلاء أنه هو العلم؛ إذ هو في الباطن، ويظن بعضهم أنه إدراك أخص من العلم؛ إذ مدركه في الظاهر، والإدراكات الخمس مختلفة الحقائق حتى يكون اختلافها بالنوع، أم الاختلاف راجع إلى المدركات والإحساس بها بمعنى واحد ؟ وهذا الخلاف بين المتكلمين والفلاسفة. قال المتكلمون : الحواس الخمس مشتملة على إدراكات خمس تختلف أنواعها وحقائقها، والإنسان يجد من نفسه أنه يرى ببصره ويسمع بأذنه، كما يجد أنه يعلم بقلبه، فالبصر محل الرؤية، والأذن محل السمع كالقلب هو محل العلم، وكما
Then there remains here another investigation: Is perception the perception of an image in the sense organ of the perceiver corresponding to the external image, or is it perception of what is outside without the intermediation of an image in the sense? And another thing: Is the locus of perception the external sense from the eye, nose, mouth, and ear, or are they instruments and tools for the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) between them, so that the true sense is in it until the different things are gathered in it, and perception is one perception? So some intellectuals think that it is knowledge ('ilm), since it is internal; and some think that it is a perception more specific than knowledge, since its perceived object is external. And the five perceptions are different in realities such that their difference is by type? Or is the difference referable to the perceived objects and the sensation of them with one meaning? And this is the disagreement between the mutakallimun and the philosophers. The mutakallimun said: The five senses include five perceptions whose types and realities differ. Man finds in himself that he sees with his sight and hears with his ear, just as he finds that he knows with his heart. So the sight is the locus of vision, and the ear is the locus of hearing, just as the heart is the locus of knowledge, and just as
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents further epistemological questions about perception. The author first asks whether perception involves an internal image matching the external object, or direct contact without an image. Then he asks whether the five external sense organs are the actual loci of perception or mere instruments for a single 'common sense' (al-hiss al-mushtarak) that unifies perceptions. If the latter, perception becomes one, leading some to identify it with knowledge, while others consider it more specific. Another question is whether the five perceptions differ by nature (type) or only by their objects. The author notes this is a point of disagreement between mutakallimun and philosophers. The mutakallimun's answer begins: they affirm five distinct perceptions of different types, citing self-awareness—we experience seeing with our eyes, hearing with our ears, and knowing with our heart, indicating distinct faculties. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the gist is that the senses are distinct and not reducible to a single common sense.

Key Terms
إدراك (perception)
صورة (image)
حاسة مشتركة (common sense)
علم (knowledge)
متكلمون (mutakallimun)
فلاسفة (philosophers)
بصّر (sight)
سمع (hearing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five senses are just different manifestations of a single perceptual faculty.
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that the senses are distinct in reality and type, as we experience them differently (e.g., seeing vs. hearing).

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun in this passage, how do we know that the five senses involve distinct perceptions?
A) Through speculative argument.
B) Through self-awareness: we find ourselves seeing with our eyes and hearing with our ears.
C) Through revelation.
D) Through agreement with philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun ground their doctrine of distinct senses in direct human experience, not abstract reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that man finds in himself that he sees with his sight and hears with his ear. When did you last pause to witness this undeniable reality in your own experience? What would you say to someone who claims that your senses are just illusions?
?
The mutakallimun ground their proof in self-awareness—the very thing you possess right now. Are you living as if your senses are distinct gifts from Allah, or do you treat them as a single blurred faculty? How will you practice gratitude for each sense separately today?
?
This passage distinguishes knowledge (in the heart) from sensory perception (in the external organs). Do you realize that your deepest convictions are not mere sensory data? Are you letting your senses dictate your belief, or does your heart's knowledge guide you? What step will you take to elevate heart-knowledge over mere sensation?

Refutation of Image Transfer in Vision
Refutes the theory that sight involves a physical image transfer from object to eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument against the theory that sight involves a physical image transferring from the object to the eye, and the conclusion that perception is a meaning in the sense organ.

Core Idea
Perception is not a physical transfer; it is a meaning in the perceiver.

Have you ever wondered how we see? One ancient theory held that a tiny image leaves the object and enters the eye. In this passage, the Ashʿarī theologian refutes this view with a simple but powerful logical argument. By examining the nature of the image, he shows that the theory is impossible, and concludes that perception is a non-physical quality ('meaning') in the sense organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: if...then...
Note the conclusion: perception is a meaning in the sense.
2
Identify the two options for the image
The author considers whether the image is an accident (arad) or a substance (jawhar). Notice the consequences of each and why both are impossible.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
The argument is a proof by contradiction. If the theory were true, absurd consequences follow. Then it is false.
This is a standard kalām method.
4
Apply the conclusion
The final statement establishes that perception is a meaning (ma‘nā) in the sense organ. Reflect on what this implies about vision: it is an immaterial quality, not a physical reception.
This connects to broader Ashʿarī epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول بانتقال صورة من المرئي إلى البصر باطل أيضا، فإن الصورة لو انتقلت لاحترقت العين برؤية النار، وهي إن كانت عرضا فهي لا تنتقل، وإن كانت جوهرا فلينقص المرئي بانفصال شيء منه، ولا ينفصل شيء منه إلا بسبب، هاهنا فهو باطل، فتحقق أن الإدراك معنى في حاسة المدرك.
And the statement that an image transfers from the seen object to the eye is also false. For if the image transferred, the eye would burn by seeing fire. If it is an accident, it does not transfer; and if it is a substance, then the seen object would decrease by the separation of something from it, and nothing separates from it except by a cause. Thus it is false. So it is established that perception (idrak) is a meaning (ma'na) in the sense organ of the perceiver.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the theory that the visual phenomenon of sight is caused by a physical image (sura) moving from the seen object to the eye. The author presents a logical proof: if such an image transferred, then seeing a fire would burn the eye, which does not occur. Then he analyzes the nature of the image: if it is an accident (arad), it cannot independently transfer; if it is a substance (jawhar), then the seen object would lose a part of itself, which does not happen without a cause. Both options are invalid, so the theory must be false. The conclusion is that perception is a meaning (ma'na) that arises in the sense organ itself, not an external image entering it. This is an important argument for the understanding that perception is an immaterial quality inherent in the perceiver.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrak)
صورة (sura)
معنى (ma'na)
عرض (arad)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision occurs by a physical image entering the eye from the object.
Correction: The passage argues that perception is not a physical transfer but a meaning that arises in the sense organ.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the passage regarding perception?
A) Perception is a physical image in the eye.
B) Perception is a meaning in the sense organ of the perceiver.
C) Perception is impossible to understand.
D) Perception is a substance that transfers.

Key Takeaway
Sight does not involve a physical image entering the eye; perception is an immaterial quality (ma‘nā) that occurs in the sense organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your sight every moment. But this lesson proves that what you see is not an external image entering you—it is a meaning created in you. Does this change how you trust your own eyes? Will you pause today to remember that your perception is a gift, not a direct capture of reality, and ask God to make you perceive rightly?
?
The fire-burning argument shows that if physical images transferred, the eye would burn—but it doesn't. Have you ever reacted to what you saw as if it were physically affecting you (e.g., anger at a sight)? How can this truth about perception being a meaning, not a transfer, help you control your reactions? Commit to one situation today where you will remind yourself of this.
?
If perception is a meaning placed in you by God, then your vision is a divine trust. Are you using your sight to see what pleases Him, or are you letting images distract you? What will you look at today that draws you closer to the truth, knowing that your eyes are not passive receivers but active interpreters of meanings?
