Sensory Perception and Ash'ari's Distinction
Author explains taste and touch, then Ash'ari's separation of hearing/sight from other senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic theologians explained sensory perception, and how al-Ash'ari distinguished between hearing/sight and the other senses.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari separated hearing and sight from other senses by requiring physical contact for taste, touch, and smell.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's school explains the mechanisms of taste and touch, then presents his distinctive view that hearing and sight do not require physical contact, unlike the other senses. This distinction has important implications for understanding divine attributes and the nature of perception.

Guided Path
1
Understand taste and touch mechanisms
Read the description of how taste works via moisture on the tongue and touch via a power diffused in skin, noting the role of the Bestower of Forms (واهب الصور).
The senses are instruments for receiving emanation, not producing perception themselves.
2
Identify Ash'ari's distinction
Find the sentence where al-Ash'ari separates hearing/sight from the other three senses and note the criteria: physical connection vs. no physical connection.
Ash'ari requires physical connection for taste, touch, and smell but not for hearing and sight.
3
Reflect on unification of senses
Consider how Ash'ari's school allows for hearing and sight to be unified in the common sensorium (الحاسة المشتركة), so that seen becomes read and vice versa.
This highlights that perception shares a single locus despite sensory differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر اللسان بواسطة الرطوبة العذبة التي لا طعم لها المنبسطة على ظاهر اللسان، فإنها تأخذ طعم ذي الطعم وتستحيل إليه وتتصل بتلك العصبة فتدركها العصبة، واللمس هو قوة مبثوثة في جميع البشرة واللحم المتصل بها يدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والصلابة واللين والخشونة والملاسة، والخفة والثقل، والذي يحمل هذه القوة جسم لطيف يجري في شباك العصب ويستمد من القلب والدماغ، وإنما يدرك إذا استحال كيفية البشرة إلى شبه المدرك بشرط أن يتفاوتا في الكيفية، وهذه القوى كلها معدات لقبول الفيض من واهب الصور، وإلا فالحاسة لا تحدث إدراكا. والأشعري فصل بين السمع والبصر وبين الثلاثة الباقية؛ حيث شرط لها هنا اتصالا جسمانيا، وأثبت إحساسا جسمانيا ولم يثبتها في صفات الباري تعالى، ولما علم أن لهما اختصاصا بالباطن ردد قوله في أن الإدراك علم مخصوص أو في الحاسة المشتركة، ولا يستبعد من مذهبه قيام الإدراك السمعي معنى آخر بالبصر والبصر بالسمع، فإن القوم حكموا باتحادهما عند الحاسة المشتركة حتى يصير المرئي مقروءاً والمقروء مرئيا، فإنهما في حقيقة الإدراك لا يختلفان، ومحلهما محل واحد فيتحدان، وكلام المعتزلة في الإدراكات مختلط لا ثبات له، أو الله أعلم.
The surface of the tongue [tastes] by means of the sweet moisture that has no taste spread over the surface of the tongue, for it takes on the taste of that which has taste and changes into it and connects to that nerve, and the nerve perceives it. And touch is a power diffused throughout all the skin and the flesh connected to it, by which one perceives heat, cold, moisture, dryness, hardness, softness, roughness, smoothness, lightness, and heaviness. And that which carries this power is a subtle body that flows in the network of nerves and draws from the heart and the brain. It only perceives when the quality of the skin changes into something like the perceived thing, provided the two differ in quality. And all these powers are instruments for receiving the emanation from the Bestower of Forms; otherwise the sense does not produce perception. And al-Ash'ari distinguished between hearing and sight and the remaining three [senses], for he required for these [two] here a physical connection, and he affirmed a physical sensation and did not affirm them in the attributes of God Most High. And when he knew that the two [hearing and sight] have a special relation to the inner [sense], he wavered in his statement as to whether perception is a specific knowledge or [occurs] in the common sensorium. And it is not improbable in his school that auditory perception has another meaning [attached] to sight, and visual perception [attached] to hearing, for the authorities ruled that they are united in the common sensorium so that the seen becomes read and the read becomes seen. For they do not differ in the reality of perception, and their locus is one, so they are unified. And the speech of the Mu'tazila concerning perceptions is confused, having no stability — and God knows best.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanisms of taste and touch, then presents al-Ash'ari's distinctive view on sensory perception and its implications. The author first describes taste: the tongue has a moisture that receives the flavor of food, transmits it to the nerve, which then perceives it. Then touch is described as a power spread throughout skin and flesh, perceiving various qualities. The vehicle of these senses is a subtle body flowing in nerves, drawing from heart and brain. Perception occurs when the quality of the skin changes to resemble the perceived object, with a condition of difference in degree. These powers are mere instruments for receiving emanation from the Bestower of Forms (واهب الصور); the sense itself does not produce perception. Then al-Ash'ari's position is explained: he separates hearing and sight from the other three senses (taste, touch, smell) by requiring physical contact for the latter but not for the former, and by affirming a physical sensation for the latter while not affirming them as attributes of God. However, he also recognized that hearing and sight have a special connection to the inner sense, so he wavered on whether perception is a specific knowledge or occurs in the common sensorium. The author notes that it is not improbable in Ash'ari's school that hearing and sight could exchange objects (e.g., seeing a sound) because they are unified in the common sensorium — the seen becomes read and vice versa, since they do not differ in the reality of perception and share a single locus. Finally, the author dismisses the Mu'tazilite view on perceptions as confused and unstable.

الحاسة
الإدراك
السمع
البصر
اللمس
الذوق
الحاسة المشتركة
واهب الصور
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'ari completely equates all senses, denying any distinction.
Correction: Ash'ari actually distinguishes hearing/sight from taste, touch, and smell by requiring physical contact only for the latter, but allows unification in the common sensorium.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did al-Ash'ari distinguish between hearing/sight and the other three senses?
A) He required physical contact for hearing and sight but not for the others.
B) He required physical contact for the other three but not for hearing and sight.
C) He denied that hearing and sight are real senses.
D) He affirmed hearing and sight as attributes of God but not the others.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari distinguished hearing and sight from touch, taste, and smell by requiring physical contact only for the latter, while affirming a unified reality of perception in the common sensorium.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ash'ari teaches that the senses are mere instruments; true perception comes from the Bestower of Forms. Do you live as though your senses give you ultimate truth, or do you recognize that God is the real source of all knowledge? How can you train yourself to rely on Him instead of your perceptions?
?
The passage says that in the common sensorium, the seen becomes read and the read becomes seen — perception is unified. In your own life, do you separate what you see from what you hear, missing the integrated truth? What step will you take today to unify your learning so that your faith is consistent across all experiences?
?
Al-Ash'ari himself wavered on the nature of perception. When you face uncertainty about matters of faith or knowledge, do you humbly admit your limits and turn to God, or do you cling to your own conclusions? How will you seek divine clarity this week on an issue where your senses or intellect fall short?

Mu'tazila Proof from Divine Wisdom
Mu'tazila proof from divine wisdom for benefit and most beneficial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila proof that God's wisdom necessitates all His actions are for benefit (ṣalāḥ) and the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ).

Core Idea
A wise God does everything with benefit and removes all obstacles.

This passage presents the foundational Mu'tazila argument that God's wisdom requires His actions to be beneficial. The proof begins with the premise that the Maker is wise, and a wise agent does not perform acts that invite questioning. Consequently, God removes all impediments, ensures capacity, and establishes recompense, leading to the conclusion that all divine actions, from creation to reward, are for benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Read the first sentence and notice the starting point: the Maker is wise (ḥakīm).
This is the key attribute from which everything follows.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow how the premise leads to God removing impediments, not charging beyond capacity, and establishing recompense.
Each step is necessary for the next.
3
Observe the conclusion
See that the proof concludes that all current and future divine actions are for benefit, most beneficial, or subtle grace, and recompense is reward, compensation, or favor.
This bridges to the definitions in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: والدليل على المذهبين أن الصانع حكيم، والحكيم لا يفعل فعلا يتوجه عليه سؤال ويلزم حجة، بل يزيح العلل كلها فلا يكلف نفسا إلا وسعها، ولا يتحقق الوسع إلا بإكمال العقل والإقدار على الفعل، ولا يتم الغرض من الفعل إلا بإثبات الجزاء ( وَلِتُجْزَى كُلُّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ ) فأصل التخليق والتكليف صلاح والجزاء صلاح وأبلغ ما يمكن في كل صلاح هو الأصلح، وزيادات الدواعي والصوارف والبواعث والزواجر في الشرع، وتقدير ألطاف بعضها خفي وبعضها جلي، فأفعال الله تعالى اليوم لا تخلو من صلاح وأصلح ولطف، وأفعال الله تعالى غدًا على سبيل الجزاء؛ إما ثواب أو عوض أو تفضل، فالصلاح ضد الفساد، وهو الفعل المتوجه إلى الخير من قوام العالم وبقاء النوع عاجلا، والمؤدي إلى السعادة السرمدية أجلا. والأصلح هو إذا صلاحان وخيران، فكان أحدهما أقرب إلى الخير المطلق فهو الأصلح، واللطف هو وجه التيسير إلى الخير، وهو الفعل الذي علم الرب تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده، وليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف وفعل لو فعله لآمن الكفار، ثم الثواب هو الجزاء على الأعمال الحسنة، والعوض هو البدل عن كالسلامة التي هي بدل الألم والنعيم الذي هو في مقابلة البلايا والرزايا والفتن، والتفضل هو اتصال منفعة خاصة إلى الغير من غير استحقاق يستحق بذلك حمداً وثناءً ومدحا وتعظيمًا، ووصف بأنه محسن مجمل، وإن لم يفعله لم يستوجب بذلك ملاماً وذما.
They said: The proof for the two schools is that the Maker is wise, and the wise does not do an act regarding which a question arises and a proof is required, but rather he removes all impediments, so He does not charge anyone except within his capacity, and capacity is not realized except by completing the intellect and enabling action, and the purpose of action is not accomplished except by establishing recompense: "and that every soul may be repaid for what it has earned" (Quran 45:22). So the origin of creation and obligation is benefit (salah), and recompense is benefit, and the utmost possible in every benefit is the most beneficial (aslah). And the increases of motives, deterrents, prompts, and restraints in the Law, and the estimation of subtle graces (alṭāf), some hidden and some manifest, so the actions of God, may He be exalted, today are not free from benefit, most beneficial, and subtle grace. And the actions of God tomorrow are by way of recompense: either reward (thawāb) or compensation (ʿiwaḍ) or favor (tafaḍḍul). Then benefit (ṣalāḥ) is the opposite of corruption, and it is the act directed towards good from the maintenance of the world and the preservation of the species in the near term, and leading to eternal happiness in the end. The most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) is when there are two benefits and two goods, and one of them is closer to absolute good, so it is the most beneficial. Subtle grace (luṭf) is the aspect of facilitation to good, and it is the act which the Lord, may He be exalted, knows that the servant will obey through it. And there is not within the power of God, may He be exalted, a grace and an act such that if He did it, the disbelievers would believe. Then reward (thawāb) is the recompense for good deeds. Compensation (ʿiwaḍ) is the substitute for, such as safety which is the substitute for pain, and the bliss which is in exchange for calamities, disasters, and trials. And favor (tafaḍḍul) is the connection of a specific benefit to another without deserving something whereby he deserves praise, commendation, laudation, and glorification, and he is described as a gracious benefactor, and if he does not do it, he does not thereby incur blame or censure.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage provides the Mu'tazila's proof for their two schools and defines key technical terms. It is a proof and definition passage. The proof begins with the premise that the Maker is wise (ḥakīm). A wise agent does not perform an act that invites questioning or requires justification; rather, He removes all impediments (ʿilal). This leads to the conclusion that God does not charge a soul beyond its capacity, and capacity is realized by completing intellect and enabling action. The purpose of action is only fulfilled by establishing recompense (jaza'), citing the Quran. Therefore, the origin of creation and obligation (taklif) is benefit (ṣalāḥ), and recompense is also benefit. The best possible benefit is the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ). The Law includes motives, deterrents, and subtle graces (alṭāf). The definitions follow: ṣalāḥ is the opposite of corruption, directed towards good for both worldly and eternal happiness. Aṣlaḥ is the better of two goods. Luṭf is an act that facilitates obedience, and there is no grace that would make disbelievers believe against their will. Thawāb is reward for good deeds; ʿiwaḍ is compensation for harms like pain; tafaḍḍul is gratuitous favor that deserves praise but not blame if omitted.

Key Terms
حكيم
صلاح
أصلح
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine wisdom means God's actions are arbitrary and need no ethical basis.
Correction: Mu'tazila argue that wisdom requires God to act for benefit and remove all impediments, establishing a moral framework.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the purpose of establishing recompense?
A) To punish
B) To complete the purpose of action
C) To test faith
D) To show mercy

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila derive their doctrine of benefit from the attribute of divine wisdom, concluding that all divine actions aim at the most beneficial outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's wisdom ensures that every trial you face is precisely calibrated to your capacity, what does that say about the moments when you feel crushed by hardship? How does this truth confront your impulse to complain or despair?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that God always does the most beneficial for you. Is there a specific situation in your life where you struggle to believe this? Write down what 'most beneficial' might mean in that situation, different from what you think is best. Are you willing to trust God's definition over your own?
?
If every divine command and prohibition is a grace (luṭf) meant to draw you closer, then which command do you find hardest to accept as a grace? What would change in your heart if you truly saw it as a 'hidden grace'?

Mu'tazila Definitions: Ṣalāḥ, Aṣlaḥ, Luṭf, Thawāb, ʿIwaḍ, Tafaḍḍul
Mu'tazila define six key ethical and theological terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise Mu'tazila definitions of ṣalāḥ, aṣlaḥ, luṭf, thawāb, ʿiwaḍ, and tafaḍḍul.

Core Idea
Six key terms define the Mu'tazila moral framework for divine actions.

After proving that God's actions are for benefit, the Mu'tazila define the terms that describe these benefits. They distinguish between benefit (ṣalāḥ), the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ), and subtle grace (luṭf) as categories of divine action in this world, and reward (thawāb), compensation (ʿiwaḍ), and favor (tafaḍḍul) as categories of recompense in the afterlife. These definitions are central to their ethical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions sequentially
The passage defines terms in order: ṣalāḥ, aṣlaḥ, luṭf, thawāb, ʿiwaḍ, tafaḍḍul. Read each definition carefully.
Note how each term is contrasted with something else (e.g., ṣalāḥ vs. corruption).
2
Understand ṣalāḥ and aṣlaḥ
ṣalāḥ is benefit directed at worldly and eternal good; aṣlaḥ is the better of two goods.
aṣlaḥ is comparative.
3
Grasp luṭf
Luṭf is facilitation to obedience, but note that there is no grace that would force belief.
This distinguishes Mu'tazila from determinism.
4
Distinguish the recompense terms
Thawāb is reward for good deeds, ʿiwaḍ is compensation for harm, and tafaḍḍul is unmerited favor.
These cover all types of divine recompense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: والدليل على المذهبين أن الصانع حكيم، والحكيم لا يفعل فعلا يتوجه عليه سؤال ويلزم حجة، بل يزيح العلل كلها فلا يكلف نفسا إلا وسعها، ولا يتحقق الوسع إلا بإكمال العقل والإقدار على الفعل، ولا يتم الغرض من الفعل إلا بإثبات الجزاء ( وَلِتُجْزَى كُلُّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ ) فأصل التخليق والتكليف صلاح والجزاء صلاح وأبلغ ما يمكن في كل صلاح هو الأصلح، وزيادات الدواعي والصوارف والبواعث والزواجر في الشرع، وتقدير ألطاف بعضها خفي وبعضها جلي، فأفعال الله تعالى اليوم لا تخلو من صلاح وأصلح ولطف، وأفعال الله تعالى غدًا على سبيل الجزاء؛ إما ثواب أو عوض أو تفضل، فالصلاح ضد الفساد، وهو الفعل المتوجه إلى الخير من قوام العالم وبقاء النوع عاجلا، والمؤدي إلى السعادة السرمدية أجلا. والأصلح هو إذا صلاحان وخيران، فكان أحدهما أقرب إلى الخير المطلق فهو الأصلح، واللطف هو وجه التيسير إلى الخير، وهو الفعل الذي علم الرب تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده، وليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف وفعل لو فعله لآمن الكفار، ثم الثواب هو الجزاء على الأعمال الحسنة، والعوض هو البدل عن كالسلامة التي هي بدل الألم والنعيم الذي هو في مقابلة البلايا والرزايا والفتن، والتفضل هو اتصال منفعة خاصة إلى الغير من غير استحقاق يستحق بذلك حمداً وثناءً ومدحا وتعظيمًا، ووصف بأنه محسن مجمل، وإن لم يفعله لم يستوجب بذلك ملاماً وذما.
They said: The proof for the two schools is that the Maker is wise, and the wise does not do an act regarding which a question arises and a proof is required, but rather he removes all impediments, so He does not charge anyone except within his capacity, and capacity is not realized except by completing the intellect and enabling action, and the purpose of action is not accomplished except by establishing recompense: "and that every soul may be repaid for what it has earned" (Quran 45:22). So the origin of creation and obligation is benefit (salah), and recompense is benefit, and the utmost possible in every benefit is the most beneficial (aslah). The most beneficial (aṣlaḥ) is when there are two benefits and two goods, and one of them is closer to absolute good, so it is the most beneficial. This passage provides the Mu'tazila's proof for their two schools and defines key technical terms. It is a proof and definition passage. The proof begins with the premise that the Maker is wise (ḥakīm). A wise agent does not perform an act that invites questioning or requires justification; rather, He removes all impediments (ʿilal). This leads to the conclusion that God does not charge a soul beyond its capacity, and capacity is realized by completing intellect and enabling action. The purpose of action is only fulfilled by establishing recompense (jaza'), citing the Quran. Therefore, the origin of creation and obligation (taklif) is benefit (ṣalāḥ), and recompense is also benefit. The best possible benefit is the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ). The Law includes motives, deterrents, and subtle graces (alṭāf). The definitions follow: ṣalāḥ is the opposite of corruption, directed towards good for both worldly and eternal happiness. Aṣlaḥ is the better of two goods. Luṭf is an act that facilitates obedience, and there is no grace that would make disbelievers believe against their will. Thawāb is reward for good deeds; ʿiwaḍ is compensation for harms like pain; tafaḍḍul is gratuitous favor that deserves praise but not blame if omitted.

Key Terms

أصلح
لطف
ثواب

تفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that luṭf (grace) obligates God to ensure everyone believes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that there is no act within God's power that would make disbelievers believe against their will.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila define ṣalāḥ as benefit, aṣlaḥ as superior benefit, luṭf as facilitation, thawāb as reward, ʿiwaḍ as compensation, and tafaḍḍul as unmerited favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God's luṭf is always present but never forces you. Where in your life today are you resisting His subtle guidance? What act of obedience are you avoiding because you want a sign?
?
You constantly enjoy tafaḍḍul—God's unearned favors. When was the last time you thanked Him for something you did not deserve? If you are honest, do you often feel entitled to His blessings?

Key Definitions: Ṣalāḥ, Luṭf, Thawāb, and Tafaḍḍul
Defines six theological terms and critiques Mu‘tazila analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise Ash'ari definitions of six key terms in the debate over divine justice, and why the Mu'tazila approach is considered anthropomorphic.

Core Idea
God's actions are not measured by human analogies.

This passage from Sanusi defines six essential terms that form the vocabulary of the Ash'ari position on divine justice and human responsibility. After defining each term, the author delivers a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila method, accusing them of anthropomorphism by relying on analogical reasoning from the created world to God. Pay close attention to how each term is carefully bounded to avoid implying obligation upon God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source once, then the literal translation. Note the flow from definitions to the final critique.
The Arabic contains some uncertain readings (e.g., 'الآمن', 'مستروح'), which may be textual corruptions.
2
Identify each defined term
Locate the definitions of ṣalāḥ, aṣlaḥ, luṭf, thawāb, ‘iwaḍ, and tafaḍḍul. Notice how each term is distinguished from the others.
These terms are often confused in popular discourse.
3
Understand the critique of Mu'tazila
The final sentence criticizes the Mu'tazila for relying solely on customary analogy (qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid). See why Sanusi calls them 'mushabbiha' in actions.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implications
Consider how these definitions protect against the notion that God is obligated (wājib) to do what is best (aṣlaḥ) or to reward/punish in a way that mirrors human justice.

الفساد يسمى صلاحا، وهو الفعل المتوجه إلى الخير من قوام العالم وبقاء النوع عاجلا، والمؤدي إلى السعادة السرمدية أجلا. والأصلح هو إذا صلاحان وخيران، فكان أحدهما أقرب إلى الخير المطلق فهو الأصلح، واللطف هو وجه التيسير إلى الخير، وهو الفعل الذي علم الرب تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده، وليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف وفعل لو فعله الآمن [؟] الكفار، ثم الثواب هو الجزاء على الأعمال الحسنة، والعوض هو البدل عن كالسلامة التي هي بدل الألم والنعيم الذي هو في مقابلة البلايا والرزايا والفتن، والتفضل هو اتصال منفعة خاصة إلى الغير من غير استحقاق يستحق بذلك حمدا وثناء ومدحا وتعظيما، ووصف بأنه محسن مجمل، وإن لم يفعله لم يستوجب بذلك ملاما وذما، وليس للمعتزلة فيما ذكروه مستند عقلي ولا مستروح [؟] شرعي إلا مجرد اعتبار العادة في الشاهد وتشنيعا [؟] معقولا، ثم اعتبار الغائب بالشاهد وهم في الحقيقة مشبهة في الأفعال.
Corruption is called soundness, and it is the act directed towards good from the maintenance of the world and the preservation of the species in the immediate term, and leading to eternal happiness in the hereafter. And the 'aslaḥ' (most sound) is when there are two soundnesses and two goods, so one of them is closer to the absolute good, so it is the most sound. And al-luṭf (grace) is the aspect of facilitation towards good, and it is the act which the Lord, exalted, knows that the servant will obey by means of it, and there is not within God's power a grace and an act which, if He did it, the disbelievers would be safe. Then al-thawāb (reward) is the recompense for good deeds, and al-‘iwaḍ (compensation) is the substitute for, like safety which is the substitute for pain and the bliss which is in exchange for trials and calamities and tribulations, and al-tafaḍḍul (benevolence) is the connection of a particular benefit to another without entitlement that would thereby deserve praise and commendation and laud and glorification, and he is described as a doer of good absolutely, and if he does not do it, he does not thereby incur blame and censure. And there is not for the Mu‘tazila in what they mentioned any rational basis nor any scriptural foundation except mere consideration of custom in the witnessed realm and a rational censure [?], then analogy of the unseen to the witnessed, and they are in reality anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) in actions.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines six key terms used in the debate between Ash‘ariyya and Mu‘tazila regarding divine action and justice. First, 'corruption is called soundness' — possibly a heading? Then defines al-ṣalāḥ as the act directed towards good that maintains the world and leads to eternal happiness. Al-aṣlaḥ is that which is closer to absolute good when two goods exist. Al-luṭf is the act by which God knows the servant will obey; it is not within God's power to do a grace that would make the disbelievers safe (implying that God does not do certain things because He knows they would not benefit). Then al-thawāb is reward for good deeds, al-‘iwaḍ is compensation for harms, and al-tafaḍḍul is a gratuitous benefit not owed. Finally, the passage criticizes the Mu‘tazila for lacking rational or scriptural basis for their doctrines other than analogy from the witnessed to the unseen and custom, accusing them of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) in actions.

?

?

General and Specific Meanings of Ni'mah and Rizq
Classifies ni'mah and rizq into general and specific definitions with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two complementary definitions of blessing (ni'mah) and provision (rizq) in Islamic theology, and why both are valid depending on context.

Core Idea
Ni'mah and rizq have both general and specific meanings—both are correct.

In this passage, the author clarifies that key theological terms like ni'mah (blessing) and rizq (provision) have two levels of meaning: a general sense encompassing all benefits or nourishment, and a specific sense tied to religious outcome and lawfulness. This distinction helps avoid confusion when interpreting Qur'anic verses and theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
Notice the two contrasting views introduced by 'من راعى فيهما عموماً' and 'ومن راعى فيهما خصوصاً'.
2
Identify the two perspectives
Find the general definition (umūm) and the specific definition (khuṣūṣ) for both ni'mah and rizq. Note how each is supported by Qur'anic verses.
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Look at the verses quoted: 17:83 and 11:6 for the general sense, and 23:55-56 and 2:254 for the specific sense. Ask how each verse illustrates the respective definition.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author ends by saying both views are correct when considering general vs. specific contexts. Reflect on why this distinction matters for theological discussion.
The key phrase: 'وكلا القولين صحيح إذا اعتبر فيهما الخصوص والعموم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول في النعمة والرزق قريب مما ذكرناه، فمن راعى فيهما عموما قال : النعمة كل ما ينعم به الإنسان في الحال والمال والرزق كل ما يتغذى به من الحلال والحرام، وقد سماها الله تعالى باسمها فقال : ( وَإِذَا أَنْعَمْنَا عَلَى الإِنسَنِ أَعْرَضَ وَنَا بِجَانِبِهِ )، ( وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللَّهِ رِزْقُهَا )
وم من راعى فيهما خصوصاً قال : النعمة في الحقيقة ما يكون محمود العاقبة وهو مقصور على الدين، قال الله تعالى فيهم : أيحسبون أن ما نمدهم به من مال وبنين نسارع لهم في الخيرات بل لا يشعرون.
والرزق في الحقيقة ما يكون مباحًا شرعًا ، قال الله تعالى : ( مِمَّا رَزَقْتَكُم مِّن )، والحرام لا يجوز الإنفاق منه، وكلا القولين صحيح إذا اعتبر فيهما الخصوص والعموم، ولا مشاحه في المواضعات
The discussion regarding blessing (ni‘ma) and provision (rizq) is close to what we have mentioned. Whoever considers them in a general sense (umūman) says: blessing is everything by which a human being is blessed (yun‘am) in state and wealth, and provision is everything by which one nourishes himself, whether lawful (ḥalāl) or unlawful (ḥarām). Allah has called them by their names and said: 'And when We bestow favor upon man, he turns away and distances himself' [17:83], and 'There is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision' [11:6]. And whoever considers them in a specific sense (khuṣūṣan) says: blessing in reality is that which has a praiseworthy consequence, and it is confined to religion. Allah said concerning them: 'Do they think that what We extend to them of wealth and children is that We hasten for them good things? Rather, they do not perceive' [23:55-56]. And provision in reality is that which is lawful (mubāḥ) according to the Law. Allah said: 'from what We have provided you (mimmā razaqnākum)' [2:254], and the unlawful cannot be spent from it. Both statements are correct when the specific and general are considered in them, and there is no contention in terminology (muwāḍa‘āt).
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the key terms ni‘ma (blessing) and rizq (provision) from two valid perspectives: general (umūm) and specific (khuṣūṣ). The author first presents the general view: ni‘ma is any benefit in state or wealth, and rizq is any nourishment, whether lawful or unlawful. He supports this with Qur'anic verses that use these terms in a broad sense (e.g., 17:83 for ni‘ma and 11:6 for rizq). Then he presents the specific view, which restricts ni‘ma to that which has a good outcome in the Hereafter, namely religious blessings, and restricts rizq to what is lawful (ḥalāl) according to the Sharia. This is supported by verses that distinguish true blessing from mere worldly gifts (23:55-56) and the requirement that rizq be from lawful means (2:254). The author concludes that both definitions are correct provided one takes into account whether the context is general or specific. He adds that there is no need for dispute over these conventional definitions (muwāḍa‘āt), implying that both meanings are lexically and legally acceptable depending on usage.

Correction: The passage clarifies that both general and specific definitions are valid in their proper contexts, and that there is no contention in terminology.

Key Takeaway

Evidence for the Imam from the Consensus of the Companions
Author cites historical consensus to prove wajib al-imama

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the practice of the Companions—especially the succession of the Caliphs—provides a decisive proof for the obligation of having an Imam.

Core Idea
The unanimous practice of the Companions proves the imamate is an obligation.

This lesson examines a key argument from the early community: the historical record of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The author, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, argues that the Companions' consistent establishment of a leader after each death shows they considered an Imam essential. This consensus (ijma) constitutes a decisive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical narrative
The passage recounts the succession of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali. Notice how each transition involved consultation and appointment.
The author uses these specific examples to build his case.
2
Identify the core argument
The author infers that because the Companions never left the community without a leader, they must have considered an Imam obligatory.
This is an inductive argument from practice to belief.
3
Consider the supporting evidence
The suspension of legal judgments during interregnums further shows the Companions' commitment to having an Imam.
4
Draw the conclusion
This consensus, maintained across generations, is a decisive proof for the obligation of the imamate.
The author calls it a 'decisive proof' (dalil qati').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما قرب وفاة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه فقال : تشاوروا في هذا الأمر، ثم وصف عمر بصفاته وعهد إليه واستقر الأمر عليه، وما دار في قلبه ولا في قلب أحد أن يجوز خلو الأرض عن إمام، ولما قربت وفاة عمر رضي الله عنه جعل الأمر شوری بين ستة ، وكان الاتفاق على عثمان رضي الله عنه، وبعد ذلك كان الاتفاق على علي رضي الله عنه، فدل ذلك كله (۸) على أن الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم وهم الصدر الأول كانوا على بكرة أبيهم (۱) متفقين على أنه لا بد من إمام، ويدل على ذلك إجماعهم على التوقف (۱۱) في الأحكام عند موت الإمام إلى أن يقوم إمام آخر، ومن ذلك الزمان إلى زماننا كانت الإمامة على المنهاج الأول عصراً بعد عصر (1) من إمام إلى إمام إما بإجماع من الأمة، أو بعهد ووصية، وإما بهما جميعا، فذلك الإجماع على هذا الوجه دليل قاطع على وجوب الإمامة العالم، هذا كلامنا في وجوب الإمامة على الإطلاق.
When the death of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, drew near, he said: 'Consult about this matter.' Then he described 'Umar with his qualities and appointed him, and the matter was settled upon him. Nothing occurred in his heart nor in the heart of anyone [thinking] that it is permissible for the earth to be without an Imam. When the death of 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, drew near, he made the matter a consultation among six, and agreement was upon 'Uthman, may God be pleased with him, and after that agreement was upon 'Ali, may God be pleased with him. So all of this indicates that the Companions, may God be pleased with them, who are the first generation, were all of one accord agreeing that an Imam is necessary. This is also indicated by their consensus to suspend judgments at the death of the Imam until another Imam arises. From that time until our time, the imamate has been upon the first method, age after age, from Imam to Imam, either by consensus of the community, or by a covenant and will, or by both together. So this consensus in this manner is a decisive proof for the obligation of the imamate for the world. This is our speech on the absolute obligation of the imamate.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a historical proof for the necessity of the Imam (wujub al-imama). The author argues from the practice of the early Muslim community, particularly the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The key points: (1) When Abu Bakr was nearing death, he did not leave the community without a leader; rather, he consulted and appointed 'Umar, and no one considered it permissible for the earth to be without an Imam. (2) 'Umar similarly set up a consultative committee (Shura) of six, and they agreed on 'Uthman, and later the community agreed on 'Ali. (3) This shows that the Companions (al-sahaba) were unanimously agreed that an Imam is indispensable. (4) Further evidence is their consensus to suspend legal judgments (tawaqquf fi al-ahkam) during an interregnum until a new Imam is appointed, proving they considered it a necessity. (5) This pattern continued after them, with imams succeeding one another either by consensus of the community, or by a preceding testament (ahd wa wasiyya), or both. Thus, the consensus of the community on this method constitutes a decisive proof (dalil qati') for the obligation of the imamate for the entire world. The passage concludes by stating that this is the author's position on the absolute obligation of the imamate.

وجوب الإمامة

Proof that a Finite Body Cannot Have Infinite Parts
Reductio proof against infinite parts in a finite body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a finite body cannot contain an infinite number of parts, and why this refutes the possibility of actual infinity in created things.

Core Idea
A finite body with infinite parts leads to logical absurdity.

The kalam tradition uses rational arguments to establish limits on what can exist. One key proof shows that a finite body cannot contain an infinite number of parts, because halving it would lead to impossible consequences. This passage from Al-Juwayni presents a concise reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the argument unfold in its own terms before analyzing it.
Notice the compact style: every phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The author assumes the opposite (finite body has infinite parts) and derives two absurd consequences. Find the two impossibilities he identifies.
The argument uses halving as a thought experiment.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this proof matters for theology: it shows that created bodies are limited and cannot host actual infinities. This protects the distinction between Creator and creation.
Potential infinite division is not the issue; actual infinite parts are.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانفصاله يجب أن يكون كذلك، وأيضا فإن المقدار الذي اشتمل عليه الجسم متناه فلو نصف الجسم نصفين، وكان أحد المقدارين يقبل التجزي إلى غير النهاية حتى يصير ذا مقادير بغير نهاية فيلزم أن يكون الأقل مثل الأكثر وهو الجملة ويلزم أن يكون فيما لا يتناهى من المقادير تفاوت الأقل والأكثر، وكلاهما محال.
So its separation must be likewise. And also: the quantity which the body contains is finite. For if we halve the body into halves, and one of the two quantities admits division to infinity until it becomes quantities without end, then it would follow that the lesser is like the greater, which is the whole; and it would follow that there is, among infinite quantities, a difference between lesser and greater—both of which are impossible.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage contains a proof by reductio ad absurdum against the possibility of a finite body containing an infinite number of parts. The author begins by noting that a finite body must have a finite quantity. He then imagines halving that body repeatedly. If one of the halves were divisible infinitely, it would produce an infinite number of quantities. But then the original half (the lesser) would equal the whole body (the greater), since both would be infinite. Moreover, among infinite quantities, the distinction 'less and more' would still apply, which is absurd because infinite cannot admit of more or less. Both consequences are impossible, so the premise—that a finite body has infinite parts—is false. The argument relies on the principle that an actual infinite cannot be contained in a finite magnitude.

Key Terms
متناه (finite)
غير النهاية (infinite)
أقل (lesser)
أكثر (greater)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because a line can be divided infinitely in theory, a finite body could actually have an infinite number of parts.
Correction: The proof shows that actual infinite parts in a finite body lead to logical absurdities. Potential infinite division (as in mathematics) does not entail actual infinite parts.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence that results from assuming a finite body has parts divisible to infinity?
A) The body would become non-existent.
B) The lesser part would equal the greater whole.
C) The body would become infinite in size.
D) Division would stop at an indivisible atom.

Key Takeaway
A finite body cannot contain an infinite number of actual parts because that would lead to the absurdity that a part equals the whole and that infinite quantities admit of more and less.

?

?

Objection of Potential Parts and Its Refutation
Objection that parts are potential not actual, and the reply showing actualization leads to absurdity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction between actual and potential parts does not allow for an infinite within a finite body, and how the reply uses a dilemma to refute the objection.

Core Idea
Potential infinite parts do not escape actual infinity's absurdity.

In this passage, an objector tries to save the possibility of infinite parts by appealing to potentiality. But the reply shows that if potential parts cannot become actual, they are mere imagination; if they can, actualizing them yields the same contradiction. This tight argument uses a classic dilemma to close a loophole.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector concedes a finite body has no actual infinite parts, but claims it has potentially infinite parts. Understand the distinction between bi-l-fiʿl (actual) and bi-l-quwwa (potential).
The key term 'bi-l-quwwa' means potential or in power.
2
Follow the reply's dilemma
The reply asks: can these potential parts come into actuality? Two branches: if no, then impossibility is exposed; if yes, actualizing them leads to the same absurdity (the finite containing infinite).
Notice the logical structure: a dilemma that eliminates both options.
3
Examine the analogy
The reply adds an analogy: any body can have another body appended in imagination without end, but this does not prove an actually infinite body exists. Potential extension is not actual infinity.
Imagination (al-wahm) is contrasted with rational proof (burhān al-ʿaql).
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the objection fails: potential parts are irrelevant because they either cannot be actualized (mere fantasy) or can be (leading to contradiction). The potential infinite does not rescue the doctrine from absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ما ذكرتموه صحيح في تقدير أجزاء الجسم بالفعل، فإن الجسم المتناهي لا يشتمل على أجزاء بالفعل غير متناهية لكنا نقول: هو يشتمل على أجزاء بالقوة غير متناهية، فالمحصور في الفعل لا يحصر غير المتناهي بالفعل، فلم لا يجوز أن يحصر غير المتناهي بالقوة وفيه النزاع؟ قلنا: ما قدرتموه بالقوة أيجوز في العقل أن يخرج إلى الفعل، فإن لم يجز ظهرت الاستحالة وبقي الوهم المجرد الذي دل على خلافه برهان العقل أو جاز خروجه إلى الفعل، وقدرنا خروجه إلى الفعل لزم المحال الذي ذكرنا هذا، كما يقال ما من جسم إلا ويمكن أن يتصل به جسم آخر، بحيث لا يقف الوهم إلى حد ونهاية، ثم لا يدل ذلك على أن جسما لا يتناهى متصور الوجود، بل الاتصال في الجسم يجوز إلى حد لا إلى هذا المحال، وهو تصور جسم لا يتناهى وإن بقي الوهم في حال التقدير الوهمي.
If it is said: 'What you have mentioned is correct with respect to estimating the parts of the body actually (bi-l-fiʿl), for a finite body does not contain actually infinite parts, but we say: it contains parts potentially (bi-l-quwwa) infinite. So what is limited actually does not contain what is actually infinite, so why may it not contain what is potentially infinite, and therein lies the dispute?' We say: 'What you have estimated as potential—is it intellectually possible for it to come into actuality? If it is not possible, then impossibility appears and there remains bare imagination, which the rational proof has shown to be contrary. Or if its coming into actuality is possible, and we suppose it comes into actuality, then the absurdity we mentioned follows. This is like it is said: there is no body but that another body can be connected to it, such that imagination does not stop at a limit or end; yet that does not indicate that an infinite body is conceivable in existence. Rather, connection in a body is permissible only to a limit, not to this impossibility—namely, conceiving a body without end, even if imagination remains in the state of imaginative estimation.'
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objector distinguishes between actual (bi-l-fiʿl) and potential (bi-l-quwwa) parts. He concedes that a finite body has no actual infinite parts, but claims it has potentially infinite parts. He asks: why can't a finite body contain something potentially infinite? The reply uses a dilemma: either those potential parts can become actual or not. If they cannot become actual, then the impossibility is exposed, and the mere imagination (al-wahm al-mujarrad) is refuted by rational proof. If they can become actual, then we suppose them actual, and the same absurdity (the lesser equaling the greater) follows. The reply then draws an analogy: any body can have another body appended to it ad infinitum in imagination, but this does not prove an actually infinite body; it only shows potential extension, not actual infinity. Thus, the potential infinite does not yield a real infinite in the finite.

Key Terms
بالفعل (actual)
بالقوة (potential)
الوهم (imagination)
برهان العقل (rational proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potential infinite parts in a finite body pose no problem because they are not actual.
Correction: The reply shows that if the potential parts cannot become actual, they are mere imagination, contradicting rational proof. If they can become actual, the finite body would then have actual infinite parts, leading to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities the reply examines for potential parts?
A) They are either philosophical or theological.
B) They either can come into actuality or cannot.
C) They are either finite or infinite in number.
D) They either exist or do not exist.

Key Takeaway
The potential infinite in a finite body does not escape the rational impossibility of an actual infinite, as shown by the dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical contradiction in your beliefs, do you rely on imagination (al-wahm) to soothe your doubts, or do you submit to rational proof (burhān al-ʿaql) even when it is uncomfortable? Identify one belief you hold that may be based on imagination rather than evidence, and commit to examining it today.
?
The objector hid behind 'potential' to avoid facing the absurdity of infinite parts. In your life, where do you use 'potential' as an excuse to delay repentance, obedience, or decisive action? What is one area where you can turn that potential into actual commitment starting now?

Imagination of void beyond the world is irrational
Critique of imagining a void beyond the finite world as contrary to reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why imagining a void beyond the finite world leads to an irrational empty void, according to kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Imagining an infinite void beyond the world contradicts reason.

This passage from a classical kalam text addresses a key argument for the finitude of the world. The author points out that if you try to picture a void beyond the world's boundary, you end up either stopping at that boundary or positing an endless empty space—the latter being contrary to reason. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the author describes the process of imagination.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the steps: imagine a limit for the world, then estimate a space beyond it, then consider the consequence of not stopping—leading to an empty void.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that positing an empty void is contrary to reason and its proof. This supports the world's finitude.
Think about why an infinite void is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك مما يتمثل في الوهم من خلا خارج العالم، فإنه يتوهم حدا ونهاية للعالم، ثم يقدر فضاء وخلاء ينتهي به وإلا فيثبت خلاء فارغا، وذلك خلاف العقل وبرهانه.
And that is among what is represented in imagination from the void outside the world: for it imagines a limit and an end for the world, then it estimates a space and void that terminates it; otherwise, it would posit an empty void, and that is contrary to reason and its proof.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage criticizes the imagination of a void (khala') beyond the finite world. The author states that the imagination (al-wahm) pictures a boundary and end for the world, and then supposes a space or void that the world terminates at. If one does not stop there, one ends up positing a completely empty void. But this is contrary to reason (ʿaql) and its proof (burhān). The implication is that positing an infinite void is irrational; the world must have a boundary without an actual void beyond it. This argument supports the finitude of the world and rejects the idea of an infinite empty space, which was a philosophical position.

Key Terms
خلا (void)
وهم (imagination)
خلاء فارغ (empty void)
برهان (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the passage denies the very possibility of imagining a void.
Correction: The passage does not deny that one can imagine a void; it argues that such imagination leads to an irrational conclusion (an empty void), which reason rejects.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say results from imagining a void beyond the world?
A) An infinite regress of voids.
B) Positing an empty void, which is contrary to reason.
C) The world becomes infinite.
D) The void is filled with atoms.

Key Takeaway
Imagining a void beyond the world leads to an empty void, which reason finds unacceptable, thereby supporting the world's finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Imam al-Haramayn's Geometric Proof of the Indivisible Atom
Imam al-Haramayn's geometric proof for the indivisible atom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's geometric argument for the indivisible atom and how it uses the nature of contact and boundaries to establish atomism.

Core Idea
Contact between a sphere and a plane reveals the necessity of an indivisible point.

In this lesson, we examine a distinctive proof from Imam al-Haramayn that uses geometry to argue for atomism. Rather than relying on physical observation, he uses a thought experiment about a true sphere touching a true plane. The reasoning shows that finite extension requires indivisible constituents. Let us follow his logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup: true sphere and true plane
Imam al-Haramayn asks us to imagine a perfectly spherical ball and a perfectly flat surface. This is an idealised thought experiment, not a physical measurement.
The terms 'true sphere' and 'true plane' mean geometrically perfect.
2
Analyse the contact scenario
When the sphere touches the plane, does it make contact over a divisible area or an indivisible point? If the contact area were divisible, the sphere would be flattened, contradicting its perfection. Therefore the contact must be at an indivisible point – the atom.
This is the core inference: for a true sphere, contact cannot be divisible.
3
Apply the same reasoning to boundaries
The proof then extends to any finite body bounded by a line. The boundary line has length but no breadth. If the line were divisible in breadth, it would have thickness; if divisible in length, it would divide into points. Points are indivisible, and that is the atom.
This shows the argument works for one-dimensional and two-dimensional boundaries too.
4
Grasp the general conclusion
Imam al-Haramayn concludes that every finite thing must terminate in something indivisible – a surface, line, or point – otherwise it would have no end. This indivisible terminus is the jawhar fard of the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسلك إمام الحرمين في إثبات الجزء الفرد مسلكا آخر فقال: نفرض الكلام في كرة حقيقية وبسيط حقيقي، ونضرب بالكرة على البسيط، أفيلاقيه أم لا؟ فإن لاقاه أفيلاقيه بمنقسم أم لا؟ فإن لاقاه بمنقسم فليست كرة، بل هو بسيط، وإن لاقاه بغير منقسم فذلك الجوهر الفرد، ويمكن أن يطرد هذا البرهان أيضا في البسيط المتناهي بحده، فإن الحد خط والخط طول لا عرض له فقد تناهى الجسم، فإن كان الحد الذي يتناهى به منقسما لم يكن خطا، وإن لم ينقسم عرضا وانقسم طولا فينقسم إلى نقط وهو أمر لا ينقسم، وذلك هو الجزء الفرد عند المتكلم، وعلى كل حال متناءٍ فإنما يتناهى بأمر لا ينقسم سطحا وخطا ونقطة وإلا لم يكن نهاية.
Imam al-Haramayn pursues another method in proving the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). He said: Let us suppose a true sphere (kura ḥaqīqiyya) and a true plane (basīṭ ḥaqīqī), and strike the sphere upon the plane. Does it touch it or not? If it touches, does it touch by something divisible or not? If it touches by something divisible, then it is not a sphere; rather it is a plane. And if it touches by something indivisible, then that is the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). This proof can also be applied to a finite plane by its boundary, for the boundary is a line, and the line has length without breadth—thus the body is finite. If the boundary by which it is finite is divisible, it would not be a line; and if it is not divisible in breadth but divisible in length, then it divides into points, which is something indivisible, and that is the indivisible atom according to the mutakallim. In any case, every finite thing is finite only by something that is indivisible as surface, line, or point; otherwise it would have no end.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Haramayn's geometric proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The proof proceeds in two stages. First, consider a true sphere (perfectly round) and a true plane (perfectly flat). When the sphere touches the plane, it must do so either at a divisible region or an indivisible point. If the contact area were divisible, then the sphere would be flattened at that region, contradicting its sphericity; hence the contact must be at an indivisible point, which is the atom. Second, the same reasoning applies to any finite body bounded by a line. The boundary line has length but no breadth; if it were divisible transversely, it would have breadth and not be a line. If it is divisible longitudinally, it divides into points, which are indivisible. Thus every finite boundary terminates in something indivisible—a point, which is the atom. The passage concludes that for any finite thing, its terminus must be indivisible, otherwise it would not have a definite end. This argument is a standard kalām proof for atomism.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد (indivisible atom)
كرة (sphere)
بسيط (plane)
منقسم (divisible)
نقطة (point)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof assumes actual physical contact; it is a geometric thought experiment about necessary conditions for finite extension.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn is reasoning about the logical requirements of a true sphere and true plane, not about empirical measurement. The argument is a priori, not empirical.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn's proof, what does a sphere touching a plane by something divisible imply?
A) The sphere is actually an atom.
B) The sphere is not a true sphere.
C) The plane is infinite.
D) The contact is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn's geometric argument shows that finite extension necessarily terminates in indivisible points, the jawhar fard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Haramayn used pure reason to prove something about the physical world. Do you rely more on revelation or reason when forming your beliefs about unseen realities? What might be the danger of neglecting either?
?
The proof insists that finite things must have an indivisible endpoint. Your life in this world is finite. Are you living as if your days will truly end, or do you act as if you have infinite time? What indivisible point is your life heading toward?
?
If the smallest particle of matter is indivisible, what does that tell you about the precision and power of the One who created it? How does that change the way you view the objects around you today?

Al-Juwayni's Proof for the Indivisible Atom
Al-Juwayni proves the indivisible atom via contact of sphere and simple body, and via boundary analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Juwayni's two arguments for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-juz' al-fard) and why contact between a sphere and a simple body necessitates an indivisible point.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom is proven by the geometric necessity of contact.

Al-Juwayni presents a thought experiment using a sphere and a simple body to argue that contact must occur at an indivisible point, which he identifies as the indivisible atom. He then extends the argument to the boundaries of finite bodies, showing that lines and surfaces ultimately resolve into indivisible points. This lesson will guide you through these two interlocking proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Note that the proof has two parts: sphere contact and boundary analysis.
2
Understand the sphere argument
Consider the hypothetical: a true sphere contacting a true simple body. If the contact is at a divisible point, the sphere would not be truly spherical because a true sphere contacts at a single point. Therefore, contact must be at an indivisible point, proving the atom.
This is the core of the first argument.
3
Understand the boundary argument
Every finite body has a boundary (a line). A line is length without width. If the boundary were divisible widthwise, it would not be a line. If divisible lengthwise, it divides into points, which are indivisible. Thus, finiteness implies indivisible points.
This parallels the sphere argument.
4
Connect to the indivisible atom
Both arguments converge on the same conclusion: there exist indivisible units (atoms) that are the ultimate constituents of matter. In kalam, these are called al-juz' al-fard.
5
Reflect on the significance
These proofs are foundational for the occasionalist cosmology of the Ash'ari school. They defend the idea that matter is not infinitely divisible, which supports the doctrine of continuous creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسلك إمام الحرمين في إثبات الجزء الفرد مسلكا آخر فقال: نفرض الكلام في كرة حقيقية وبسيط حقيقي، ونضرب بالكرة على البسيط، أفيلاقيه أم لا؟ فإن لاقاه أفيلاقيه بمنقسم أم لا؟ فإن لاقاه بمنقسم فليست كرة، بل هو بسيط، وإن لاقاه بغير منقسم فذلك الجوهر الفرد، ويمكن أن يطرد هذا البرهان أيضا في البسيط المتناهي بحده، فإن الحد خط والخط طول لا عرض له فقد تناهى الجسم، فإن كان الحد الذي يتناهى به منقسماً لم يكن خطا، وإن لم ينقسم عرضا وانقسم طولا فينقسم إلى نقط وهو أمر لا ينقسم، وذلك هو الجزء الفرد عند المتكلم، وعلى كل حال متناءٍ فإنما يتناهى بأمر لا ينقسم سطحا وخطا ونقطة وإلا لم يكن نهاية.
And Imam al-Haramayn follows another path in proving the indivisible atom (al-juz' al-fard). He said: Let us hypothesize a true sphere and a true simple body, and we strike the sphere upon the simple body; does it contact it or not? If it contacts it, does it contact it with a divisible [point] or not? If it contacts it with a divisible [point], then it is not a sphere but is simple; and if it contacts it with an indivisible [point], then that is the indivisible atom. And this proof can also be applied to the simple body that is finite by its boundary, for the boundary is a line, and a line is length without width, so the body is finite. If the boundary by which it is finite is divisible, it would not be a line; and if it is not divisible in width but is divisible in length, then it divides into points, which are indivisible things, and that is the indivisible atom according to the mutakallimun. And in every case of finiteness, it is finite only by something that is indivisible in surface, line, and point; otherwise it would have no end.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Haramayn's (al-Juwayni) proof for the existence of the indivisible atom (al-juz' al-fard). The author begins by describing a thought experiment: take a true sphere and a true simple body (a flat surface). The sphere is struck upon the simple body. The question is whether they make contact, and if so, whether the contact is at a divisible point or an indivisible point. If the contact is at a divisible point, then the sphere would not be a true sphere but a simple body (since a sphere contacts at a single point if truly spherical). If the contact is at an indivisible point, that point is the indivisible atom. The proof is then extended to a simple body that is finite or bounded. The boundary of a body is a line, which has length but no width. If that boundary were divisible, it would not be a line (since a line must be continuous in length but indivisible in width). If it is not divisible in width but divisible in length, then the line divides into points, which are indivisible. Those points are the indivisible atoms according to the mutakallimun. The conclusion is that anything finite must end in something indivisible (whether surface, line, or point); otherwise it would have no end, implying infinite divisibility and contradicting finiteness.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد (al-juz' al-fard)
كرة (sphere)
بسيط (simple body)
حَدّ (boundary)
نقطة (point)
خط (line)
سطح (surface)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisible atom is a physical speck of dust that can be seen or handled, like a tiny particle of matter.
Correction: In kalam, the indivisible atom (al-juz' al-fard) is a theoretical unit of division that has no physical extension; it is a conceptual limit that is not itself divisible. It is not a tiny physical object.

Checkpoint
What is the key conclusion from al-Juwayni's sphere-and-simple-body argument?
A) The sphere is divisible into parts.
B) The contact must be at an indivisible point, proving the indivisible atom.
C) The simple body must be spherical.
D) The boundary of a body is always a line.

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni's proof establishes that contact and finiteness logically require indivisible units, known as al-juz' al-fard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the boundaries of your own existence—your life, your body, your resources—do you ever think of the final indivisible point that marks its end? Al-Juwayni's proof shows that everything finite ends in an indivisible unit. How does that truth change the way you view the finite span of your life? Before you move on, commit to one concrete action today that acknowledges your life's finiteness: write down the single most important thing you must do before you die, and take a step toward it today.
?
The proof demands that contact between a sphere and a surface must be at a single indivisible point. In your relationships, do you ever try to 'divide' your attention or commitment, thinking you can have contact that is divisible? This lesson says true contact is indivisible—you are either fully present or not. Is there a relationship where you need to replace fragmented attention with a complete, undivided point of contact? Decide today to give someone your full, undivided presence for a specified time, no distractions.
?
Al-Juwayni argues that without an indivisible atom, nothing would have an end—everything would be infinitely divisible, never reaching completion. In your spiritual journey, are you hiding behind the idea that you can always 'divide' your repentance or your efforts indefinitely, never giving a decisive turning point? The lesson forces you to see that finiteness requires a final, indivisible act. What is the one specific sin, habit, or shortcoming you have been putting off by 'dividing' your resolve? intermediate

Objection and Response on Surfaces, Lines, Points as Accidents
Objection that surfaces, lines, points are accidents and response showing they divide with the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun respond to the objection that surfaces, lines, and points are indivisible accidents, thereby defending the possibility of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Accidents divide with their substrate; the indivisible atom is an end, not a body with ends.

The objection attacks a key argument for the indivisible atom by claiming that surfaces, lines, and points are accidents and therefore cannot be divided. The response cleverly concedes that they are accidents but shows that accidents divide when their substrate divides. It also reinterprets the indivisible atom as an end or boundary of a body, not as a composite thing with its own parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage (فإن قيل...) which presents the objection that surfaces, lines, and points are accidents and thus indivisible, and that the indivisible atom claimed by the mutakallimun has volume and area, making it unlike those accidents.
Notice the claim that what is an accident does not admit division.
2
Identify the response's starting point
The response begins with 'قلنا' (We said). It concedes the premise that these are accidents but challenges the conclusion that they do not divide.
The response does not deny the objection's premise.
3
Understand how accidents divide with the body
The response explains that these accidents divide according to the dimensions of the body: point divides in none, line in length, surface in length and width, body in all three dimensions. This shows that accidents are divisible when their substrate divides.
Division here is conceptual – the accident follows the substrate's division.
4
Grasp the analogy with 'ends'
The response ends by comparing the indivisible atom to the 'ends' (نهايات) of a body. Just as surface, line, point are called ends of the body, the atom is an end or limit, not something that itself has ends.
This reframes the atom as a boundary, not a miniature body.
5
Reflect on the argument's effectiveness
Consider how this response defends the indivisible atom by showing that divisibility of accidents does not refute the existence of an indivisible limit. The objection fails because it assumes accidents are absolutely indivisible.
The argument relies on the principle that accidents follow their substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: السطح والخط والنقطة أعراض عندنا فالنقطة عرض الخط، والخط عرض في السطح، والسطح عرض في الجسم، وما كان عرضا لا يقبل الوصف بالتجزئة، والجوهر الفرد عندكم حجم له جثة ومساحة، فلذلك لزمكم ما لا يلزمنا في أبعاده الثلاثة، قلنا: هب أنه أعراض قائمة بالجسم، أليست هي أعراضا تنقسم بانقسام الجسم، فإن النقطة على أصلكم شيء ما لا تنقسم، والخط ينقسم طولا ولا ينقسم عرضا، والسطح ينقسم طولا وعرضا ولا ينقسم عمقا، والجسم ينقسم طولا وعرضا وعمقا، وكما أن الأعراض تنقسم بانقسام المحل وتتحد باتحاد المحل، ويتحد المحل باتحاده، وكما أنكم سميتم هذه الأعراض نهايات الجسم، كذلك الجوهر عندنا أطراف ونهايات وليست بذي أطراف كما سنبين.
For it was said: Surface, line, and point are accidents according to us, for the point is an accident of the line, the line is an accident of the surface, and the surface is an accident of the body. And whatever is an accident does not admit description with division. And the indivisible atom according to you has a volume, a bulk and area; therefore, what is necessary for you is not necessary for us regarding its three dimensions. We said: Suppose they are accidents subsisting in the body. Do they not divide with the division of the body? For the point according to your own principle is something that does not divide, the line divides in length but not in width, the surface divides in length and width but not in depth, and the body divides in length, width, and depth. And just as accidents divide with the division of the substrate and unite with the union of the substrate, and the substrate unites with its union, and just as you have named these accidents 'ends' of the body, similarly the atom according to us is ends and limits and is not something that itself has ends, as we will explain.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response regarding the status of surfaces, lines, and points. The objector argues that these are accidents and thus cannot be divided or composed of parts; therefore, they cannot serve as evidence for an indivisible substance. The objector claims that the mutakallimun's indivisible atom has volume and area, which makes it unlike these accidents, and so the argument from boundaries does not apply to them. The response (beginning with 'قلنا') argues that even if these are accidents, they still divide with the division of the body they inhere in. The point does not divide, but the line divides in length, the surface in length and width, and the body in all three dimensions. As accidents, they are subject to the division of their substrate. The response further argues that just as these accidents are called 'ends' of the body, the indivisible atom is an end or limit of the body without itself having ends. Thus the objection fails to show that the indivisible atom is impossible.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
نقطة (point)
خط (line)
سطح (surface)
محل (substrate)
أبعاد (dimensions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accidents cannot divide at all.
Correction: The response clarifies that accidents divide when their substrate divides; they are not absolutely indivisible.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that surfaces, lines, and points are accidents that do not admit division?
A) He denies that they are accidents.
B) He argues that accidents divide with the division of the body.
C) He claims the indivisible atom is not an accident.
D) He says the point is not a real thing.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom is defended as an end or limit of the body, not a composite body, and accidents divide when their substrate divides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed atoms must have volume like bodies. The responder corrected by showing atoms are ends, not composites. In your own life, have you misjudged something by applying the wrong category? What assumption about Allah or creation might you be unknowingly making that needs correction?
?
The response uses careful logical distinctions (dimensions of division) to clarify a theological point. When you face a challenge to your faith, do you take the time to analyze the argument deeply, or do you react emotionally? Commit to studying one objection thoroughly this week.
?
The indivisible atom is a limit—it does not itself have parts. How does this reframe your understanding of divine attributes like Allah's oneness? Are you tempted to imagine attributes as separate parts, when they are really indivisible aspects of the same reality?

Objection: A Substance Between Two Substances
Objection against indivisible atom using contact dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma raised by the objection against the indivisible atom: if a substance is between two others, it must be able to contact both without contradiction, which seems to require divisibility.

Core Idea
A substance between two neighbors forces a choice: divisibility or absurdity.

The mutakallimun's opponents often challenged the idea of indivisible atoms (al-jawhar al-fard) with clever scenarios. This passage presents one such objection: imagine a single substance sandwiched between two others. How can it contact both neighbors without either using the same part for both (which is impossible) or different parts (which implies division)? The objection sets up a dialectical trap that atomists must escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection scenario
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector sets up a hypothetical situation: a substance located between two other substances. The question is about how it contacts its neighbors.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logic.
2
Understand the first dilemma
The objector asks: does the substance contact the right neighbor with exactly the same part as the left neighbor, or with a different part? If the same, that's absurd (sophistry). If different, then the substance has multiple parts and is divisible. This is the core of the objection.
This dilemma strikes at the heart of indivisibility.
3
Analyze the second dilemma
The objector then asks about the manner of contact: does the substance contact one neighbor with all of itself or only a part? If with all, there is no remaining part to contact the other neighbor. If with a part, again division is proven. This reinforces the first dilemma.
Note how the objector systematically closes all escape routes.
4
Examine the expanded scenario
The passage then presents a more detailed picture: two parallel lines of six parts each, with substances at the ends, moving with equal speed. The objector argues that this motion will cause one substance to surpass the other, implying contact and thus division. The passage ends here, but the reasoning is clear.
This scenario aims to show that contact and motion force divisibility.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why this objection matters. If unanswered, it would refute the atomist doctrine. As you read, think about how a mutakallim might respond, and note that the following pages likely contain the response.
The objection is a test of the coherence of the atomist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما شبههم قالوا لو قدرنا جوهرًا بين جوهرين أفيلاقي ما على يمينه بعين ما على يساره أم بغيره؟ فإن قلتم: بعينه فهو سفسطة، فإن قلتم: بغيره فقد ثبت التجزي في الجوهر الفرد، وكذلك لاقى أحدهما بكله أو ببعضه، فإن لاقاه بكله وأسره لم يبق للثاني ملاقاة، فإن المشغول بكل وأسر كيف يصير مشغولا بغيره، وإن لاقاه ببعضه فقد تجزى، وكذلك صوروا جوهرًا على متصل جوهرين، فإنه لاقي الجوهرين السفلاوين، وإنما يلاقي كل واحد منهما بطرف غير الآخر، وإن هذا التصوير فصورا خطا مركبا من ستة أجزاء، وخطا يحاذيه من ستة أجزاء وعلى رأس أحد الخطين جوهر، وعلى ذنب الخط الآخر جوهر، وقدرنا تحرك الجوهرين في حالة واحدة على تساوي الحركتين فلا شك أنه يمتاز أحدهما على
As for their objections, they said: If we suppose a substance between two substances, does it contact what is on its right with exactly what is on its left or with something else? If you say: with exactly it, that is sophistry. If you say: with something else, then division in the indivisible atom has been proven. Similarly, it contacts one of them with all of it or with a part of it. If it contacts it with all and entirety, there remains no contact for the second, for how can that which is occupied with all and entirety become occupied with something else? And if it contacts it with a part of it, then it has divided. And similarly they pictured a substance upon a conjoined two substances, for it contacts the two lower substances, and it only contacts each of them with a different side from the other. And this picturing: they pictured a line composed of six parts, and a line parallel to it of six parts, and at the head of one of the lines a substance, and at the tail of the other line a substance, and we supposed that the two substances move in a single state with equal motion, then there is no doubt that one of them surpasses the other
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the indivisible atom based on the scenario of a substance located between two other substances. The objector argues that if a substance is between two neighbors, it must contact each neighbor. The question is whether it contacts the right neighbor with exactly the same part as it contacts the left neighbor, or with a different part. If with exactly the same part, that is absurd (sophistry) because a single point cannot simultaneously contact two distinct things. If with a different part, then the substance has two distinct parts, proving it is divisible. Similarly, if it contacts one neighbor with all of it, there is no remaining part to contact the other neighbor; if it contacts with a part, then it is divisible. The objectors then set up a more detailed scenario: a line of six parts, a parallel line of six parts, with substances at ends, and imagine them moving with equal speed — one will surpass the other, implying contact and division. The passage ends mid-argument; the full response would likely be in the following pages.

Key Terms
خدم (substance)
تماس (contact)
تجزئة (division)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this objection is unanswerable and definitively disproves the atom.
Correction: Mutakallimun have responses, e.g., that contact does not require physical partitive contact but rather immediate adjacency, and that the notion of 'part' here is equivocal.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical dilemma raised in the objection about a substance between two substances?
A) The substance must be divisible to contact both neighbors.
B) The substance cannot exist because it has no location.
C) The substance must be composed of multiple atoms.
D) The substance must be spherical.

Key Takeaway
The objection against the indivisible atom uses the scenario of a substance between two substances to argue that indivisibility leads to contradiction, but mutakallimun have responses to such dilemmas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection forces a choice between two seemingly unacceptable options: sophistry or division. Think of a time when you felt trapped between two equally difficult paths. How did you find a third way, or did you accept one despite its cost? How does this relate to the atomist's position of rejecting both horns of the dilemma?
?
The mutakallimun were willing to defend a view that many considered absurd (indivisible atom in contact with multiple neighbors). What belief do you hold that others might consider irrational or outdated? Are you willing to face such objections without abandoning your conviction? Write about a specific case and how you would respond.
?
The argument uses a very concrete spatial picture to challenge an abstract metaphysical doctrine. Have you ever been convinced by a vivid example or analogy to change your mind about a deep belief? Describe that experience and whether the analogy truly settled the issue or just seemed compelling.
